href="http://www.civicspacelabs.org">Civicspace</a>, a derivative of <a

the internals of a large program work. 

	Does anyone here know of any good references (books, websites, etc) I can 
look at that guide people in studying the source code of large programs? I'm 
not sure this is the right place to be asking for this kind of help, but I've 
tried google and have found nothing too useful.

	Any help would be appreciated. Thanks in advance.


From daniel at digitree.org  Tue Aug  1 02:51:24 2006
From: daniel at digitree.org (Daniel Hedlund)
Date: Tue, 01 Aug 2006 02:51:24 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Does anyone know of references that detail how to study
	Source Code?
In-Reply-To: <200608010112.08674.bobby_miesen@yahoo.com>
References: <200608010112.08674.bobby_miesen@yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <44CF241C.6080903@digitree.org>

Robert Jeffrey Miesen wrote:
> Hi.
>
> 	I'm currently tasked with the job of learning how the internals (source code) 
> of two large programs work---namely <a 
> href="http://www.moodle.org">Moodle</a> and <a 
> href="http://www.civicspacelabs.org">Civicspace</a>, a derivative of <a 
> href="http://www.drupal.com">Drupal</a>. Although I know how to program at 
> least semi-competently, I have never really taken on the task of learning how 
> the internals of a large program work. 
>   
I'm assuming that you're planning on modifying the mentioned products in 
some way or adding additional features, not merely using their public 
APIs.  With most source code, I think one of the only ways is simply to 
dive in.  You may want to learn the basics of the programming languages 
that the source code is written in first, but it's not necessary because 
you'll probably learn the language as you read and try to understand the 
source anyway.  Many projects come with at least some development 
documentation such as their public API, class structures, etc.  It is 
worth familiarizing yourself with this information, and you should also 
join any related [development] mailing lists if you have not yet done 
so.  I doubt there will be that many books on the internal workings of 
any particular product; if so, they will usually be out of date.

Some tips based on how I personally tackle new source code:
1.  try to read as much information about the product as you can and try 
to understand the bigger picture.  Maybe try to use the application from 
a user's point of view.
2.  look for any API or internal-related documentation on their official 
website(s); try to determine how pieces fit together as much as possible.
3.  download and install the source code, play around with the 
management interfaces (command-line options, configuration settings, 
etc) and get a feel for how they affect the product.
4.  start to slowly examine the source code looking for features that 
are familiar to you (either from public APIs or configuration settings) 
and track these changes through the source code.  Some useful places to 
start is a main() type function if they have one, or from the outermost 
code that interacts with the user in some form; these are the best 
places to start because you are already somewhat familiar with what they 
are supposed to do.
5.  document anything that appears interesting to you or things you do 
not immediately understand.  You can do this in the source code using 
comments, possibly with special markers to make them easier to 
find/search ("// !!! I don't know what this does, might be related to 
... !!! //").  It's also worth writing down on paper a little bit bigger 
piece of the picture such as how some functions and/or classes fit 
together if there is not already good documentation on this.  I find 
documenting things (especially in source) very important for me to learn 
things, even if there is some documentation already.  The actually 
process of documenting helps you learn; sometimes I will comment every 
single line of source code in a function with a comment that is a 
English sentence equivalent of what the code should do.  In theory, I 
could strip all the code, leaving only comments and be able to read 
and/or rewrite the code from scratch if I wanted to.
6.  start to make minor modifications to the source code and see if the 
changes you make produce the results you expect.  Some examples of this 
could be resizing a window or changing a color.  You're basically 
starting to test yourself to see if you can predict the behavior of the 
program.  If you can, you're well on your way.

Cheers,

Daniel Hedlund
daniel at digitree.org

Daniel,

   I was thinking of this when I responded. In 1991 I bought my first laptop,
from Gateway (but it was made for them by TI). In the 13th month the power
supply -- internally -- failed. The warranty was 12 months. So, I paid for a
repair. Thirteen months later the same thing happened. Gateway admitted it
was a design or manufacturing flaw and there were many of us with the same
problem. That's when I gave it away. And wouldn't consider another Gateway
product.

   So, you're correct that it may be an internal problem. That's why I
suggested NCS. They'll run the tests to determine the source of the problem
first.

what functions call other functions. Source navigator (I don't recall the

completely.

Your laptop is a different make but I've seen this sort of behavior with Dell 
laptops at one company I worked at. In one case the battery wasn't being kept 
charged but in other cases with the same laptop model the battery was OK. We 
had a Dell tech on site quite a bit replacing mobos in a number of laptops 
which were all the same model. 

The laptops were P4 based and ran extremely hot. Doing anything that required 
any sort of computing power had the fan running full speed making a great 
deal of noise. Other problems with the laptops was after turning it off and 
placing it into a case some of them would mysteriously turn themselves back 
on and cook itself in its own juices inside the case.

With all of the issues though I actually bought my own Dell laptop but it's 
Pentium M-based and runs much cooler than the old P4-based one I had at work. 
I also got a 3 year warranty included as part of the package with the laptop 
when I ordered it from Costco. I don't think I'd buy any brand of laptop 
without an extended warranty though but that's just me.

One thing I would recommend would be to involve yourself in any
listserves or other devices that may get you in contact with the
specific development community. 
Just recently I got a bur in my saddle and wanted to change a piece of
software. First I went to their site and gathered as much info as
possible and then contacted the mailing list. To my surprise someone had
already proposed the changes I was thinking of and they were available
in the next release.
So get yourself as close to the central nerve as possible. This will
save you time in duplicated efforts and you will probably be able to
collaborate and help others. 
Hope this helps.

On Tue, Aug 01, 2006 at 08:53:44AM -0700, alan wrote:
> 
> When I bought my HP laptop two years ago I made sure that I got the 3 year 
> uber warranty.  I am very glad I did.  I have had to use it twice.  Once 
> to fix a damaged cardbus slot and once for a dead power brick.  (Both in 
> the space of a month. In fact, I need to get it in the mail for the second 
> repair.)  One of the main factors in getting the HP zv5000z was because of 
> the warranty. (Besides being the fastest laptop I could find.)
> 
> It is worth the money to get the extra warranty as long as they will 
> replace or fix it with no questions asked.

You do not buy laptops, you rent them from the repair shop.  My Thinkpad
has been back 4 times, and when I bought it (with the max warranty, and
later an extension) that was reputedly the best warranty available.
If you buy a third-tier brand such as an Acer, you may end up treating
it as a disposable.  Designers must cut a lot of reliability corners to
pack the performance into a small case, and laptops get a lot more
pounding than a desktop, so it makes sense that they will fail.

There are many things that can go wrong with laptop power.  If the
laptop goes on for 5 seconds and quits, it is likely because the AC
adapter or the internal charger is dead.  It is always good to have
more than one AC adapter just because it is a pain in the rear to plug
and unplug them;  if they have good switcher circuits inside they do
not waste much power.  So, an adapter at home, an adapter at work,
and an adapter for travel.  Free Geek may have spares, or check Ebay.

You can test an AC adapter with a /volt/meter.  

If the AC adapter is good, then the problem could be the charging
circuit inside the laptop.  This is responsible for both charging
the battery, and for sending power to the laptop bypassing the 
battery.  These charger circuits go out a lot - I've lost two so far.   
If your laptop is out of warranty, there are shops that can fix it.
If the charger went out because something else is shorted inside
the laptop, the repair may be too expensive.  On the other hand,
it might just be a loose connector or a killer dust bunny.

This is why I am skeptical of the One Laptop Per Child initiative.
Very high stress environment (kids, bad power, dirty, hot, humid),
with repair facilities a long way away.  I hope the massive failures
do not get blamed on Linux.

Keith

Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993

My HP has been pretty good. One failure was damage that I did to it and 
one was the external power brick dying.  I have not dropped it yet.  (Eric 
dropped his and it survived pretty well. He has almost the same model as I 
do.)

My last couple of laptops had no real problems until the end of life.

The Toshiba died from being knocked off a shelf by a stack of PGP 
software boxes.

The Vaio died after one or more of the soldered on the motherboard 
batteries died.  (Planned obsolecense.)  I have vet to fix them.

This is my third laptop and it has worked very well.  It is a heavy 
monster, but sturdy.  I guess when it has that big of a screen, you have 
more room to place components and less need for shortcuts.

On Tue, 1 Aug 2006, Keith Lofstrom wrote:

> You do not buy laptops, you rent them from the repair shop.

rotfl!

I think I'd be a bit more qualified. The majority (I'm guessing 60 to 
65%, but it's just a guess) of laptops I've administered have needed 
one or zero repairs during the three years an extended warranty would 
cover. That is to say that, most of the time, the $300 warranty cost 
is wasted or, at best, a wash.

However ... a solid minority of laptops have required multiple 
repairs. Some are lemons. Some just have bad luck. Some have, er, 
demanding users. Whatever the reason, the extended warranty has been 
essential to keeping the machine running.

Like any insurance, an extended warranty is a bet. For me, I'll take 
the insurance on laptops every time. I've dealt with too many 
situations where I'd have essentially thrown away the purchase price 
of the laptop had it not been for the warranty.

Ken Benderly wrote:
> I'm testing out Qwest DSL w/MSN Premium and I can't seem to find any 
> mail server or SMTP server information.  It seems as though there is 
> some proprietary MSN/micro$oft mail http connection.  Is this correct, 
> or is there a regular mail setup somewhere?

The following blog seems to have some answers to your question:
http://www.alexking.org/blog/2003/12/30/msn-qwest-dsl-does-not-support-smtp/

Essentially, some people suggest that you can sign up with FastMail 
(http://www.fastmail.fm/).  Some people later comment that there is a 
pop and smtp server at:
"pop3.email.msn.com
smtp.email.msn.com

youruserid at msn.com
your passwd"

Someone else suggests that it's possible to use QWest's SMTP server 
(smtp.qwest.com).  I didn't read much further than that.

Cheers,

Daniel Hedlund
daniel at digitree.org

From renegadepenguin at comcast.net  Wed Aug  2 07:19:16 2006
From: renegadepenguin at comcast.net (Renegade Penguin)
Date: Wed, 02 Aug 2006 07:19:16 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Laptops and warranties
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0608011250390.31036@blackbox.fnordora.org>
References: <200607311631.34278.dalefv@k-com.net>	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0607311944500.11858@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>	<44CEC924.9040702@digitree.org>	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0608010610560.11570@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0608010849330.28859@blackbox.fnordora.org>	<20060801123037.GB21705@gate.kl-ic.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0608011250390.31036@blackbox.fnordora.org>
Message-ID: <44D0B464.9080002@comcast.net>

I have an HP 17" widescreen, that I bought the 5 year warranty for.  The 
only thing that the warranty doesn't cover is a battery after 1 year.

EVEN if I drop it, run over it, spill coke, etc. - they send a new one.  
So far I've killed 2 DVD drives, one hard drive, a set of hinges for the 
screen, and one motherboard/processor combo (the integrated video 
died).  Total cost of the repairs would have been about what I paid for 
the entire setup, so it was majorly worth it.

I purchased the business warranty (5 years) vs. the 4 years for home 
use.  Worth every penny.  I also obtained the additional hard drive bays 
so I can swap in & out drives from the 3 media bays.  You can put a HDD 
into the DVD and/or floppy bays - for a total of 3.  Backups are in full 
real-time rather than going through a USB drive, very slowly.

It DOES get hot - so I have a hole cut in my desk beneath the fans for 
forced air, using 2 case fans.  It's AMAZINGLY cool.  I basically have a 
desktop replacement.

It's a 3 gigahertz computer so it will stand up to YEARS of use and be 
totally usable.  64-bit - well, they still compile for i386 on many 
Linux platforms so I think I'm safe with the P4 rather than the 64-bit.

Plus the processor is a desktop processor - fully socketed so I can 
replace or upgrade as I wish.

4 USB ports and 1 mini firewire so that is pretty well taken care of 
too.  The screen was the brightest available at the time, and has 
1600x1050 resolution.

If you get one, GET the warranty - next business day on-site.  Have some 
backup items like spare hard drives and a spare power brick, spare 
battery if you can afford it.  The power bricks are the cheap part.  And 
get the additional hard drive trays & you'll be all set!

I couldn't be happier with mine.  Just make SURE you back up your data 
regularly - notebook drive platters are made of glass and shatter like a 
windshield if dropped too hard, COMPLETELY wiping out all hope of 
recovery if there is no backup.

RP

Don't forget to check the fine print on your credit card benefits
statement.  You may be able to get coverage beyond the manufacturer's
warranty.  For example:

  "Extended Warranty Protection: When you purchase your item entirely on
  your REI Visa Gold card, Visa will automatically double the
  manufacturer's written U.S. repair warranty up to one year on
  warranties of three years or less."

It should be noted that I've never actually made a claim under this.

On Wed, 2006-08-02 at 08:03 -0700, Russ Gilman-Hunt wrote:

> I was just using one of "your" nodes last night, considering this.  (At
> Anna Bananas in st johns, tyvm).   I'm guessing that to do this I would
> follow these steps:
> 
> 1) set up squid/privoxy (privoxy? really?)  on my local (home) linux
> box. 
> 
> 2) open up the firewall for a specific port to match the
> squid/privoxy. 

No, as long as you're allowing SSH in, you don't need this.

> 3) when I was out in the wild, using my (gag) windows
> based laptop, use putty and forward a local port on the laptop to the
> port at my home firewall.
> 
> 4) configure firefox on the laptop to use the laptop as a proxy.
> 
> When the packets get to the linux box, how does the linux box know to
> decrypt them?

Uh, it's just part of the SSH connection?

> Am I missing any steps? That's pretty much the extent of
> my knowledge- so if you know of an already written how-to, I could use
> it :) But don't write one, I can google with the rest of the herd.

You should just read up on SSH port-forwarding.  You're just going to
forward a port on your laptop (localhost) over an SSH tunnel to the
proxy port on your firewall or server (localhost, again).  From the
outside, it's all just SSH port 22 traffic.


Wil

From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Tue Aug  1 12:37:03 2006
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (Unknown)
Date: Tue, 01 Aug 2006 12:37:03 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Backuppc...
Message-ID: <1154461024.8464.62.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>

Anyone here use this?

http://backuppc.sourceforge.net/

How do people side step the, "no bare metal recovery," issue?

I don't want to fish for say a working Windows XP install CD
so I can install fresh far enough to overwrite with a backed
up system.  I want to be able to network boot the machine
with the necessary replacement hardware installed, push a 
button, and leave.

I'm hoping I can use something other than dd.  If I
dd image say Windows XP/2000 so I can restore it back exactly 
the same, compression isn't possible.  If I know exactly
where Windows XP bootloader is...  I can probably use dd
to only image that piece.  It would make sense to figure out 
which files on an XP/2000 partition have to be put back in 
the exact same place and image just those files recording 
an offset to put them back.  The trouble if you target image, 
how do you back up only what remains afterwards?
You don't want to restore a file in the place it has to
be written to and somewhere else as this will probably
corrupt the filesystem.  If you image everything, there is
no compression and the added trouble of writing multiple 
images back in the right order.  Smaller images might be
more manageable, but organization is tougher.

Is bare metal recovery possible without dd or is dd the only
way, especially if you don't have say NTFS filesystem support
and you need it?



Begin Digression...
So far, I'm assuming it's okay to say no to NTFS when 
installing 2000/XP to sidestep the lack of open source 
support.  A filesystem that you can't back up isn't a 
good deal in my opinion.  I bet Reiserfs would give 
Windows a better boost than NTFS does.

I suppose if all your Windows systems run on top of Linux,
backup gets enormously easier.  Thing is, vmware costs a
lot of money where I am concerned that not everything
works under emulation.  You don't keep Windows around to
browse the web unless you have to have Weatherbug or a 
similar web app that isn't available for Linux.  It bothers
me that there aren't better sites to address migration
problems if you're trying to get people out of Windows.
I checked out the Linspire web site and wasn't impressed, 
people need to support some XP/2000 programs to get out of 
Windows completely.  A friend needs to support Mapinfo for 
example.  He is into Family Tree also.  TurboTax is popular, 
but will Intuit or whoever puts it out ever release a Linux 
version?  I don't know what went wrong when Corel attempted 
to move their major software to Linux.  $50+ for Linspire.
Geez, I can almost buy XP Home for that and not worry about
compatibility.  Do Linspire users get free upgrades???

Are there any success stories of companies leaving MS Windows
and moving their major software to Linux?

What is stopping talented programmers from making it in 
commercial softwares ventures that target Linux?
...end Digression.



Regards,

Michael C. Robinson

From wcooley at nakedape.cc  Wed Aug  2 12:16:31 2006
From: wcooley at nakedape.cc (Wil Cooley)
Date: Wed, 02 Aug 2006 12:16:31 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Data exchange/transfer application?
Message-ID: <1154546191.1983.69.camel@willow.odshp.com>


I've looked on Freshmeat and maybe I'm not using the right keywords.
Does anyone know of an server or application for automating moving
around files?  This is for EDI and similar files; basically, we have
dozens of jobs that pull files from one server and push them to another,
in some cases making transformations like encrypting them with PGP.
These transfers happen mainly over FTP and SFTP, depending on the other
party's capabilities; sometimes they happen on a schedule, sometimes
they happen on some external trigger like an e-mail with a particular
subject (I don't like that, but it's what I've got at the moment), with
notification and error reporting via log, e-mail, etc.  I don't need to
read or write or transform EDI or HIPAA file formats, I just need to
move them around, basically.

I can imagine describing these processes in a higher-level language than
what I have now, but I haven't actually found anything that does this.

Wil
-- 
Wil Cooley <wcooley at nakedape.cc>
Naked Ape Consulting, Ltd

From wcooley at nakedape.cc  Wed Aug  2 14:17:44 2006
From: wcooley at nakedape.cc (Wil Cooley)
Date: Wed, 02 Aug 2006 14:17:44 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Data exchange/transfer application?
In-Reply-To: <ff01ea180608021325y7dfc054brb41b967c44b7bf15@mail.gmail.com>
References: <1154546191.1983.69.camel@willow.odshp.com>
	<ff01ea180608021325y7dfc054brb41b967c44b7bf15@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <1154553464.1983.97.camel@willow.odshp.com>

On Wed, 2006-08-02 at 13:25 -0700, Steve D... wrote:

>   It sounds like a task for a standard batch control system.  Batch
> systems commonly use the absence or presence of a file to trigger a
> action.

Triggering the action isn't the problem; the action is the problem.
What I want to do is to model a transfer "recipe" like this (using a
vaguely fetchmail-ish syntax):

id FOOBASH834:
  get via ftp
    username userx
    password passw0rd
    server datamunger.mycompany.com
    path /munged_stuff/${JOB_ID}.txt

  encrypt with pgp key id 0xffffff
  sign with pgp key id ${MY_PGP_KEY}
  compress with gzip

  send via sftp
    username mycompanyuser
    password p4ssword
    server sftpserver.othercompany.com
    path /whatever/mycompany/

  notify
    mail info to notify-users at mycompany.com
    mail errors to data-transfer-admins at mycompany.com
    syslog info "data transfer ${JOB_ID} ${JOB_STATUS}"
    cmd info send_nsca -H nagioshost ... # Some Nagios passive info her

  keep local file 60 days
  delete source file

  # Some other sort of stuff here        


The salient points here are:
  o I don't care about the contents of the files, because those are 
    generated or processed by other systems, except for transformations 
    like encrypting, signing, gzipping.
  o I don't care about triggering the jobs; that's also external.
  o Notifications, transfers, and standard transformations are critical.
  o All the tools necessary to do this are available--sundry scripting 
    languages, ftp/sftp/pgp/gpg/gzip clients, etc., but the tools alone 
    are not the whole task.

Wil
-- 
Wil Cooley <wcooley at nakedape.cc>
Naked Ape Consulting, Ltd

On Wed, 2006-08-02 at 15:25 -0700, Aaron Baer wrote:

> In the Commercial Enterprise non-opensource sort of world you're talking
> about something like Tidal Enterprise Scheduler right?
> 
> http://www.tidalsoftware.com/products/products_TES_Overview.asp
> 
> Although the site sucks and it's all marketing speak and there are no
> screen shots of the actual product. You're looking for something similar
> in the Open Source world?

I don't think so.  We've actually got Tidal, although I haven't looked
carefully at it, it's just a batch scheduler, AFAIK.  There are several
open source batch schedulers.

What I'm looking for is something more like this, I think:

http://www.proginet.com/products/cyberfusion_integration_suite/cfi_overview.cfm
http://www.proginet.com/solutions/filetransfer/overview.cfm

I'm suspecting that this is a pretty expensive package, given prices are
discreetly not mentioned on their site.  It also doesn't seem to
directly support PGP encryption, although it seems to support a myriad
of other means of securing transfer.

So far, this is actually about the only thing I've found, proprietary,
open source or otherwise.  I'm rather surprised, really--this was a
recurring problem with my last job too.  I wonder if it's a problem
that's too easily solved in the simple cases for anyone to have bothered
solving it in the bigger?  When I say bigger, I'm talking about 200+
configured transfers, which is why a pile of shell scripts around wget,
scp, etc are no longer adequate.

Wil
-- 
Wil Cooley <wcooley at nakedape.cc>
Naked Ape Consulting, Ltd

Kids, please don't try this at home!  I assumed that suggestion would be
interpreted as a joke, but apparently not.  While touching a standard 9V
battery to your tongue doesn't present much danger, an AC adapter is a
different story.  A poorly designed or failing adapter could present a
serious risk.  Please use a voltmeter to check something like this.

From bobby_miesen at yahoo.com  Wed Aug  2 17:11:46 2006
From: bobby_miesen at yahoo.com (Robert Jeffrey Miesen)
Date: Wed, 2 Aug 2006 17:11:46 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Backuppc...
In-Reply-To: <1154461024.8464.62.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
References: <1154461024.8464.62.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <200608021711.47018.bobby_miesen@yahoo.com>

Hi.

	I don't know anything about backuppc. However, to answer your question about 
software existing to perform bare metal restores (if I understand you 
correctly, a bare metal restore is a level 0 restore), mondo does the job 
beautifully. It can even backup NTFS partitions. There are some limitations 
to this, though, and it would be good to read mondo's documentation at 
http://www.mondorescue.org/docs/mondorescue-howto/index.html



On Tuesday 01 August 2006 12:37, Unknown wrote:
> Anyone here use this?
>
> http://backuppc.sourceforge.net/
>
> How do people side step the, "no bare metal recovery," issue?
>
> I don't want to fish for say a working Windows XP install CD
> so I can install fresh far enough to overwrite with a backed
> up system.  I want to be able to network boot the machine
> with the necessary replacement hardware installed, push a
> button, and leave.
>
> I'm hoping I can use something other than dd.  If I
> dd image say Windows XP/2000 so I can restore it back exactly
> the same, compression isn't possible.  If I know exactly
> where Windows XP bootloader is...  I can probably use dd
> to only image that piece.  It would make sense to figure out
> which files on an XP/2000 partition have to be put back in
> the exact same place and image just those files recording
> an offset to put them back.  The trouble if you target image,
> how do you back up only what remains afterwards?
> You don't want to restore a file in the place it has to
> be written to and somewhere else as this will probably
> corrupt the filesystem.  If you image everything, there is
> no compression and the added trouble of writing multiple
> images back in the right order.  Smaller images might be
> more manageable, but organization is tougher.
>
> Is bare metal recovery possible without dd or is dd the only
> way, especially if you don't have say NTFS filesystem support
> and you need it?
>
>
>
> Begin Digression...

From daniel at digitree.org  Wed Aug  2 19:42:03 2006
From: daniel at digitree.org (Daniel Hedlund)
Date: Wed, 02 Aug 2006 19:42:03 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Thanks for the help Acer Laptop
In-Reply-To: <20060802231604.D0F778F0D@tinman.seitzassoc.com>

Galen Seitz wrote:
>> I did stick my tongue onto the end of the power supply and I can say
>> it works.
>>
> Kids, please don't try this at home!  I assumed that suggestion would be
> interpreted as a joke, but apparently not.  While touching a standard 9V
> battery to your tongue doesn't present much danger, an AC adapter is a
> different story.  A poorly designed or failing adapter could present a
> serious risk.  Please use a voltmeter to check something like this.

Absolutely!  I had mentioned for the user to use a ammeter, but was 
probably foolish enough to mention the alternative which they could try 
if they were "stupid enough".  I probably should have added an 
additional warning explaining "why" I said "stupid enough ..." though. 
It's really about weighing risks.  The voltage that comes out of the 
power brick is DC 12-17v and thus doesn't oscillate near your heart 
rate, but the amperage (1-2.5amp) is sufficiently high enough that it 
definitely is no 9v battery either!  A wet tongue and the proximity 
between positive and negative make it less likely that it could kill 
you, but with an undiagnosed heart problem it is still possible.  I 
never thought to take in consideration that the power brick might 
exhibit excessive current such as if a resistor failed or a capacitor 
was overcharging itself; you have brought this to my attention as a 
valid possibility.  In my case, I've been stupid enough far too many 
times when an ammeter isn't handy, but I was willing to take that risk 
at the time.  For reference, what I originally recommended is below:

"You can test this by using an ammeter on the end of the cable...or for 
those people who are poor or stupid enough like myself, you could always 
try sticking your tongue on the end of the cord"

Cheers,

Daniel Hedlund
daniel at digitree.org

> attention as a valid possibility.  In my case, I've been stupid enough
> far too many times when an ammeter isn't handy, but I was willing to
> take that risk at the time.  For reference, what I originally
> recommended is below:

The real danger is from the possibility of the higher voltage from the
wall outlet leaking through the adapter.  This could happen due to poor
design or failing components.  Here's a good link:

http://www.allaboutcircuits.com/vol_1/chpt_3/4.html

and a quote from the link:

"In industry, 30 volts is generally considered to be a conservative
threshold value for dangerous voltage. The cautious person should regard
any voltage above 30 volts as threatening, not relying on normal body
resistance for protection against shock. That being said, it is still an
excellent idea to keep one's hands clean and dry, and remove all metal
jewelry when working around electricity. Even around lower voltages,
metal jewelry can present a hazard by conducting enough current to burn
the skin if brought into contact between two points in a circuit. Metal
rings, especially, have been the cause of more than a few burnt fingers
by bridging between points in a low-voltage, high-current circuit."

galen

Starting to sound like a new open source project to me.
A few primary pieces.
1) something to parse configuration files for transfers
2) Simple linkage mechanism for starting transfers (triggers,cron,
directory watch)
3) Generalized code for running/monitoring the transfer utilities with
    a method to re-schedule when the transfer fails.
4) Possibly a database to allow this to all be tied together (sqlite)
5) A GUI interface to allow non-technical people to easily add and
remove transfers

	<1154621877.4662.25.camel@willow.odshp.com>
	<20060803170025.GA21044@syrinxpc.com>
Message-ID: <1154629496.4662.98.camel@willow.odshp.com>

On Thu, 2006-08-03 at 10:00 -0700, Aaron Burt wrote:
> On Thu, Aug 03, 2006 at 09:17:57AM -0700, Wil Cooley wrote:
> > On Wed, 2006-08-02 at 17:08 -0700, Aaron Burt wrote:
> > > Exactly.  I still don't understand what about your problem wouldn't be
> > > trivial to handle with common shell tools, and a simple tabular file of
> > > file-transfer specs.
> > 
> > It would be like implementing an MTA with netcat and shell.
> 
> Yes, you've given that impression.  File transfers are easy.  Scheduling
> is easy.  What is it about your problem that makes it *not* easy?
> 
> I'm totally missing something here, and I'd rather find out here than by
> making a fool of myself at work.

Individual file transfers are easy.  Gluing it all together into a
cohesive system handling the myriad combinations of transfer protocols,
login information, encryption applications, notifications, auditing,
gracefully handling the myriad of ways each component can fail -- and
doing it well -- is hard.  But it's not hard in any interesting
sense--not like, say, the Red Army / Blue Army problem or solving a
Rubik's cube is hard.  It's hard in the sense that doing it right would
require 20-80 man-hours that I necessarily have to spare.

What I've got now is several dozen scripts that do all of this stuff,
each of which is "easy" on its own, but all piled on top of one another,
is a huge fucking mess.  Almost all of these are descended from three or
four proto-scripts and copied and slightly tweaked to do something
different.  I *can* try to refactor them, eliminate the duplication,
etc., but these scripts are in production and tinkering around with them
is not a good idea.  Many of them are ksh, which is harder to unit-test
than Perl/Python/Ruby.  Network transactions are also a lot harder to
design unit-tests for.

Let's consider the implementation details for a single FTP PUT.  You've
got to:

  o Check that the local file exists and produce an appropriate message 
    if it doesn't.
  o Check if the file is zero-length.  For legacy reasons, some files 
    will always exist, but not really be the desired file until it 
    actually has data.  In other cases, you have to upload an empty 
    file even if there is no file, or copy a CSV template with just 
    headers (a CSV file with no records, in other words).
  o Figure out the name the resulting file should have.
  o Figure out the mode, binary or text?
  o Connect and login to the FTP server, which can result in:
    o Success
    o Login failed
    o Too many users
      o Sleep, retry until ...
    o Timeout
      o Sleep, retry until ...
  o Actually upload the file, which can result in:
    o Success
    o Permission denied error
    o Out-of-space error
      o Sleep, retry until ... ?
    o Server too busy 
      o Sleep, retry until ...
    o Timeout
      o Sleep, retry until ...
    o Wrong transfer mode (active/passive)
      o Reconfigure and try again
  o Configured to verify upload?
    o No
    o Yes
      o Check file existence and size (that's about all you get w/FTP)
        o File is there and correct size
        o File isn't there
        o File size is wrong
          o How do you handle file size changes in text mode?
        o Listing is denied
        o Timeout
  o Archive local file afterwards?
    o No
    o Yes
    o ... (All the stuff that can go wrong with just mv'ing a file)
  o Delete local file afterwards?
    o No
    o Yes
    o ... (All the stuff that can go wrong with rm'ing a file)
  o Produce an execution report
  o Produce an error report, if any
  o Produce a trace report, if enabled
  o Route reports to correct people

It took me twenty minutes or so just to write this summary; it'd take me
about two to four hours to code it, more to test, then integrate into
larger framework.  And then there's designing the framework,
implementing FTP GET, SFTP GET, SFTP PUT, ...

And why do you have to be anal about handling every error as much as
possible, by retrying, etc., instead of just aborting and throwing up a
message?  Because you have hundreds of such jobs and putting out that
many fires gets tiresome really fast.

And then there are other things that are maybe outside the core module,
like looking at audit logs and producing reports, or verifying that a
particular job ran within the allowed time periods (it's not unusual
that there will be files that come or are sent certain times of the
month or quarter or year, that could arrive within some particular delta
of time, but need to have arrived within it).

You see now why _managing_ lots of transfers for _unattended_ operation
is non-trivial?

I don't know how many systems you've managed at one time, but you can
start out crafting each installation for the particular need, being
absolutely parsimonious with disk space used, using scp in "for" loops
in the shell to keep critical files in sync (maybe you even write a
wrapper script or shell function), but at some point you realize that
you could spend more time reading Slashdot and ranting on the PLUG list
if you invested some time up front in automating the OS installation and
learning and using a tool like cfengine to everything in sync.  This is
analogous to that.

Wil
-- 
Wil Cooley <wcooley at nakedape.cc>
Naked Ape Consulting, Ltd

Subject: [PLUG] Thanks for the help Acer Laptop
In-Reply-To: <44D1627B.9080600@digitree.org>

>I did stick my tongue onto the end of the power supply and I can say
>it works.

> Galen Seitz wrote:
>Kids, please don't try this at home!  I assumed that suggestion would be
>interpreted as a joke, but apparently not.  While touching a standard 9V
>battery to your tongue doesn't present much danger, an AC adapter is a
>different story.  A poorly designed or failing adapter could present a
>serious risk.  Please use a voltmeter to check something like this.

On Wed, Aug 02, 2006 at 07:42:03PM -0700, Daniel Hedlund wrote:
> Absolutely!  I had mentioned for the user to use a ammeter, but was 
> probably foolish enough to mention the alternative which they could try 
...
> 
> "You can test this by using an ammeter on the end of the cable...or for 
> those people who are poor or stupid enough like myself, you could always 
> try sticking your tongue on the end of the cord"

Keith weighs in, again:

VOLTMETER, not ammeter.  Voltmeters measures voltage in volts, ammeters
measure current in amps.  There are ways you could use an ammeter, but
they are too complicated to get into here.  Ammeters are designed to
pass current with very low voltage drop, and will conduct a hell of a
lot of current when subjected to many volts.  AC adapters are supposed to
limit the current, but the current limit circuit can fail.  An ammeter
shorted across an AC adapter could blow up the adapter, the ammeter,
or both.   You might as well test the adapter with a hammer: "it might
have been broken before, but it is definitely broken now!"

The difference between current and voltage is like the difference
between salary and education.   One is a flow, the other a potential.

And I agree with others - testing with a tongue could be lethal, because
there could be leakage in the AC adapter.  There is not supposed to be,
but electrocution is a lethal way to find out.  9V batteries do not have
line cords so they won't kill you through your tongue (though you can
still damage something).

The mouth is not an electrical measurement device, it is a beer 
processing device.  Don't use it to measure voltage (or current),
and your voltmeter won't drink your beer.

Keith

Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

From daniel at digitree.org  Fri Aug  4 10:02:28 2006
From: daniel at digitree.org (Daniel Hedlund)
Date: Fri, 04 Aug 2006 10:02:28 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Any tools for visualizing physical memory layout?
In-Reply-To: <20060803201243.E88168@eehouse.org>
References: <20060803201243.E88168@eehouse.org>
Message-ID: <44D37DA4.2020105@digitree.org>

eehouse wrote:
> I'm trying to educate myself on how the physical memory in my
> (swapless; it's a WRAP box with a CF card for storage) linux router is
> used.  It has 128 meg of RAM and is running a 2.4 kernel.  I'd like to
> know how the 128M is allocated: how many pages by each process, within
> the process how many are used to map libraries into memory, etc.
> 
> I'm happy to hack something into the proc filesystem if I have to but
> it seems somebody must have done this before.  Any ideas or pointers?

Why would you want to know how the memory is physically laid out unless 
you're planning on modifying memory management within the kernel itself; 
different versions of the kernel may handle memory layouts completely 
differently.  If you want to know how much memory is being used for 
shared libraries and how much for each individual application, look into 
the 'top' program as Rich suggested.

You can get a mapping of the memory used by an individual application in 
the /proc/[pid]/maps file.  This includes all the shared libraries used 
by the application, memory allocated for heaps and stacks.  Some 
information about maps file syntax can be found at:
http://www.linuxforums.org/misc/understanding_/proc_3.html

I don't know of any applications that produce a visual (graphical-based) 
description of how memory is allocated.  It doesn't seem like it would 
very difficult to implement based on the maps files, but the image 
manipulation libraries you'd need for common image formats probably 
wouldn't be on your router. You could use the XBM image file format 
(http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/XBM) without any libraries using just 
about any scripting language, even bash, due to its simplicity.

Also note, the maps files would not contain information such as files 
that are cached in memory, buffers, kernel modules or the kernel itself; 
I don't see any easy way of extracting this information without using 
/dev/mem or /proc/kcore and manually parsing the raw memory.

Cheers,

Daniel Hedlund
daniel at digitree.org

> > the process how many are used to map libraries into memory, etc.
> > 
> > I'm happy to hack something into the proc filesystem if I have to but
> > it seems somebody must have done this before.  Any ideas or pointers?
> 
> Why would you want to know how the memory is physically laid out unless 
> you're planning on modifying memory management within the kernel itself; 
> different versions of the kernel may handle memory layouts completely 
> differently.  If you want to know how much memory is being used for 
> shared libraries and how much for each individual application, look into 
> the 'top' program as Rich suggested.

I'm looking for something more accurate.  And as I read more, I'm
finding out that the information may not be there.

For example, multiple threads show up as different processes though
their resident sets are shared and so to the extent they're mapped
into memory it's to the same pages.  So if I use top, or
/proc/<pid>/maps, I need to weed out duplicates (and am not sure how.)

I found a patch producing a proc fs entry called /proc/memmap that
produces output with one bit per page of physical memory, either free
or in use.  And I'm following the data structures for in-use pages
hoping they'll point to the processes that own them.  That's the kind
of information I'm hoping for -- and so far I'm not getting too far,
but also reading that it may just not be there.

I'm just curious, so not knowing won't kill me. :-)

Thanks for the suggestions,

--Eric

> You can get a mapping of the memory used by an individual application in 
> the /proc/[pid]/maps file.  This includes all the shared libraries used 
> by the application, memory allocated for heaps and stacks.  Some 
> information about maps file syntax can be found at:
> http://www.linuxforums.org/misc/understanding_/proc_3.html
> 
> I don't know of any applications that produce a visual (graphical-based) 
> description of how memory is allocated.  It doesn't seem like it would 
> very difficult to implement based on the maps files, but the image 
> manipulation libraries you'd need for common image formats probably 
> wouldn't be on your router. You could use the XBM image file format 
> (http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/XBM) without any libraries using just 
> about any scripting language, even bash, due to its simplicity.
> 
> Also note, the maps files would not contain information such as files 
> that are cached in memory, buffers, kernel modules or the kernel itself; 
> I don't see any easy way of extracting this information without using 
> /dev/mem or /proc/kcore and manually parsing the raw memory.
> 
> Cheers,
> 
> Daniel Hedlund
> daniel at digitree.org

--

From galens at seitzassoc.com  Fri Aug  4 15:37:17 2006
From: galens at seitzassoc.com (Galen Seitz)
Date: Fri, 04 Aug 2006 15:37:17 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] creating a bootable FreeDOS USB flash drive
Message-ID: <20060804223722.DFA838F0D@tinman.seitzassoc.com>


I recently needed to RUN some DOS-only tools(BIOS upgrade, etc.) on a
system without a floppy.  This forced me to figure out how to run
FreeDOS from my flash drive.  There are instructions on the net for
this, but they weren't completely clear from my perspective.  Perhaps
these notes will be useful to others.

galen


Steps for creating a bootable FreeDOS on a USB Flash drive that is
already FAT formatted.  Note that these instructions apply to USB drives
that look like hard disks(ie they contain a partition table), and not to
drives that are set up as super floppies.

NOTE #2  BE VERY CAREFUL IF YOU HAVE SCSI DISKS.  The fact that SCSI and
USB use similar device names could really spoil your day.


1. Make sure USB drive is not mounted.

2. Save the 1000 sectors or so.  This should be large enough to include the
   first sector of the first partition.  Alternatively you could determine
   the first sector of the first partition and save everything up to and
   including that sector.  The LBA address of the first sector of each
   partition is in the partition table.

     dd if=/dev/sdX of=saved_sectors count=1000

3. If the USB drive lacks a valid master boot record(MBR), install one.
   This can be done using the install-mbr command on Debian systems.  My
   Redhat systems don't have this command, and I'm not sure what the
   alternatives are.  My USB drive already had an MBR.

4. Mark the partition as bootable using fdisk or similar.

5. Install syslinux.  This copies ldlinux.sys to the filesystem and writes
   to the partition boot sector.

     syslinux -s /dev/sdX1 

6. Mount the USB drive.

7. Copy memdisk to the USB drive.  This allows syslinux to boot from a
   floppy disk image.

     cp /usr/lib/syslinux/memdisk /USB_drive_mount_point

8. Copy a FreeDOS floppy image to the USB drive.  I found a suitable one 
   here:
     http://www.ibiblio.org/pub/micro/pc-stuff/freedos/files/distributions/old/beta9sr1/disksets/ODIN/fdodin91.img

     cp fdodin91.img /USB_drive_mount_point

9. Create a syslinux.cfg file on the USB drive that contains the following
   lines:

     default freedos
     prompt 1
     label freedos
     kernel memdisk
     append initrd=fdodin91.img


That's it.  After booting from the drive, A: is a ramdisk version of the
freedos image file.  Any writes to A: will be lost on a reboot.  C: is
the filesystem on the USB drive.

> 3. If the USB drive lacks a valid master boot record(MBR), install one.
>    This can be done using the install-mbr command on Debian systems.  My
>    Redhat systems don't have this command, and I'm not sure what the
>    alternatives are.

lilo -M /dev/sdX

- or -

grub-install /dev/sdX --root-directory=/mnt/<mount_name>

But then you have to followup with LILO or Grub.

From russell at personaltelco.net  Fri Aug  4 23:19:40 2006
From: russell at personaltelco.net (Russell Senior)
Date: 04 Aug 2006 23:19:40 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] rebuilding your network after a crash... literally!
Message-ID: <86fygbu2kj.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>


Here is a moderately amusing story that occurred today.  Some of you
may be aware of the neighborhood-wide wireless network that Personal
Telco Project built (and continues to build) on North Mississippi
Avenue.  This morning, I checked the cacti graphs for the network and
noticed downage.  It was strange.  It was strange because different
nodes disappeared at different times.  The "total associations" graph
pretty much lawn-darted at about 3:15 or 3:30 this morning.  It wasn't
like any of the typical outages we've seen, where someone unplugs
something, a flakey cat5 connector gets bumped, a neighborhood-wide
power outage, etc.  

Hmm, I thought.  I'll need to get over there ASAP to figure out what
is going on.  I have the only PTP key with access to the building
where we bring in the DSL.  I stew a little.

A little while later, I check my email and find a message from the
building manager.  He reports in three terse sentences, that: someone
got on the roof last night and *threw* our gear off onto the sidewalk;
the police arrested the guy (who was drunk); and could we please call
to help figure out how to fix it!

Oh.  Shit.

Our gear there consists of non-penetrating "tripods", which are held
down with cinderblocks, with a vertical mast about 4-feet tall, to
which we have attached an additional 6 foot mast (with some overlap),
to which is attached our radio gear and antennas.  Each of the radios
are connected to the building via a length of UV-rated cat5 (power is
supplied over the unused pairs of the cat5 [1]).

I call and get a few more details.  There are three tripods, all three
went over the side.

Building manager tells me two of the masts are damaged.  The other one
got held up by a tree and was dangling from the cat5 until morning
(this explains the non-simultaneous failures).  The perp had
(thankfully) removed the cinderblocks from the tripod before hurling
it over.  Of course, with the cinderblocks it weighed about 400 lbs.
The cinderblocks stayed on the roof.  The building manager (and,
later, Don Park) took some photos of the carnage [2].

So, I rustle up some helpers and head over.  Don and I arrive first.
I find two of the masts sitting inside partially recombobulated.  One
of the radio enclosures has some damage (broke off the polycarbonate
mounting bolts at one end, and a small crack at the top.  Just the
small crack penetrates to the weatherproof section.  Don starts
opening up the boxes with a screwdriver, while I run home to get some
power supplies to fire up the radios to see if they still work, and
some rope with which to haul the gear back up onto the roof.

It's about 1pm when I get back, Don has the boxes open.  In the
cracked-case box, the board is loose.  The four plastic standoffs
that hold the single-board computer inside either broke or pulled
out.  I find one that isn't damaged and hang the board from it.  One
of the mini-PCI radios is slightly ajar in its socket.  We reseat the
radio.  We apply power, to each of these two, and happily they still
function.  We identify which one goes where, and start hauling the
gear outside to where we can rope-lift it up the side of the building.

Meanwhile, Caleb Phillips and Michael Weinberg let me know they are on
their way to help.  Don and I get the masts up on the roof.  When
Caleb arrives, a little after 2pm, he and Don carefully pull up the
tripods. 

In the process we discover one of the sector antennas we are using for
local coverage was also damaged.  A couple-inch long hole was broken
in the polycarbonate radome.  It doesn't rattle, but peaking through
the opening it appears the antenna elements might be damaged.

I run off to get a spare replacement antenna from my house and a
replacement bolt from the hardware store for one that got munged in
the fall.  By the time I get back, they have finished determining that
the cat5 runs are still okay, but they've fried my cat5 tester in the
process.  Well, two leds of my cat5 tester.  The leds didn't like the
48V supplied by the PoE.  Oops.

The only remaining problem is disassembling and reassembling the
Amphenol IP67 weatherproof ethernet plugs [3] on a newly crimped tip.
There is a special tool to do the disassembly, but small screwdrivers
and similar devices work also, with persistance.

That solved, we plug in the devices again, a little electrical tape to
temporarily patch the radio enclosure crack, and bingo, back online.
Total downtime, about 13 hours.

The only remaining questions are:  

  a) Why did the perp do it?  Was it just random drunken exhuberance,
     and a crime of opportunity?  Or did he have something in for PTP?

and

  b) Can we recruit this guy?  He's obviously not scared of climbing
     on roofs.  PTP needs people like that, we just need to curb his
     caber tossing[4] impulses a bit.

oh, and maybe:

  c) is this what I get for asking questions of last night's PLUG
     speaker regarding backhoes (or equivalent) taking out his fiber
     runs?

Notes:

[1] <http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IEEE_802.3af>
[2] <http://www.personaltelco.net/gallery/ballroomvandal>
[3] <http://metrix.net/images/MET-RJ45-M-01.jpg>
[4] <http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caber_toss>

-- 
Russell Senior, Secretary
russell at personaltelco.net

Don't be surprised if the SBC or radio fails at some point in the
future.  After a fall like that, the circuit board traces and
connections could have hairline cracks.  A little expansion and
contraction due to temperature swings may cause a trace to fail.  Of
course you probably knew that.

General News or Inquiries

***O'Reilly Author Gorden Meyer is Available as a Speaker
Gordon Meyer ("Smart Home Hacks") gives lively presentations about 
fun and practical do-it-yourself home automation projects. His 
talkS appeal to those who want to live the 21st century life, 
as well as those who just appreciate clever and interesting ways 
to exploit and combine different technologies. Gordon is a frequent 
speaker at Apple's WWDC, MacWorld, and many other conferences. He 
can tailor his  talk to Mac, Windows, Unix, and other user groups. 
He lives in Chicago, but travels frequently and will consider 
invitations for other locations. For more info, go to:
<http://www.gordonmeyer.com or email bb at g2meyer.com>

----------------------------------------------------------------

or Syngress books you purchase directly from O'Reilly.

Free ground shipping on orders of $29.95 or more.
For more details, go to:
<http://www.oreilly.com/news/freeshipping_0703.html>

Did you know you can request a free book to review for your
group? Ask your group leader for more information.

For book review writing tips and suggestions, go to:
<http://ug.oreilly.com/bookreviews.html>

***Building a VoIP Network with Nortel's Multimedia Communication 
   Server 5100
Publisher: Syngress
ISBN: 1597490784
This is the only book you need if you are tasked with designing,
installing, configuring, and troubleshooting a converged network built
with Nortel's Multimedia Concentration Server 5100, and Multimedia
Communications Portfolio (MCP) products. With this book, you'll be able
to design, build, secure, and maintain a cutting-edge converged
network to satisfy all of your business requirements.
<http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/1597490784/index.html>


***CSS: The Missing Manual
Publisher: O'Reilly
ISBN: 0596526873
Cascading Style Sheets are now a reliable method for handling all kinds
of Web page presentations--from fonts and colors to page layout. But
due to CSS's complexity, most designers treat it as a kind of
window-dressing to spruce up the appearance of their sites without
tapping into the real power of CSS. "CSS: The Missing Manual" explains 
how you can use this powerful to build sparklingly new web sites, or 
refurbish old sites that are ready for an upgrade.
<http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/csstmm>


***Designing and Building Enterprise DMZs
Publisher: Syngress
ISBN: 1597491004
"Building DMZs for Enterprise Networks" covers a sorely needed area in
critical business infrastructure: the Demilitarized Zone. DMZs play a
crucial role in any network consisting of a Hosted Internet Web Server,
internal servers which need to be segregated, External to Internal DNS
Server, and an email SMTP Relay Agent. This book covers what an
administrator needs to plan out and integrate a DMZ into a network for
small, medium, and Enterprise networks.
<http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/1597491004>


***Firewall Policies and VPN Configurations
Publisher: Syngress
ISBN: 1597490881
The security provided by firewalls and VPNs is only as strong as the
protocol rules and policies that you create. This book provides 
fundamental information necessary to configure firewalls and build VPNs 
and DMZs to survive the dangers of today's internet. Products covered 
include Cisco PIX Firewall, ISA Server 2004, Check Point NGX, Juniper's 
NetScreen Firewalls, SonicWall, and Nokia. 
<http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/1597490881>


***Google Hacks, Third Edition
Publisher: O'Reilly
ISBN: 0596527063
Now that new features and services such as Google Maps, Google Talk, and
Google Desktop have been added to the expanding Google universe, we've
made the third edition of this bestseller into an infinitely more useful
book for this powerful search engine. You'll not only find dozens of
hacks for new Google services, but plenty of updated tips, tricks, and
scripts for hacking the old ones. 
<http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/googlehks3>


***JavaScript: The Definitive Guide, Fifth Edition
Publisher: O'Reilly
ISBN: 0596101996
The indispensable reference for JavaScript programmers since 1996,
"JavaScript: The Definitive Guide, Fifth Edition" is completely revised
and expanded to cover JavaScript as it is used in today's Web 2.0
applications.
<http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/jscript5>


***Mastering Regular Expressions, Third Edition
Publisher: O'Reilly
ISBN: 0596528124
Written in the lucid, entertaining tone that makes a complex, dry topic
become crystal-clear to programmers, and sprinkled with solutions to
complex real-world problems, "Mastering Regular Expressions, Third
Edition" offers a wealth information that you can put to immediate use.
<http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/regex3>


***Metasploit Toolkit for Penetration Testing, Exploit Development, and
Vulnerabiity Research
Publisher: Syngress
ISBN: 1597490741
This is the first book available for the Metasploit Framework (MSF),
the attack platform of choice for one of the fastest growing careers 
in IT security: Penetration Testing. The book and companion web site 
will provide professional penetration testers and security researchers 
with a fully integrated suite of tools for discovering, running, and 
testing exploit code.
<http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/1597490741>


***Music Technology Magazine's Ten Minute Masters
Publisher: PC Publishing
ISBN: 187077504X
"Music Tech Magazine's Ten Minute Masters" is a collection of explanations 
of music theories, technologies, protocols, and processes. As useful to the 
newcomer as to the expert, the collection gives everyone sufficient 
information to grasp a concept in the space of 1,500 words--or just ten 
minutes! From analogue recording to Audio CDs, from cabling to copyright, 
and from filters to Firewire, this is an invaluable collection for anyone 
involved in the music technology field.
<http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/187077504X>


***Network Security Assessment
Publisher: Syngress
ISBN: 1597491012
This book will take readers from the discovery of vulnerabilities and
the creation of the corresponding exploits, through a complete security
assessment, all the way through deploying patches against these
vulnerabilities to protect their networks. This book is unique in that
it details both the management and technical skill and tools required to
develop an effective vulnerability management system. 
<http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/1597491012>


***The Photoshop Anthology
Publisher: SitePoint
ISBN: 0975841920
"The Photoshop Anthology" is full-color, 101 question-and-answer book
for web designers who want to use Photoshop more effectively to build 
web sites and create better looking web graphics. The book will show 
you how you can use Photoshop to create buttons, backgrounds, text 
effects, optimized photos, web interfaces, animated GIFs, and more for 
your web site. Additionally, the book will walk you through the entire 
process of designing a Website in Photoshop--from using layers to keep
organized to using the slice tool to create individual web site elements.
<http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/0975841920>


***PHP Cookbook
Publisher: O'Reilly
ISBN: 0596101015
When it comes to creating dynamic web sites, the open source PHP
language is red-hot property: used on more than 20 million web sites
today, PHP is now more popular than Microsoft's ASP.NET technology. With
our Cookbook's unique format, you can learn how to build dynamic web
applications that work on any web browser. This revised new edition
makes it easy to find specific solutions for programming challenges.
<http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/phpckbk2>


***Pragmatic Unit Testing in C# with NUnit, Second Edition
Publisher: Pragmatic Bookshelf
ISBN: 0977616673
All over the world, software teams are using unit testing both to verify
their code and as a way of helping them design better code. This book is
unique in the way it covers two aspects: showing developers both how and 
what?
<http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/0977616673>


***Programming Python, Third Edition
Publisher: O'Reilly
ISBN: 0596009259
An industry standard just got even better. This bestseller from O'Reilly
has been updated to reflect the many changes introduced by Python 2.5.
It teaches the most common Python application domains from the ground up
with plenty of real-world examples and in-depth discussion. Ideal for
novices as well as advanced practitioners.
<http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/python3>


***The Relational Database Dictionary
Publisher: O'Reilly
ISBN: 0596527985
"The Relational Database Dictionary" is exactly what designers,
programmers, students, and database administrators (DBAs) need, a single
source where they can find precise definitions. Authored by C.J. Date, a
respected expert, this pocket-sized dictionary is the definitive
glossary of terms and concepts used with relational databases such as
Oracle products, IBM's DB2, Microsoft's SQL Server, MySQL, and
PostgreSQL. It provides structure and clarity that will help build
proficiency and accuracy in working with today's relational databases.
<http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/relationaldb>


***Ruby Cookbook
Publisher: O'Reilly
ISBN: 0596523696
>From data structures and algorithms, to integration with cutting-edge
technologies, the "Ruby Cookbook" has something for every programmer.
When you need to solve a problem, don't reinvent the wheel: look it up
in the Cookbook.
<http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/rubyckbk>


***Scripting VMware Power Tools
Publisher: Syngress
ISBN: 1597490598
This book covers the native tools that VMware provides with ESX Server.
It then discusses in detail the different scripting API's and how they
can be leveraged to provide some very useful, practical and time saving
tools to manage a virtual infrastructure. From virtual server
provisioning to backups and everything in between, all will be covered
in detail with real world examples that have been tested and will work
either copied directly from the book or with slight modifications for
the reader's specific environments.
<http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/1597490598>


***Stealing the Network
Publisher: Syngress
ISBN: 1597490814
The best-selling "Stealing the Network" series reaches its climactic
conclusion as law enforcement and organized crime form a high-tech web
in an attempt to bring down the shadowy hacker-villain known as Knuth in
the most technically sophisticated Stealing book yet. As with previous
titles, "How to Own a Shadow" is a fictional story that demonstrates
accurate, highly detailed scenarios of computer intrusions, and
counter-strikes.
<http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/1597490814>


***Stephen Johnson on Digital Photography
Publisher: O'Reilly
ISBN: 059652370X
A master photographer and teacher since 1977, Stephen Johnson is widely
recognized as a pioneer of digital photography. His new book chronicles
his ride on the bleeding edge of this medium's evolution, and provides a
practical in-depth introduction to digital photography that includes the
latest techniques. Complete with beautiful color photographic examples
and illustrations, this book is a unique, passionate, holistic
examination for every student of photography.
<http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/stephenjohnson>


***Ubuntu Linux for Non-Geeks
Publisher: No Starch Press
ISBN: 1593271182
This newbie's guide to Ubuntu lets readers learn by doing. Step-by-step
projects build upon earlier tutorial concepts and increase the reader's
understanding of topics such as installing new software; Internet
connectivity; working with removable devices; and even handling DVDs and
iPods. Average desktop users are eased into the world of commands, thus
allowing them to work with script-based applications; converting RPMs to
DEB files; and compiling software from source.
<http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/1593271182>


***Web Application Security
Publisher: Syngress
ISBN: 159749061X
This book defines web application security, why it should be addressed
earlier in the lifecycle in development and quality assurance, and how
it differs from other types of internet security. Additionally, the book
examines the procedures and technologies that are essential to
developing, penetration testing, and releasing a secure web application.
Through a review of recent web application breaches, the book will
expose the prolific methods hackers use to execute web attacks using
common vulnerabilities such as SQL Injection, Cross-Site Scripting, and
Buffer Overflows in the application layer. By taking an in-depth look at
the techniques hackers use to exploit web applications, readers will be
better equipped to protect confidential. 
<http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/159749061X>


***Windows Developer Power Tools (Rough Cuts)
Publisher: O'Reilly
ISBN: 0596527926
There is a wealth of open and free software available today for Windows
developers who want to extend the development environment, reduce
development effort and increase productivity. "Windows Developer Power
Tools" is an encyclopedic guide to more than 100 free and open source
tools available to programmers who build applications for Windows
desktops and servers, including web applications and services. With its
unique task-oriented organization, this book will help you find the
tools you need to solve common (and uncommon) problems. Each tool in the
book includes a capsule summary--a mini user's guide--to help
readers get up to speed quickly. This one-stop resource covers a wide range of open
source and freeware tools to help you answer questions around planning,
developing, testing, and rolling out great software.
<http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/windowsdpt>


***Windows for Intel Macs (PDF)
Publisher: O'Reilly
ISBN: 059652840X
Mac users can easily install and use Windows thanks to Boot Camp and
Parallels Desktop for Macs with Intel processors. But once you've
installed Windows, then what? This guide by Todd Ogasawara, a Microsoft
MVP and Mac journeyman, will help you unite Windows software with
Mac hardware.
<http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/windowsforintelmacs>


***Windows Media Center Wizardry (PDF)
Publisher: O'Reilly
ISBN:0596527845
"Windows Media Center Wizardy" is a guide to getting the most out of a
PC running Windows XP Media Center Edition 2005, delving into the hidden
features and extended capabilities not at first apparent when using this
extremely powerful interface. This book is written as a series of "How
To" sections, each concentrating on an area of functionality little
exploited by typical users, although extremely useful when exposed.
<http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/coolthings>

Open Source
---------------------
***Building a Desktop Firewall 
By now, many internet users know that they need a firewall to protect 
their computers while they're online. A nice GUI firewall 
builder called fwbuilder makes it possible to set up a working firewall 
in ten minutes--on Linux, *BSD, and Mac OS X. Dru Lavigne shows how it 
works on FreeBSD.
<http://www.onlamp.com/pub/a/bsd/2006/08/03/FreeBSDBasics.html>


***What Is OpenDocument?
The OpenDocument Format (ODF), an open source file format standard for
electronic office documents, is poised to change the world from an
application-centric model of computing to a document-centric model. Sam
Hiser looks at this new standard, how it implements XML for office
documents, the technical and political wranglings in the standard,
available tools, applications that offer ODF support, who's implementing
ODF, and more. 
<http://www.onlamp.com/pub/a/onlamp/2006/07/27/what-is-opendocument.html>


***Linux on the Nokia 770 Internet Tablet 
Nokia's 770 Internet Tablet is more than a phone, according to John
Littler. It's a Debian GNU/Linux system. This makes it a prime target
for hackers. Littler explores some of the built-in utilities and some of
the other tricks you can use.
<http://linux.oreillynet.com/pub/a/linux/2006/07/20/nokia-770.html>


***The CakePHP Framework
CakePHP is a mature framework for PHP developers who want the structure
and time-saving benefits of Ruby on Rails, without having to leave their
comfort zone.
<http://www.sitepoint.com/article/application-development-cakephp>

---------------------
Digital Media
---------------------
***Review: Frontier Design TranzPort
This wireless, two-way remote control won acclaim in the pro audio
community for simplifying computer recording. Now it works with iTunes
as well. Songwriter Spencer Critchley tests it with iTunes and Reason,
discovering how to loop song sections, create remote playlists, and even
tranzform iTunes into a foot-controlled backup band.
<http://digitalmedia.oreilly.com/2006/07/20/review-frontier-design-tranzport.html>


***Automate Your Favorite Photoshop Routines
If you find yourself performing the same tasks over and over again in
Photoshop, it's time for you to tap the power of actions. Scott Bourne
shows you how.
<http://digitalmedia.oreilly.com/2006/07/13/automating-photoshop-routines.html>

---------------------
Mac
---------------------
***So Del.icio.us! Intelligent Tagging for the Mac User 
It's time to learn about del.icio.us, a glorious, minimalist, tag-tastic
bookmarks database. It was designed to be a "large-scale outboard
memory," with added sharing smarts. It works like this: you take all
your digital stuff and throw it into your del.icio.us account. Thanks to
tagging and searching, you can find your stuff easily later. And you can
find other people's stuff, too. Giles Turnbull shows you how.
<http://www.macdevcenter.com/pub/a/mac/2006/07/18/delicious.html>


***How Does Open Source Software Stack Up on the Mac? 
Apple does a great job of providing elegant software for its platform.
But there are plenty of good offerings beyond the fruits of Cupertino.
In this amazing survey of proprietary and open source software, Matthew
Russell attempts to organize what's available on both fronts and even
dares to assign grades. Has he missed anything?
<http://www.macdevcenter.com/pub/a/mac/2006/07/25/software-stack-for-the-mac.html>

---------------------
Windows/.NET
---------------------
***Best Windows Admin Downloads 
There are more than 9,000 tools, templates, white papers, and other
items available from the Microsoft Download Center. Which are the best
for Windows administrators? Mitch Tulloch clues you in.
<http://www.oreillynet.com/pub/a/windows/2006/07/18/best-windows-admin-downloads.html>


***Drag and Drop Ajax Programming With Atlas 
Think you need to write scripts and use JavaScript if you want to write
Atlas apps? Think again. Jesse Liberty shows you an easier, more
effective, and more productive way--using drag-and-drop programming.
<http://www.windowsdevcenter.com/pub/a/windows/2006/08/01/drag-and-drop-ajax-programming-with-atlas.html>

---------------------
Java
---------------------
***Deploying BIRT 
If you have a need for generating or displaying reports, you may want to
have a word with BIRT. The Business Intelligence and Reporting Tools
project is an open source Eclipse effort to enable the creation and
deployment of complex reports. Jason Weathersby shows you how to grind
out reports and display them in web applications and RCP-based desktop
apps.
<http://www.onjava.com/pub/a/onjava/2006/07/26/deploying-birt.html>


***What Is Geronimo? 
Geronimo is Apache's open source, Java EE-compatible application server,
based on a flexible system of interchangeable components. This makes it
ideal for a wide range of enterprise deployments. In this article, Kunal
Jaggi shows you how to get started with Geronimo.
<http://www.onjava.com/pub/a/onjava/2006/07/19/what-is-geronimo.html>

---------------------
MAKE
---------------------
***Receive MAKE's "Weekend Project" PDF Podcast Through iTunes

Picnic Geometry:
In this weeks video edition of Weekend Projects, learn to make geometric 
spheres from all sorts of things you might find at a picnic. Watch the 
video to see how to build an icosohedron out of paper plates, cups, and 
plastic sheeting. This is a great project to do as a family, just make 
sure you have enough supplies for each person involved to make one.
If possible, use recycled materials.
<http://www.makezine.com/blog/archive/2006/08/make_podcast_weekend_projects_3.html>

Recent projects include:
"Make A Kite Aerial Photography Rig"
<http://www.makezine.com/blog/archive/2006/07/weekend_project_instructions_m.html>

Rodent Powered Nightlight
<http://www.makezine.com/blog/archive/2006/07/>
weekend_project_instructions_r.html

From bobby_miesen at yahoo.com  Sat Aug  5 22:05:39 2006
From: bobby_miesen at yahoo.com (Robert Jeffrey Miesen)
Date: Sat, 5 Aug 2006 22:05:39 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Does anyone know of references that detail how to study
	Source Code?
In-Reply-To: <44CF241C.6080903@digitree.org>
References: <200608010112.08674.bobby_miesen@yahoo.com>
	<44CF241C.6080903@digitree.org>
Message-ID: <200608052205.39185.bobby_miesen@yahoo.com>

Thank you for your help. This will help me out tremendously. 


On Tuesday 01 August 2006 02:51, Daniel Hedlund wrote:
> Robert Jeffrey Miesen wrote:
> > Hi.
> >
> > 	I'm currently tasked with the job of learning how the internals (source
> > code) of two large programs work---namely <a
> > href="http://www.moodle.org">Moodle</a> and <a
> > href="http://www.civicspacelabs.org">Civicspace</a>, a derivative of <a
> > href="http://www.drupal.com">Drupal</a>. Although I know how to program
> > at least semi-competently, I have never really taken on the task of
> > learning how the internals of a large program work.
>
> I'm assuming that you're planning on modifying the mentioned products in
> some way or adding additional features, not merely using their public
> APIs.  With most source code, I think one of the only ways is simply to
> dive in.  You may want to learn the basics of the programming languages
> that the source code is written in first, but it's not necessary because
> you'll probably learn the language as you read and try to understand the
> source anyway.  Many projects come with at least some development
> documentation such as their public API, class structures, etc.  It is
> worth familiarizing yourself with this information, and you should also
> join any related [development] mailing lists if you have not yet done
> so.  I doubt there will be that many books on the internal workings of
> any particular product; if so, they will usually be out of date.
>
> Some tips based on how I personally tackle new source code:
> 1.  try to read as much information about the product as you can and try
> to understand the bigger picture.  Maybe try to use the application from
> a user's point of view.
> 2.  look for any API or internal-related documentation on their official
> website(s); try to determine how pieces fit together as much as possible.
> 3.  download and install the source code, play around with the
> management interfaces (command-line options, configuration settings,
> etc) and get a feel for how they affect the product.
> 4.  start to slowly examine the source code looking for features that
> are familiar to you (either from public APIs or configuration settings)
> and track these changes through the source code.  Some useful places to
> start is a main() type function if they have one, or from the outermost
> code that interacts with the user in some form; these are the best
> places to start because you are already somewhat familiar with what they
> are supposed to do.
> 5.  document anything that appears interesting to you or things you do
> not immediately understand.  You can do this in the source code using
> comments, possibly with special markers to make them easier to
> find/search ("// !!! I don't know what this does, might be related to
> ... !!! //").  It's also worth writing down on paper a little bit bigger
> piece of the picture such as how some functions and/or classes fit
> together if there is not already good documentation on this.  I find
> documenting things (especially in source) very important for me to learn
> things, even if there is some documentation already.  The actually
> process of documenting helps you learn; sometimes I will comment every
> single line of source code in a function with a comment that is a
> English sentence equivalent of what the code should do.  In theory, I
> could strip all the code, leaving only comments and be able to read
> and/or rewrite the code from scratch if I wanted to.
> 6.  start to make minor modifications to the source code and see if the
> changes you make produce the results you expect.  Some examples of this
> could be resizing a window or changing a color.  You're basically
> starting to test yourself to see if you can predict the behavior of the
> program.  If you can, you're well on your way.
>
> Cheers,
>
> Daniel Hedlund
> daniel at digitree.org
> _______________________________________________
> PLUG mailing list
> PLUG at lists.pdxlinux.org
> http://lists.pdxlinux.org/mailman/listinfo/plug

-- 

Robert Miesen

From larry.brigman at gmail.com  Sun Aug  6 18:32:26 2006
From: larry.brigman at gmail.com (Larry Brigman)
Date: Sun, 6 Aug 2006 18:32:26 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] HD and smartd offline sectors
In-Reply-To: <44D67072.2010609@pacificscreening.com>
References: <44D67072.2010609@pacificscreening.com>
Message-ID: <ca5165530608061832w17ea3e50n3c5038dfcfc1c6d@mail.gmail.com>

On 8/6/06, Robert McIntosh <robert at pacificscreening.com> wrote:
> Smartd reports 38 offline uncorrectable errors every half hour in
> /var/log/messages on my installation of FC5.  There are no other
> messages from smartd regarding this drive.
>
> I know I should replace the drive, but has the system marked these
> sectors as bad and not trying to write new data to them?  I have good
> back-ups and am not too worried about the situation.  Run or walk to the
>   hardware store?
>

If you can run the long smart test and comes back with no
errors, then you just had a few bad spots on the drive.
The problem with un-correctable errors is that the firmware of
modern drives won't re-allocate un-recoverable sectors, it
requires manual intervention.

smartd drive status should report good, then your ok.
A few bad sectors don't qualify as a dying drive.

   The result is that my system was FUBAR this morning. I got X11 running

*** glibc detected *** free(): invalid pointer: 0x0810b2e8 ***

jpilot from the original (freshly downloaded) package, but that didn't
change the situation.

   I don't understand what the error message is telling me. There's nothing
in /var/log/messages. I cannot find where the pidfile was.

   Your collective wisdom and focused insight is greatly appreciated in
helping me get this application running again.

Rich
