that message indicates that a subcomponent of glibc detected badness, and the 
program that was trying to execute this badness was trying to free memory that 
it didn't own (invalid pointer).

That's a huge bug in either jpilot or any of the libraries that jpilot uses 
(gtk+-2 etc). it should be reported upstream, and perhaps already has been.

>   Your collective wisdom and focused insight is greatly appreciated in
> helping me get this application running again.

unfortunately I fear that downgrading gtk+-2 is your best option. Gtk+ seems 
to be very unstable the last period, and many non-backwards-compatible changes 
have gone in recently, driving some of my fellow coders insane. 2.6 or 2.8 
seem a better choice. Perhaps it helps...

Cheers,

Auke

Rich Shepard wrote:
> On Mon, 7 Aug 2006, Auke Kok wrote:
> 
>> that message indicates that a subcomponent of glibc detected badness, and
>> the program that was trying to execute this badness was trying to free
>> memory that it didn't own (invalid pointer).
> 
> Auke,
> 
>   Yeah, I got that. Why, when I dropped back to previous versions and the
> error still occured, is beyone my ken.
> 
>> That's a huge bug in either jpilot or any of the libraries that jpilot
>> uses (gtk+-2 etc). it should be reported upstream, and perhaps already 
>> has
>> been.
> 
>   There's the rub. The same jpilot-0.99.8 has been running here since last
> November without a problem. Heck, I've never before had _any_ problem with
> jpilot. I re-installed the Freerock Gnome (to get gnucash running again),
> and that put gtk+2-2.8.12 back on the box.
> 
>   Neither of these caused any problems before I dug the hole into which I
> fell.
> 
>> unfortunately I fear that downgrading gtk+-2 is your best option. Gtk+
>> seems to be very unstable the last period, and many 
>> non-backwards-compatible changes have gone in recently, driving some 
>> of my
>> fellow coders insane. 2.6 or 2.8 seem a better choice. Perhaps it 
>> helps...
> 
>   Any other thoughts or magic incantations that might help? :-)

well, if you run `ldd /usr/bin/jpilot` you'd probably see it pull in about 200 
libraries. My guess is that some gtk+ related library is mucking about too, 
and glib-2 comes to mind immediately. Other than putting traps and debugging 
the whole thing, I would see if downgrading that helps. You should have at 
least the same glib2 version as the gtk+-2 version installed.

Auke

From wcooley at nakedape.cc  Mon Aug  7 10:02:29 2006
From: wcooley at nakedape.cc (Wil Cooley)
Date: Mon, 07 Aug 2006 17:02:29 +0000
Subject: [PLUG] creating a bootable FreeDOS USB flash drive
In-Reply-To: <20060807161653.51AA68F0D@tinman.seitzassoc.com>
References: <20060807161653.51AA68F0D@tinman.seitzassoc.com>
Message-ID: <1154970149.7220.22.camel@willow.odshp.com>

On Mon, 2006-08-07 at 09:16 -0700, Galen Seitz wrote:

> No, I hadn't considered GRUB.  I assumed that it needed to reside in
> an ext2/3 filesystem.  If it can live on a FAT filesystem, I'll have
> to give it a try.

Well, I think GRUB will work on a FAT filesystem, but that's not really
the point--the point is that the existing GRUB on your HD (assuming, of
course, you're using GRUB and not LILO) can boot the image directly.
Hm, well, now that I think about it, I think you'll still need memdisk
from syslinux.  Something like this in your /boot/grub/menu.lst, after
coyping memdisk and your FreeDOS floppy image to /boot:

title FreeDOS boot
  kernel /memdisk 
  initrd /freedos.img

Not being one to recommend things I cannot try too easily, I just did it
and it works.  My point, here, is to try to avoid booting off USB, which
too often doesn't on my lousy old hardware.

Wil
-- 
Wil Cooley <wcooley at nakedape.cc>
Naked Ape Consulting, Ltd

From galens at seitzassoc.com  Mon Aug  7 10:03:27 2006
From: galens at seitzassoc.com (Galen Seitz)

Subject: [PLUG] centos4.3 rpmbuild not working as normal user
Message-ID: <20060807170332.6FBDE8F0D@tinman.seitzassoc.com>


In the past I have been able to build rpm packages on my CentOS 4.3
(x86_64) system as a normal user, but something has broken.  Now the
prep and build stages seem to be NOPs, causing the install stage to
complain about missing files.

I'm using the cdecl source rpm as distributed by CentOS for testing.
This is a small and presumably known-good package.

Any ideas what might be wrong?

galen


[galens at toto ~]$ rpm -qi rpm-build
Name        : rpm-build                    Relocations: (not relocatable)
Version     : 4.3.3                             Vendor: CentOS
Release     : 13_nonptl                     Build Date: Thu 09 Mar 2006 01:02:49 AM PST

Description :
The rpm-build package contains the scripts and executable programs
that are used to build packages using the RPM Package Manager.

not get created under BUILD.

Auke,

   It's no where near 200 libraries, but there are about 50. It's finding
glib-2, which does not track the gtk+2 version numbers. glib2-2.8.6 from the
freerock gnome project is installed, and jpilot is finding that library
without difficulties. Running ldd shows nothing unresolved.

   This is a particularly frustrating error to find and fix as I've not had
problems with jpilot since Judd first released it a number of years ago. I'm
sure it will turn out to be some simple missing package, but I've no idea
which one.

   I've asked for ideas on the jpilot mail list, too.

Many thanks,

Rich

> 
> The files in /var/tmp are being removed and I don't know how to 
> prevent that.

I don't think rpmbuild provides an option for keeping temp files. What 
I've typically done is pressed Ctrl-c shortly after the temp script 
begins its execution run. The temp file won't be cleaned up if 
rpmbuild is killed that way.

Honestly, I would much rather put the money I would spend on the warranty
into a decent investment when I buy the laptop.  I do this for three
reasons:
1. Depreciation - By the time the manufacturers warranty is up often times
the computer is worth about the same or just slightly more than the amount
spent for the extedded warranty.
2. If I get a lemon laptop that keeps requiring repairs, use the money I put
aside to buy a different brand or model of laptop, extedded manufacturer
warranties effectively lock you into that model of laptop.
3. None of the money is getting wasted to pay people who's job it is to find
some reason the repair isn't covered.
4. The money can be used to replace the battery
5. I can use the money in the investment to upgrade to a new laptop if I
don't use it for repairs.

Like you said the warranty is a bet.  Just like in Vegas the companies
offering the warranty have done the math and made sure they will on average
make more money off of you than they pay out.

From heinlein at madboa.com  Mon Aug  7 11:36:40 2006
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Mon, 7 Aug 2006 11:36:40 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] centos4.3 rpmbuild not working as normal user 
In-Reply-To: <20060807180626.D241A8F0D@tinman.seitzassoc.com>
References: <20060807180626.D241A8F0D@tinman.seitzassoc.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.OSX.4.64.0608071116180.1153@arrowhead.galois.com>

On Mon, 7 Aug 2006, Galen Seitz wrote:

> > I'm guessing you tried 'df /var/tmp'?  Also, I think if you put 
> > 'exit 1' in the appropriate section the tmp file will not be 
> > removed (not verifying this time :)
> 
> Doh!!!  Thank you!  I recently caught up on a bunch of CentOS 
> updates. I thought maybe one of the new packages had broken 
> something, but it was just that the yum cache filled /var.

So some of the reserved filesystem blocks were used when root ran the 
rpm process, resulting in a normal rpm-tmp file in /var/tmp, but your 
normal user account saw 0 free blocks.

That's a good symptom set to tuck away in the ol' gray matter. :-)

Oh, in case someone doesn't know -- most Unix filesystems will reserve 
a certain percentage of blocks purely for root's use, just for this 
sort of case when a filesystem fills up. By default, ext2 and ext3 
reserve 5% of the filesystem blocks for root, though on quite large 
filesystems I usually reduce that to 2%.

You can see your current settings using tune2fs:

  tune2fs -l /dev/hda1 | grep -i 'block count'

Using tune2fs, you can also adjust the percentage of reserved 
filesystem blocks.

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

From heinlein at madboa.com  Mon Aug  7 13:10:35 2006
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Mon, 7 Aug 2006 13:10:35 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Laptops and warranties
In-Reply-To: <321871a60608071125m14a3312by234fbf5e4539af06@mail.gmail.com>
References: <200607311631.34278.dalefv@k-com.net>
	<20060801123037.GB21705@gate.kl-ic.com>
	<Pine.OSX.4.64.0608011420010.687@arrowhead.galois.com>
	<200608012139.23846.aschlemm@comcast.net>
	<321871a60608071125m14a3312by234fbf5e4539af06@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.OSX.4.64.0608071137430.1153@arrowhead.galois.com>

On Mon, 7 Aug 2006, Dwight Hubbard wrote:

> Like you said the warranty is a bet.  Just like in Vegas the 
> companies offering the warranty have done the math and made sure 
> they will on average make more money off of you than they pay out.

The trick is in how they cover the warranty expenses.

I've heard from fairly reliable sources that of the types of insurance 
carried by most people -- life, health, homeowners, and auto -- only 
auto policies are able to create profits on their own. The others are 
all break-even or losing propositions.

Companies that offer those types of policies are often little more 
than brokerage houses -- using premiums as cash flow to fund their 
investment portfolios.

With laptops and warranties, the question becomes, do laptop 
manufacturers fund warranty work strictly from warranty fees or do 
they underwrite part of the warranty expenses by building some extra 
cost into all machines.

I can imagine the logic working this way:

 * people who buy laptops are not cheapskates

 * people who buy warranties are not cheapskates

 * people who buy warranties are risk-adverse and likely to stick
   with a brand that works rather than buying from this month's
   lowest-price vendor

 * it's worth encouraging a longer term relationship with these
   folks, since there's a reasonable chance that this type of
   person *wants* to be a repeat customer and avoid changing
   vendors

 * let's underwrite part of the warranty costs with a mild price
   bump on all laptops

The corporation loses only if a huge percentage of customers purchase 
warranties. It actually makes more money if fewer customers do, since 
everyone is paying part of that cost anyway.

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

On Mon, Aug 07, 2006 at 11:25:57AM -0700, Dwight Hubbard wrote:
> Honestly, I would much rather put the money I would spend on the warranty
> into a decent investment when I buy the laptop.  I do this for three
> reasons:
> 1. Depreciation - By the time the manufacturers warranty is up often times
> the computer is worth about the same or just slightly more than the amount
> spent for the extedded warranty.
> 2. If I get a lemon laptop that keeps requiring repairs, use the money I put
> aside to buy a different brand or model of laptop, extedded manufacturer
> warranties effectively lock you into that model of laptop.
> 3. None of the money is getting wasted to pay people who's job it is to find
> some reason the repair isn't covered.
> 4. The money can be used to replace the battery
> 5. I can use the money in the investment to upgrade to a new laptop if I
> don't use it for repairs.
> 
> Like you said the warranty is a bet.  Just like in Vegas the companies
> offering the warranty have done the math and made sure they will on average
> make more money off of you than they pay out.

There is nothing wrong with companies making money, IF they provide
value.  Keep in mind that part of their warranty calculation involves
keeping you as a customer rather than losing you to the competition
(worth $), and part of it involves stocking spares, keeping technicians
trained, etc.  The high prices charged for out-of-warranty repairs are
indicative of the high costs associated with unpredictable repair
demands.  It is a bet, but it is not Las Vegas, more like commodities
futures.  A warranty provides assurance to both parties.

In my case, I started with a very good laptop (a Thinkpad T30) with 
features I like a lot (trackpoint, decent speed, good quality).  It
is not a "lemon" per se, but it has needed quite a few repairs, mostly
due to getting a LOT of use.  I prefer its feature set to many of the
follow-on models,  and migrating to a new laptop ranges from annoying
to painful.  The relatively high price for used T30s on Ebay, years
after they stopped making them, indicates that others think the T30
is a good machine, too.  I'd buy a new one if they still made them.

Junking 2 year old laptops is not virtuous - if the hardware still
meets the need, it should be maintained.  So, assuming that I keep
it (earning 2x its depreciated market price per week), and that it
will need repairs from time to time,  what is the best way to do so? 
Warranty is a bargain, for me and for the manufacturer.  

Yes, there are bad warranties out there.  The IBM->Levono->Selectron
warranty is trending towards suckiness (and many manufacturers have
outsourced their repair to Selectron).  I am considering alternatives,
but I still may buy Levono next time, if that turns out to be the best
warranty available.  I consider a good warranty an important product
feature, and an comprehensive warranty indicates the manufacturer's
confidence in the quality of their product.  

You say bad warranty service is not worth it - I agree.  But that
does not mean that good warranty service is also worthless.  If you
have ever had excellent repair service, such as I have had in the 
past from IBM for my laptops, or in other domains (for cars from
State Farm / Heitzmann, for example), you would be less enamored
of the "disposable crap" approach to life.  There are still people
that take pride in quality, and I enjoy basking in their sunshine.

Keith

Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993

only way to get service was to stay "on script".  On *their* script.

got decent service when they switched us to the "corporate" support
line down in TX.

From ianburrell at gmail.com  Mon Aug  7 17:41:13 2006
From: ianburrell at gmail.com (Ian Burrell)
Date: Mon, 7 Aug 2006 17:41:13 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Fedora Core 5 errors...
In-Reply-To: <1154851400.44d5a2482ec01@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
References: <1154851400.44d5a2482ec01@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <d91f09cd0608071741j13b204d1x533688d92b570840@mail.gmail.com>

On 8/6/06, plug_0 at robinson-west.com <plug_0 at robinson-west.com> wrote:
>
> I've decided to work on picking up Fedora Core 5.  Like Fedora Core 3, every
> disc other than the first cd fail the disc test.  Is this normal if you don't
> go the DVD route?  Has one who burned the cds for Fedora Core 5 gotten all
> 5 to pass the media test?
>

Having the media checks fail is a pretty common problem.  There is a
long-standing bug in the Linux kernel where it miscalculates the
length of CD and this results in read errors or the wrong checksum.
Normal reading from the CD is okay.

One solution is to pass ide=dma as kernel option.

 - Ian

From dwight.hubbard at gmail.com  Tue Aug  8 10:25:23 2006
From: dwight.hubbard at gmail.com (Dwight Hubbard)
Date: Tue, 8 Aug 2006 10:25:23 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Laptops and warranties
In-Reply-To: <20060807132424.GB22490@gate.kl-ic.com>
References: <200607311631.34278.dalefv@k-com.net>
	<20060801123037.GB21705@gate.kl-ic.com>
	<Pine.OSX.4.64.0608011420010.687@arrowhead.galois.com>
	<200608012139.23846.aschlemm@comcast.net>
	<321871a60608071125m14a3312by234fbf5e4539af06@mail.gmail.com>
	<20060807132424.GB22490@gate.kl-ic.com>
Message-ID: <321871a60608081025g1351a4bn225225d47717c922@mail.gmail.com>

Exactly, warranties have to be evaluated for what you are getting out of
them.  Many times people buy warranties and insurance based on feelings
(usually fear) instead of taking the time to evaluate the risk/rewards of
the warranty.

Generally I think warranties make more sense on expensive laptops than on
cheaper ones.  However, it is really important to consider what isn't
covered by the warranty.  If you have a manufacturer warranty of 1 year and
your visa card doubles it to 2 years, what will the covered part of your
computer be worth?  I.E. replacement cost minus the cost of the battery and
any other non-warranty covered components.

Example:
$1000 Laptop with one year manufacturer warranty.  With a 3 year extended
warranty for $300.

If the laptop totally failed after that 2 years and you had the 3 year
extended warranty.  You would receive a refurbished laptop which has 1 year
of warranty coverage remaining and have a useless laptop battery that cost
$100+ that needs to be replaced.  While the laptop is being repaired, you
don't have a replacement.  You can also potentially run into other issues
with the laptop that aren't covered in the fine print.

Purchase the same laptop with a Visa card that doubles the manufacturer
warranty.  The laptop has warranty coverage for 2 years.   At no additional
cost.

Invest the $300 you would have spent on the extended warranty after 2 years
you would have anywhere from $310-$380 depending on how it's invested.   For
which you buy a new laptop with similar specs to the one that failed which
after 2 years is about half the original cost and has a good battery and
once again you have 2 years of warranty coverage.  Out of pocket cost
$120-$200 (not much more than the cost of a replacement battery).

Which is the better deal?  The second option has a bit more potential out of
pocket cost worst case, but if the laptop doesn't fail you still have your
money.  If it fails after 3 years, you still have your money, if it fails
after 5 years you still have the money.  If you want to run a new
application that requires a faster laptop, you can sell the old one on ebay
and use the money set aside for repairs as part of the purchase price on
another laptop.  And if you use the money you invested to purchase another
laptop, it has the manufacturer's warranty again.  Obviously for a more
expensive laptop the warranty might be a better deal, but then that isn't
necessarily so.

Bruce Kilpatrick wanted to send a picture to the list - the list
does not accept attachments.  The best way to send along a picture,
a program, a computer virus, etc. is to put it up on a website
somewhere and then post the link.

Someday, PLUG will have a wiki.  Until then, I maintain a few wikis
which permit page attachments, and I can provide access to people
I know.  Contact me off the list.

only in packs of 5 for the Cat6 ($16.99) and 10 for the Cat5e ($29.99).

$30. That's not a huge difference.

just that my wiring project needs them *now*. (I thought I had bought
them already, so here I sit unable to finish until I get them.) I can
also get Cat5e on eBay for far less. I thought of doing what you did --
use Cat6 cable (which I have already bought), and Cat5e jacks. After
all, the bandwidth I have today isn't going to come close to taxing
even Cat5e. If somewhere in the future I need full Cat6 bandwidth, I
can just replace the jacks.

However, the dude at Fry's said that the Cat5e are Leviton and the Cat6
are not -- although he couldn't tell the brand name. Then he said that
"Keystone" and "Leviton" are not interchangeable. That is, you can't
use a Keystone jack in a Leviton wall plate and vice-versa. That's the
part that sounds bogus to me. I have already bought the plates, and
they are Leviton. And I have *one* Cat6 jack that I got at Fry's, and
it fits the Leviton plate just fine. I think he was making stuff up. 

I guess tomorrow I'll call ENU, Norvac and other local suspects to see
what I can turn up.

What about your project requires Cat6? From my days in the office at 
Electrical Construction Company's Voice & Data division, I know that 
there is a significant difference between each of the levels, but only 
if you are trying to work at the extremes of performance. My 
recollections are rusty, but as I recall, Cat5 was good enough for 10Mb, 
Cat5e for 100Mb, and Cat6 for Gigabit speeds. Around the house, I get by 
fine with 10Mb. I can see where some folks might want 100Mb around the 
house, but I suspect Gigabit within the house would be more than most 
folks require. Then, again, I'm not trying to push a lot of video over 
my Intranet.

> > recollections are rusty, but as I recall, Cat5 was good enough for 10Mb,
> > Cat5e for 100Mb, and Cat6 for Gigabit speeds. Around the house, I get by
> > fine with 10Mb. I can see where some folks might want 100Mb around the
> > house, but I suspect Gigabit within the house would be more than most
> > folks require. Then, again, I'm not trying to push a lot of video over
> > my Intranet.
>
> You are absolutely correct. I do not need Cat6.
>
> [Ahem] years ago a computer salesperson, in attempting to sell me an
> IBM clone for $1499, far less than IBM wanted for their version,
> informed me that it was the last computer I would ever need to buy.
> That was, he assured me, because it would be impossible in one man's
> lifetime to fill up its state-of-the-art 5 MB hard drive.
>
> A speed of 100 Mb is more than adequate today. But ten years from now,
> after the internet matter transporter is invented, it will never do.
> That is, unless you want it to take all day to download a cup of coffee
> from the ethernet-ready coffee maker in your kitchen.
>

Cat5e will do 1000Base-T (ie Gigbit) 100mhz
Cat6 is speced to 250mhz so it could do 2-4 Gigibit fibre channel over copper

Both of need to be of runs less than 90meters to run speced speed.
There is a Cat6a which is rated to do 10Gb.


From ehem at m5p.com  Tue Aug  8 21:53:01 2006
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Tue, 8 Aug 2006 21:53:01 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Cat-6 keystone jacks in Portland
In-Reply-To: <20060809042850.5DFCD417A@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200608090453.k794r2Et026416@m5p.com>

>From: "Richard C. Steffens" <rsteff at comcast.net>
> What about your project requires Cat6? From my days in the office at 
> Electrical Construction Company's Voice & Data division, I know that 
> there is a significant difference between each of the levels, but only 
> if you are trying to work at the extremes of performance. My 
> recollections are rusty, but as I recall, Cat5 was good enough for 10Mb, 
> Cat5e for 100Mb, and Cat6 for Gigabit speeds. Around the house, I get by 
> fine with 10Mb. I can see where some folks might want 100Mb around the 
> house, but I suspect Gigabit within the house would be more than most 
> folks require. Then, again, I'm not trying to push a lot of video over 
> my Intranet.

I'm wanting to say Cat3 is sufficient for 10mb, but I'll be safe and say
Cat4 is sufficient for 10mb. Cat5 will do 100mb, in short runs might
handle 1000mb, but isn't supposed to. Cat5e will do 10gb over a pair of
cables (very short, perhaps 3-6 meters _officially_).

Nothing to my knowledge requires Cat6. In fact I'm not entirely sure
there is an official specification for Cat6. Failing that cabling labeled
"Category 6" was out well before any specification was.

If you don't believe me, I'll just need to grab whichever expert is handy
for an authoritative source (though Google might be faster).

From m0gely at telestream.com  Tue Aug  8 22:19:17 2006
From: m0gely at telestream.com (m0gely)

Subject: [PLUG] Cat-6 keystone jacks in Portland
In-Reply-To: <200608090453.k794r2Et026416@m5p.com>
References: <200608090453.k794r2Et026416@m5p.com>
Message-ID: <44D97055.9060806@telestream.com>

Elliott Mitchell wrote:

> Nothing to my knowledge requires Cat6. In fact I'm not entirely sure
> there is an official specification for Cat6. Failing that cabling labeled
> "Category 6" was out well before any specification was.

It's absolutely required.  Who in their right mind would wire a building 
with old tech?  It's not easy to replace this stuff when the dry rock is 
up and the paint is on.  I get into this with low volt guys all the 
time.  People should use what's "current" in cases like this. Gb over 
copper won't last forever.  It's going to find it's limit in some new 
application that will require higher speeds that Cat5e won't do.

> If you don't believe me, I'll just need to grab whichever expert is handy
> for an authoritative source (though Google might be faster).

The Cat6 specification *is* has been done for some time.

-- 
- m0gely


From m0gely at telestream.com  Tue Aug  8 22:29:30 2006
From: m0gely at telestream.com (m0gely)
Date: Tue, 08 Aug 2006 22:29:30 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Cat-6 keystone jacks in Portland
In-Reply-To: <20060808181043.f5e844c8.johnxj@comcast.net>

	<20060808181043.f5e844c8.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <44D972BA.4000803@telestream.com>

John Jason Jordan wrote:

> However, the dude at Fry's said that the Cat5e are Leviton and the Cat6
> are not -- although he couldn't tell the brand name. Then he said that
> "Keystone" and "Leviton" are not interchangeable.

Ah fudge.  You said "jacks" and I thought "plug".  It was the plugs I 
had in Cat5e, the jacks were Cat6.  Anyway, I still don't think it 
matters.  This is the last inch we're talking about here.  The secret 
sauce of Cat6 is in the cable itself.  Tighter twists at varying 
frequency and slightly larger diameter in the copper.  None of that 
exists in any jacks anyway, so I wouldn't worry about replacing them in 
the future.  You might be able to get a low volt guy out with a tester 
that supports bandwidth testing.  It will tell you what your lines will run.

Here's what I would do.  Look up Home Automation installers or Low 
voltage electrical contractors.  They may carry this stuff in stock. 
I'm buddies with the owner of an electrical contractor who like Leviton. 
  I will give him a call tomorrow and see if there is a local pickup for 
Leviton stuff in the area.

I might have a few extra left I could sell you, but not 30.  I would 
have to go out to the building I did and look in the big box-o-extra 
parts.  They are Leviton.

-- 
- m0gely

From johnxj at comcast.net  Wed Aug  9 20:08:44 2006
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Wed, 9 Aug 2006 20:08:44 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Cat-6 keystone jacks in Portland
In-Reply-To: <20060809054843.GB10487@gate.kl-ic.com>
References: <20060808172018.fb0150dc.johnxj@comcast.net>
	<20060809054843.GB10487@gate.kl-ic.com>
Message-ID: <20060809200844.bc27dece.johnxj@comcast.net>

Thanks to all for the suggestions. 

First, Graybar has three brands, including an ample supply in one brand
of the color I want (ivory) in stock, but of the three brands the
cheapest price is $5.58 each, for quantity 30.

Norvac has none in stock, takes seven to ten working days to order, and
wants $4.30 each, just in case I'm willing to sit around and wait.

PC Heidens is too far. Fry's, "the store you love to hate," seemed the
best choice. So this morning I drove 30 miles down to Fry's, cursing
George Bush and his oil friends every expensive mile of the way.

When I got there I discovered that they had only 15 in stock. I put
them in my shopping basket anyway. I also needed some blank fillers,
but all they had was one package of white ones. Oh well. Those are not
urgent. I can get them later elsewhere.

And I also needed more cable. Previously I had bought 100 m of Cat6
cable from Fry's but, after reconsidering my networking requirements, I
had decided I needed another 300 feet or so. I searched everywhere in
the networking area, but all they had was boxes of 1,000 feet. From
last night online I knew that the same cable I had bought before was
available on outpost.com. I asked employees where it might be, but all
I could get was a shrug of the shoulders. "Might there be more in the
back," I asked. This elicited the standard employee answer "all we have
is out on display." This from an employee standing next to the entrance
to the back stock area where additional boxes of cabling were plainly
visible. OK, I know his job is to get rid of me so he can go back to
chatting with the other employees, but you'd think he could be more
creative. I might have even accepted the brush-off gracefully if it had
been artfully contrived. Really, these people need some new lines.

I decided to give up on the floor staff and go to the checkout counter.
Perhaps they have access to a computer with a searchable inventory
list. (Why doesn't that lady who tells you which checker is free have a
clacker like the ladies in front of the elevators used to have at
downtown Meier and Frank -- "third floor, ladies apparel, bagatelle"?
She totally needs a clacker. "Checker #17 is free <clack>.")

I got to the checkout counter and asked the person behind the cash
register if she could check to see if they had Cat6 cable in stock,
other than in spools of 1,000 feet. Her response was "huh?" OK, her job
description is to scan the barcode and enter the numbers manually if it
won't scan. Other problems she is to refer to a supervisor. She
discharged the latter duty and a supervisor showed up. I repeated the
question, pointing out that it was on outlook.com, and could they
please check their inventory in the computer database. It took 10
minutes, but she finally succeded in verifying that they had 23 boxes
of 1,000 feet of cable, but none of smaller quantities. Then she
assured me, sotto voce, as though it were a state secret, that
outpost.com has nothing to do with Fry's.

For some reason that did it. My annoyance escalated to a high level of
pissedoffitude. "Fine," I said. "It appears I am going to have to go to
PC Heidens to get the cable I need. Since their prices on everything
else I have in the basket here are a bit lower, I won't be needing any
of the rest of these things." Whereupon I turned and walked out. <Note
to self: You really need to develop that haughty twist and turn of the
head for scenes like this. Check out queenie gay flics.>

On the way out the door it occurred to me that I had never been to PC
Heidens in Hillsboro. "No problem," I thought. "I'll just look them up
in that funny phone book that I keep in the car." But that was not to
be. They are not listed in the Verizon Portland area phone book. OK,
back into the store. Walking up to the greeter, I asked if there was a
Portland phone book around. "Portland phone book?" she inquired "Sonny,
you're in Wilsonville." OK, she didn't say "sonny," but that was her
intent. I could tell from the prosody of the sentence. I'm a
linguistics major.

After a few minutes trying to figure out how to divine the location of
PC Heidens without a computer, a phone book, or a phone number, I gave
up. I decided to visit that great Thriftway in the shopping area next
to Fry's. They have a very nice wine section, where I found a bottle of
Chaucer's Mead. OK, Chaucer died 700 years ago, so I doubt this is his
personal production. Nevertheless, it is a fitting reminder for History
of English spring term, and will make a nice end to an otherwise
fruitless, annoying and exasperating day.

----

Back home, it is 4:30 and there is still time to call surplusdepot.com
and order 30 Cat6 keystone jacks for $2.22 each. I do so, and they will
be here Tuesday. The network wiring will have to wait. I totally hate
it when my plans get derailed, but sometimes we must become
philosophical. The wiring this week was not meant to be. In the
meantime, the Attitude Adjustment Hour is upon us. Nobody needs an
attitude adjustment more than me. Time for that bottle of mead. :)

alternative.  Once that is firmly in your head, the attitude (aptitude?) 
of most of the employees there is working against you.  Get in, get out.

I've had to go to that store on numerous occasions and not a single time

drives are they have that little podium where one person sits like 
they're on a freaking throne and refuses to do anything for you to help 
outside of delegating you to the next available floor jocky.  They will 
sit there and do nothing right in front of you without making any eye 
contact that might sow a seed of guilt for being like they are.  I've

around and check inventory on the computer right next to

A couple of years ago, I showed moral failure and went to Fry's for
a laptop drive.  The lowest price was on outpost.com, the price was
decent at Enu, and the shelf price at Wilsonville Fry's was the
highest.  I pointed out the ad in Computer Bits to the felon\\\\\
sales associate at the Drive Altar, who proceeded to the runaround.
"Why don't we call them and verify that price?" sez he, and starts
a phone call to Enu.  At the time, it took Enu about 5 minutes to
answer the phone, and he gave up after 30 seconds.  "Too bad" sez
he, "Nobody there".  I did the John-like scowl and basket abandonment
maneuver, and burned some dinosaurs up to Hillsboro.  I didn't go
back to Fry's for a year.  While moral weakness has taken hold
again (I stop by when in Wilsonville for other reasons), it did
cost them business.

Note to John:  there is often a Portland phone book near the pay
phone by the bathrooms at Fry's.  Also, 411 on the cell phone.
And here's the info for PCH Cables (www.pchcables.com):

Monday thru Friday 10:00 AM - 6:00 PM  / (503) 629-3200 
2020 NW Aloclek Drive, Suite 107 (On Cornelius Pass Road), Hillsboro
It is closer to Portland than Wilsonville is, and Enu is nearby
for other computer gizzies.  

UPS ground takes one day from Hillsboro to Beaverton, and I have done
that a couple of times when I was willing to wait.

And finally, this is a chance to get misty eyed and remember our
dear departed fiend\\\\\friend Gary Groesshoeme of Oregon Electronics,
which was located at NE 9th and Davis and had all sorts of adapters
and cabling and jacks at pretty decent prices.  He did not have rock
bottom prices, but he was always good for half an hour of good advice
and great conversation - worth a little extra.  We miss you, Gary!

Keith

From ehem at m5p.com  Thu Aug 10 17:24:52 2006
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Thu, 10 Aug 2006 17:24:52 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Cat-6 keystone jacks in Portland
In-Reply-To: <20060809190007.A47924169@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200608110024.k7B0Oqg9041264@m5p.com>

>From: m0gely <m0gely at telestream.com>
> Elliott Mitchell wrote:
> > Nothing to my knowledge requires Cat6. In fact I'm not entirely sure
> > there is an official specification for Cat6. Failing that cabling labeled
> > "Category 6" was out well before any specification was.
> 
> It's absolutely required.  Who in their right mind would wire a building 
> with old tech?  It's not easy to replace this stuff when the dry rock is 
> up and the paint is on.  I get into this with low volt guys all the 
> time.  People should use what's "current" in cases like this. Gb over 
> copper won't last forever.  It's going to find it's limit in some new 
> application that will require higher speeds that Cat5e won't do.

On checking of sources, looks like Cat3 will do 10mbps just fine. Also
looks like the Cat5 limit is 1000mbps, though you need Cat5e for 10gbps.

You're assuming that the future is low-voltage cable. There is a pretty
good chance it will be fiber. The price differential is small enough that
Cat6 is likely a decent investment. Note that I didn't say not to use
Cat6, I merely stated nothing uses it yet. The investment only works out
only if we continue with copper, a pretty fair risk, yet the price is
still small.

From ianburrell at gmail.com  Fri Aug 11 09:30:41 2006
From: ianburrell at gmail.com (Ian Burrell)
Date: Fri, 11 Aug 2006 09:30:41 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] pkg-config reports wrong library version
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0608101320120.31049@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0608101320120.31049@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <d91f09cd0608110930g7ec70717oe955938809b5e152@mail.gmail.com>

On 8/10/06, Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> wrote:
>    Does anyone here know how to change the library version reported by
> pkg-config when it's queried during the configure phase?
>
>    My problem is that 'pkg-config --modversion glib-2.0' returns 2.8.6, when
> 2.12.1 is actually installed. The autotools get confused when they're told
> one version is installed but they find a different version.
>
>    Neither the man page nor the mail list archives on the freedesk web site
> have anything helpful.
>

You might want to figure out which .pc file pkg-config is finding and
look at it.  One possibility is that you have two versions installed
and it isn't finding the newer one's .pc file. This is quite possible
if you are using a different prefix. The PKG_CONFIG_SEARCH_PATH
controls the search path.  Another is that the new version wasn't
installed properly and didn't install its .pc file.

The --debug option spits out lots of debugging info.

 - Ian


From m0gely at telestream.com  Fri Aug 11 10:53:53 2006
From: m0gely at telestream.com (m0gely)
Date: Fri, 11 Aug 2006 10:53:53 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Cat-6 keystone jacks in Portland
In-Reply-To: <200608110024.k7B0Oqg9041264@m5p.com>
References: <200608110024.k7B0Oqg9041264@m5p.com>
Message-ID: <44DCC431.6050608@telestream.com>

Elliott Mitchell wrote:

> On checking of sources, looks like Cat3 will do 10mbps just fine. Also
> looks like the Cat5 limit is 1000mbps, though you need Cat5e for 10gbps.

Please send me a link for this info.  I was unaware of any documentation 
that said Cat5e could go that high.  I like stats. :)

> You're assuming that the future is low-voltage cable. There is a pretty
> good chance it will be fiber. The price differential is small enough that
> Cat6 is likely a decent investment. Note that I didn't say not to use
> Cat6, I merely stated nothing uses it yet. The investment only works out
> only if we continue with copper, a pretty fair risk, yet the price is
> still small.

I don't think Fiber is the (short distance) future for a while yet.  The 
cost is far more than going from 5e to 6.  I would have used Cat7 in my 
last wiring project had it been easy to get the parts.  Cat7 is what is 
officially takes copper to 10Gbs.  Cat5e and 6 are both rated for 1Gbs, 
though Cat6 has better qualities in S/N ration and NeXT.

When Fiber gets cheaper, and consumer products using it are 
(foreseeably) readily available, then it will be good for consideration.

-- 
- m0gely


From m0gely at telestream.com  Fri Aug 11 11:02:16 2006
From: m0gely at telestream.com (m0gely)
Date: Fri, 11 Aug 2006 11:02:16 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Recommended Reading for Home Networking Cable Install?
In-Reply-To: <20060810204307.GA26505@nellump.ath.cx>
References: <20060810204307.GA26505@nellump.ath.cx>
Message-ID: <44DCC628.7000601@telestream.com>

Paul Mullen wrote:
> All this talk of wiring up a private residence with Ethernet cable,
> etc. makes me realize how little I know about the procedure. Any
> pointers to good reading material regarding wiring up a home for
> low-voltage/networking?

Don't let us scare you.  It's not that difficult.  I recommend Cat6, but 
even if you use Cat5e it's still all hooked up the same.  Find a good 
central location for a switch, and as much as possible from there run a 
drop out to each location you have a computer/peripheral.  Think home 
run wiring.  Sometimes a second switch may be more convenient than 
making to runs to a location.

If this is new construction, and you're into gadgets and all this tech 
stuff, I would run Cat6 to every location in the house that may have a 
major appliance, phone, video/tv, and security camera.  Blanket the 
place in copper. :)

-- 
- m0gely

On Fri, 11 Aug 2006 10:53:53 -0700
m0gely <m0gely at telestream.com> dijo:

> I don't think Fiber is the (short distance) future for a while yet.  The 
> cost is far more than going from 5e to 6.  I would have used Cat7 in my 
> last wiring project had it been easy to get the parts.  Cat7 is what is 
> officially takes copper to 10Gbs.  Cat5e and 6 are both rated for 1Gbs, 
> though Cat6 has better qualities in S/N ration and NeXT.

I considered fiber, but then I decided that Cat6 would hold me for at
least five years, probably ten. That is, barring the invention of some
incredible new technology that requires greater bandwidth.

So what will become of my Cat6 inside the walls in ten years? Well, I
am thinking that by then wireless will have advanced greater than Cat6
can currently provide. And hopefully the security issues with wireless
will be adequately addressed as well. This is all gross speculation,
but I think when the time comes that my Cat6 will no longer provide
what I need, I'll move to whatever wireless technology is available
then. And if that is inadequate, then it's time for surface wiring. Or
I'll just tear the sheetrock off the walls, do it over, and put up new
sheetrock. In fact, all the holes I made in the studs are very large,
so I could probably just use the Cat6 to pull new fiber in. Or
whatever. Or I'll be dead. In fact, just finishing this wiring project
may kill me.


From m0gely at telestream.com  Fri Aug 11 12:19:39 2006
From: m0gely at telestream.com (m0gely)
Date: Fri, 11 Aug 2006 12:19:39 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Cat-6 keystone jacks in Portland
In-Reply-To: <20060811111547.dc62c286.johnxj@comcast.net>

John Jason Jordan wrote:

> In fact, all the holes I made in the studs are very large,
> so I could probably just use the Cat6 to pull new fiber in.

I don't fasten wire to anything if I don't have to.  It help for just 
that purpose.  Another thing you can do is when you run wire, leave 
sections of the pull string in the walls.  That way you can use the 
string to pull more wire (and more pull string for the next time) though 
without using the old wire to do it.  In case you don't want to remove 
the old wire when running new that it.

> whatever. Or I'll be dead. In fact, just finishing this wiring project
> may kill me.

i'm going to be rewiring a old but now minus all its sheetrock house
later this year. for LV i'm thinking about running flexible ENT conduit
to many of the destinations. The ENT may run into wireways that
then end into a small room dedicated to utilities.

checkout http://www.carlon.com/brochures/2f58.pdf

on those holes, be careful not too big

The diameter of drilled holes in studs should not exceed 40 percent of the
stud depth, and the minimum edge distance to the boundary of the hole shall
not be less than 5/8-inch. When studs are doubled, drilled holes should not
exceed 60 percent of the stud depth. Holes of maximum diameter should not
occur in more than two consecutive studs. Adjacent holes, or holes and
notches
should not occur in the same cross section. As an example, a 2x4 can have
a maximum hole of 1 3/8-inch or 2 inches if studs are doubled.

&&&&&&&&&&&&&&& Public Information &&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&&& Follows..


   - *802.11* -- applies to wireless
LANs<http://www.webopedia.com/TERM/8/local_area_network_LAN.html>and
provides 1 or 2 Mbps transmission in the
   2.4 GHz band using either frequency hopping spread
spectrum<http://www.webopedia.com/TERM/8/FHSS.html>(FHSS) or direct
   sequence spread spectrum <http://www.webopedia.com/TERM/8/DSSS.html>(DSSS).
   - *802.11a* -- an extension to 802.11 that applies to wireless
LANs<http://www.webopedia.com/TERM/8/802_11.html#>and provides up to
54 Mbps in the 5GHz band.
   802.11a uses an orthogonal frequency division
multiplexing<http://www.webopedia.com/TERM/8/OFDM.html>encoding scheme
rather than FHSS or DSSS.
   - *802.11b* (also referred to as *802.11 High Rate* or
*Wi-Fi*<http://www.webopedia.com/TERM/8/Wi_Fi.html>)
   -- an extension to 802.11 that applies to wireless LANS and provides
   11 Mbps transmission (with a fallback to 5.5, 2 and 1 Mbps) in the
2.4GHz band.
   802.11b uses only DSSS. 802.11b was a 1999 ratification to the
   original 802.11 standard, allowing wireless functionality comparable
   to Ethernet <http://www.webopedia.com/TERM/8/802_11.html#>.
   - *802.11g* -- applies to wireless LANs and provides 20+ Mbps in the
   2.4 GHz band.



@@@@@@@@@@@@@@@@@@@@@@@@@@@@@


These look like pretty good speeds to me....


You forgot to mention what type of connection you have to the house..
DIAL_UP?????????


Still my Thought

On Fri, Aug 11, 2006 at 12:51:27PM -0700, User Iam wrote:
> 
> It is only "slow", "insecure", and "unreliable" if you make it
> so.....

It's all very relative. Wireless (and by that I mean 802.11a/b/g) is
inarguably slower than fast/gigabit Ethernet. It will never be as
secure (regardless of any software-based security measures are used),
and in many environments the reliability (connection quality) is
spotty.

Bear in mind that many of us use our home networks for far more than
simply accessing the Internet.

-- 
Paul

From rsteff at comcast.net  Fri Aug 11 15:16:33 2006
From: rsteff at comcast.net (Richard C. Steffens)
Date: Fri, 11 Aug 2006 15:16:33 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Cat-6 keystone jacks in Portland
In-Reply-To: <200608111105.04978.carla@bratgrrl.com>
References: <200608110024.k7B0Oqg9041264@m5p.com>	<44DCC431.6050608@telestream.com>
	<200608111105.04978.carla@bratgrrl.com>
Message-ID: <44DD01C1.1020101@comcast.net>

Carla Schroder wrote:

> An additional problem with fiber is it's extremely difficult to splice. But 
> that's doubtless as solvable as bringing the cost down.

Back at the turn of the century (I can say that now, can't I?), the 
toughest part about fiber was the terminations. At EC we had just a few 
of the techs who could do it, and only one who specialized in it. The 
trick was to polish the glass the right way. They use a puck that looks 
something like a checker game piece with a short rigid tube to hold the 
fiber perpendicular to the very fine emery cloth that was sitting on a 
very flat piece of thick glass. They did something of a figure-8 pattern 
as they polished. It seems like a straight forward task, but the 
"perpendicular" to "very flat" parts were critical for it to work well. 
I'm assuming that the objective is to get the two pieces of fiber to be 
parallel to each other so that refraction is minimized.

They also kept a package of band-aids (TM) on hand. Fiber is glass, and 
apparently, it was not uncommon for them to poke themselves with a sharp 
edge of a broken fiber.

-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens

> a pair of wire cutters to sever all network cables, followed by 
> embedding the system in a combination of concrete, thermite and magnesium.

We weren't talking about absolute security (the fairy-tale), or security 
against someone that has physical access to the computer and/or network 
(a very very difficult problem as implied above).

We were talking about wireless vs wired, i.e., we'd already decided to 
network.  All other things being equal, I say:

[OS/net weakness]   <   ( [OS/net weakness] + [wireless weakness] )

Is it worth it to use wireless when there isn't any convenient 
alternative?  That's a completely different question.

> -----Original Message-----
> From: m0gely [mailto:m0gely at telestream.com]

> Install?
> 
> 
> Paul Mullen wrote:
> > All this talk of wiring up a private residence with Ethernet cable,
> > etc. makes me realize how little I know about the procedure. Any
> > pointers to good reading material regarding wiring up a home for
> > low-voltage/networking?
> 
> Don't let us scare you.  It's not that difficult.  I 
> recommend Cat6, but 
> even if you use Cat5e it's still all hooked up the same.  Find a good 
> central location for a switch, and as much as possible from 
> there run a 
> drop out to each location you have a computer/peripheral.  Think home 
> run wiring.  Sometimes a second switch may be more convenient than 
> making to runs to a location.
> 
> If this is new construction, and you're into gadgets and all 
> this tech 
> stuff, I would run Cat6 to every location in the house that 
> may have a 
> major appliance, phone, video/tv, and security camera.  Blanket the 
> place in copper. :)
> 
> -- 
> - m0gely

From raanders at acm.org  Tue Aug 15 12:16:40 2006
From: raanders at acm.org (Roderick A. Anderson)
Date: Tue, 15 Aug 2006 12:16:40 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Apache and mod_perl configuration
Message-ID: <44E21D98.6080906@acm.org>

My adventure has me at "wit's end" ( whit's end?  Been too many years. )

I'm working on a application that use Catalyst and it is set up to use 
mod_perl.

The problem I've run into is I can't get past mod_perl when accessing 
static files ... *.js *.css with out placing the whole path in the XHTML.

For those not familiar with Catalyst the standard 
installation/configuration places javascript, css, and ( static ) html 
files directories in [/var/www/]root/static/.

The only way I've been able to get to them is to use the full path from 
DocumentRoot.

DocumentRoot is set to /var/www/root/

But even with Aliases I have to use /static/css/site.css or 
/static/js/site.js

I've tried reordering the declaration in httpd.conf, going from 
<Directory> to <Location>, with and without trailing slashes; finally 
arriving at wit's end.  ( The dragon looks pretty simple right now. )

All the documentation I've found for mod_perl helps with over-riding the 
Apache default handlers but nothing on circumventing mod_perl -- which 
is suppose to be the "default" behavior.  Go figure, eh?

Any suggestions, examples, clue-sticks?


Thanks,
Rod
--

From creswick at gmail.com  Tue Aug 15 14:48:25 2006
From: creswick at gmail.com (Rogan Creswick)
Date: Tue, 15 Aug 2006 14:48:25 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] For The SysAdmin Gurus
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0608151409540.16187@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0608151409540.16187@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <c834ec80608151448v14fa58adw1560c667a8431e4f@mail.gmail.com>

On 8/15/06, Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> wrote:
>    My question is: how can I systematically check to learn what broke firefox
> printing on the one machine? Other than some applications I run on the big
> box but not on the notebook, they are synchronized in kernel and GNU
> utilities.

Have you tried printing the same pages as a different (ideally,
brand-new) user on the problematic box?  If you run Gnome or Kde I
would suggest trying with the other, or some other WM. running.  I've
found that gnome often puts it's fingers in places they most certainly
don't belong (eg: hibernation stopped working on my thinkpad when I
went back to fvwm.) It's possible that there is some weird layer
between firefox and the printer driver that is broken due to some
other strange problem.

It could (possibly) be a firefox plugin that has gone haywire, perhaps
not updating correctly because of some minute version difference on
one system that wasn't present on the other.

When faced with weird problems like this, one of the first things to
do is to determine if it is a problem with the system, or a problem
with your settings, and the easiest way to do that is by creating a
new user for testing purposes.  Hopefully the new user doesn't have
any problems, in which case you can move your dot-files over to the
new dir. one at a time until the problem arises again, at which point
you can figure out how to fix it.

--Rogan

>    I am not sufficiently clever to know where to start looking for the source

On Tue, 2006-08-15 at 15:08 -0700, Rich Shepard wrote:
> On Tue, 15 Aug 2006, Wil Cooley wrote:
> 
> > Now you understand the problems with using a distro that doesn't have
> > package management.
> 
>    Guess that's why I'm not a SysAdmin, Wil. 

How many years have you been denying that, Rich?  You've got to admit
that to yourself; you've been doing it for a long time now.  Admitting
it is the first step to alt.sysadmin.recovery :)

The difference is that you don't do it often enough to have gotten sick
of doing the manual stuff over and over again and realized that
sometimes you should trade off control for certain worries.  That
encapsulates my opinion of Slackware pretty well--but it's not just
Slackware, I used to worry and fiddle with an installation to keep the
size down as small as possible, to tweak and tune and fiddle bits here
and there.  Nowadays, I could mostly care less.  I'd rather work within
the constraints of the system and focus my time where I get either the
least work down with the most accomplishment, or the most fun.  I mean,
if doing stuff manually is your thing, and you've got the time, then by
all means, love yourself all over it.  But it doesn't sound like you do.

> When I go to the firefox web
> site it identifies my need as linux in English. I've never seen a package up
> there, not Fedora, Red Hat, Debian, Slackware, Gentoo, Ubuntu, or any other.
> It's a gzipped tar that needs no configuration, making, or installing other
> than untarring in the directory of our choice.

I've only ever downloaded Firefox from mozilla.org for Windows
installations; for Linux installations, I used the included binary (or
one from an established 3rd party), built for my particular system.  The
problem that people complain about with Linux systems is the diversity
of environments; with a huge application like Firefox that depends so
deeply on many parts of the system, it's particularly bad.

>    How would a package management distribution prevent this problem?

 o If you think you've missed uninstalling something, package management
would have let you know that you'd completely uninstalled it.

 o If you installed things with library version dependencies that got
mangled (which it sounds like it has), package management would have
made it harder for you to shoot yourself in that particular foot.

 o Most of the distros with package management would also have provided
you a binary, the right libraries, etc.

Wil
-- 
Wil Cooley <wcooley at nakedape.cc>
Naked Ape Consulting, Ltd

Wil,

   I don't use Slackware for control, but for stability 'way back from the

   I've not been a twiddler and tweaker, but a learner. When I learned enough

back to use the system as a means to an end, not an end in itself. Other
than security upgrades to the system, and certain security or bug fixes to
the applications I use daily, I don't futz with the system until I get a new
distribution set in the mail. It takes me perhaps an hour to upgrade all
three hosts here, and I'm done with system administration other than
checking the log summaries mailed to me each morning and changing the backup
tapes on Friday and Monday.

upgrades much less of a hassle than I had with Red Hat.

From daniel at digitree.org  Tue Aug 15 16:40:33 2006
From: daniel at digitree.org (Daniel Hedlund)
Date: Tue, 15 Aug 2006 16:40:33 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] For The SysAdmin Gurus
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0608151409540.16187@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0608151409540.16187@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <44E25B71.5090702@digitree.org>

Rich,

You could have at least one backend library that Firefox uses that could 
be different versions, or at least compiled against different versions. 
  It could be anything in a printing library or even a rendering one. 
Use the 'ldd' command on the firefox binary (firefox-bin) to get a list 
of libraries it depends on and then check those library version numbers 
between the systems.  Please note that most of the versions listed by 
ldd are symlinks so you should not use those numbers, just get the names 
of the libraries.  Also note that some libraries depend on other 
libraries and so on.  Some libraries linked against my version of 
firefox that seem likely causes include, but not limited to (from most 
likely to least):
- libcairo
- libpangocairo
- libpango
- libgdk_pixbuf
- libgdk-x11
- libpangoft2
- libgtk-x11

Cheers,

Daniel Hedlund
daniel at digitree.org

From daniel at digitree.org  Tue Aug 15 17:38:39 2006
From: daniel at digitree.org (Daniel Hedlund)
Date: Tue, 15 Aug 2006 17:38:39 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] For The SysAdmin Gurus
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0608151717370.23615@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0608151409540.16187@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>	<44E25B71.5090702@digitree.org>
	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0608151717370.23615@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <44E2690F.5010307@digitree.org>

Rich,

>   All right, already! I got it that libplds4.so isn't on the system. 
> Neither
> is libxpcom.so. So, my first task is to find in which package those are
> located and install them.

It appears that most of the libraries you are missing (libplds4.so) are 
part of the nspr (Netscape Portable Runtime) library:
"NSPR provides platform independence for non-GUI operating system
facilities. These facilities include threads, thread synchronization,
normal file and network I/O, interval timing and calendar time, basic
memory management (malloc and free) and shared library linking."
http://www.mozilla.org/projects/nspr/

The libxpcom.so library should be in Firefox's directory 
(/usr/lib/firefox-1.5.0.x/libxpcom.so) for me.  Because it's in the same 
directory as the binary, it is detected by firefox-bin when started.

Daniel Hedlund
daniel at digitree.org

From daniel at digitree.org  Tue Aug 15 18:10:11 2006
From: daniel at digitree.org (Daniel Hedlund)
Date: Tue, 15 Aug 2006 18:10:11 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] For The SysAdmin Gurus
In-Reply-To: <1155689361.13141.3.camel@spartacus.nakedape.priv>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0608151409540.16187@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>	<44E25B71.5090702@digitree.org>	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0608151717370.23615@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0608151738120.23615@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
	<1155689361.13141.3.camel@spartacus.nakedape.priv>
Message-ID: <44E27073.3030002@digitree.org>

Wil Cooley wrote:
> /usr/bin/firefox is usually a shell script that sets up LD_LIBRARY_PATH
> to include /usr/lib/firefox; if that didn't work, you've know a lot
> sooner because it wouldn't start at all.

I agree with this, especially about the binary not loading if there are 
any missing libraries.  I'm not personally concerned with Rich having 
any missing libraries, but rather the versions of his libraries being 
used might not be in sync; his printing problems could be because of a 
simple/trivial bug in a library somewhere.  One thing Rich could/should 
also take into account is modifications made by the "firefox" shell 
script that adds to the LD_LIBRARY_PATH.  This can modify the libraries 
that Firefox uses as compared to the results that 'ldd' returns.  The 
script could be overriding system libraries or the other way around.

Playing around with the firefox script, I have noticed that firefox 
seems to be executed not necessarily just by "firefox-bin", but also by 
"mozilla-xremote-client".

Rich, try running the following (assuming you are using Bash):

MOZILLA_DIST_BIN="<path_to_firefox_lib_dir>"

if [ "$LD_LIBRARY_PATH" ]
then
   LD_LIBRARY_PATH=$MOZ_DIST_BIN:$MOZ_DIST_BIN/plugins:$LD_LIBRARY_PATH
else
   LD_LIBRARY_PATH=$MOZ_DIST_BIN:$MOZ_DIST_BIN/plugins
fi

This should act like the shell script (since I took it from there) and 
resolve all the libraries the same way as the firefox binary.  From a 
realistic point of view, I don't think this will lead to a result that 
will fix your problem since you have identical versions of the firefox 
binary and libraries.  The libraries overridden by firefox will probably 
be the same.

Daniel Hedlund
daniel at digitree.org

Jeme A Brelin wrote:

I think it's important to just stick with what you're comfortable with 
and what works.  The question itself is very vague and I hope it doesn't 
turn into a discussion about which email server is the best.  I don't 
see the Sendmail community completely dieing out anytime soon, so as 
long as you're comfortable [with security and other related issues], 
just pick the tool that is right for the job and has the features you're 
looking for.  If you're lacking a feature or feel uncomfortable with a 
particular product, please let us know so we can make recommendations 
based on that.

Also, doesn't the Debian community officially prefer Exim[4] now?  I 
think that's the default email server under Debian.  Does anybody know 
any other distributions that also choose Exim as their default?

Daniel Hedlund wrote:
> Go to a website this is not printing properly using your laptop (which 
> does print), save the complete web page to disk ("Web Page, complete"). 
>  Take that page and the subdirectory it creates to your desktop computer 
> and see if it prints.  This way you can at least have a static page for 
> reference (?).

Also, with this static website loaded on both browsers, try printing to 
file (postscript) with the same printer driver.  You (or someone who 
knows a little about the postscript file format) might be able to use 
the differences between the printer output to determine how far into the 
rendering/printing stage things made it before things started going 
funny; there might be some signs of an attempt to start rendering the 
page but it stopped a few lines in for example, or possibly corrupt 
postscript data.  This is unlikely, but at least you might be able to 
find out if Firefox at least attempted to generate valid content within 
the postscript document.

If you do a "man firefox", it also appears that there are some GTK 
options you can pass to Firefox to enable some debugging output.  If you 
  can figure out the options to pass, you might be able to produce some 
debugging output that could reveal your problem or differences between 
the inner workings of the two Firefox installations.

Cheers,

Daniel Hedlund
daniel at digitree.org

On Tue, 2006-08-15 at 16:31 -0700, Rich Shepard wrote:

>    I don't use Slackware for control, but for stability 'way back from the
> bleeding edge.

Then why are you installing Firefox?  Netscape 4.78 was rock-solid.
(Okay, it wasn't.)

If Slackware isn't providing you with Firefox updates, then how stable
can it be?  Firefox is one of your core tools--and a common one at that.
It's like saying a particular car is rock-solid but you have to supply
your own transmission.

>    I've not been a twiddler and tweaker, but a learner. When I learned enough
> to know how to do the things I wanted, I simplified my network and settled
> back to use the system as a means to an end, not an end in itself. Other
> than security upgrades to the system, and certain security or bug fixes to
> the applications I use daily, I don't futz with the system until I get a new
> distribution set in the mail. It takes me perhaps an hour to upgrade all
> three hosts here, and I'm done with system administration other than
> checking the log summaries mailed to me each morning and changing the backup
> tapes on Friday and Monday.

That's now how it looks on this side of the mailing list.  Let's see, in
the past few weeks you've:
 o Had a /usr/lib/ with broken symlinks to NSPR libraries
 o Had a Firefox that wouldn't print certain pages
 o Had a broken pkg-config for glib (probably caused by an incomplete 
   attempt at manual installation w/gtk+)
 o Had jpilot crashing with an invalid pointer to free()
 o Attempted to rebuild and install gtk+, atk, pango, cairo, which you 
   then had to (manually) uninstall
 o Had jpilot not displaying the calendar correctly
 o Which seems to have been caused by a prior attempt to manually 
   build  and install gtk+
 o Had GPG break mysteriously by installing the package from some 
   other site on the net
 
That's a lot of problems for a stable system that you don't twiddle or
tweak.

You do realize that second-level-version upgrades to libraries like
glib, gtk+, atk, pango, etc. are the kind of things that only get
upgraded on major releases, even on a bleeding-edge distro like Fedora,
because everything that links to them should be relinked.

>    I don't know why you think I do things manually. The Slackware package
> tools (installpkg, upgradepkg, removepkg) make distribution and application
> upgrades much less of a hassle than I had with Red Hat.

Wrappers around 'tar'.  The reason you had hassles with Red Hat was
because it was enforcing dependency tracking, which exists to prevent
exactly the kind of problems you're having now.  You can't list the
files a particular package owns, find the dependencies of a package,
find what packages depend on a package, verify that all the files are
installed and are correct checksums, etc.

>    Well, the problem also occurs with packages created specifically for
> Slackware. The same package won't properly print on the one box, but will on
> another. What that has to do with the distribution I don't know.

Then they're badly packaged or badly built?  As I said before, a core
application like Firefox should be supported out of the box in a desktop
distro.

>    I know full well that you've a ton of experience. When three systems are
> running the same distribution and the same versions of everything, but one
> has a problem with one application that is not a problem on the other hosts,
> I fail to see how it's the result of my choice of a linux distribution.
> However, ...

There's no way to know what's different between these supposedly
identical systems because you don't have package management.  Well,
that's not /entirely/ true--you can do it the brute-force method--use
'find' to create a list of every file on the system,
pruning /home, /var, maybe others, and then diff them.

From daniel at digitree.org  Tue Aug 15 19:24:17 2006
From: daniel at digitree.org (Daniel Hedlund)
Date: Tue, 15 Aug 2006 19:24:17 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] flash plugin and sound
Message-ID: <44E281D1.7030200@digitree.org>

I have a simple script that I use to enable and disable the flash plugin 
for Firefox that I normally keep as "~/bin/flash".  Unfortunately the 
script isn't perfect and there are a few things I'd like to improve upon:
- if you have sound playing when the flash plugin is first used on a web 
page, you no longer have any sound support in flash until after you've 
completely restarted Firefox.
- when you disable the plugin, you have to restart Firefox for the 
changes to take effect.  Enabling flash while the web browser is open is 
okay and changes take effect immediately.

What I'd like to do is to find some way to unload or replace the flash 
plugin with an empty/shell plugin to cause it unload without restarting 
Firefox.  This is becoming a huge pain for me in that I am almost always 
listening to music using rhythmbox or mplayer while browsing the web. 
Fairly often I like to listen to streaming music (radio, GarageBand.com, 
CDBaby.com, etc) and would simply like to stop rhythmbox and hit 'play' 
within the flash plugin.  I have even reverted to such tactics as 
loading Firefox as another user using sudo so I don't have to restart my 
Firefox client with 20+ pages open.  I think the problem is mostly 
because flash doesn't use alsa and thus doesn't allow mixing?

Any ideas, solutions or success stories?  For anyone interested in 
enabling/disabling the flash plugin, my script is as follows:

#!/bin/bash

display_usage() {
     echo "USAGE: $0 [ --enable | --disable ]"
     echo
}

if [ ! $# -eq 1 ]; then
     display_usage && exit 1
fi

if [ $1 = "--enable" ]; then
     echo "enabling flash..."
     chmod 644 ~/.mozilla/plugins/flashplayer.xpt
     chmod 755 ~/.mozilla/plugins/libflashplayer.so

elif [ $1 = "--disable" ]; then
     echo "disabling flash..."
     chmod 000 ~/.mozilla/plugins/{flashplayer.xpt,libflashplayer.so}

elif [ $1 = "--help" ]; then
     display_usage && exit 0

else
     display_usage && exit 1
fi


Cheers,

Daniel Hedlund
daniel at digitree.org


From ehem at m5p.com  Tue Aug 15 19:45:40 2006
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)

>From: Jeme A Brelin <jeme at brelin.net>
> Does anyone else use milter or is it just time for me to stop using 
> sendmail?

Yes and no. Folks using Postfix or Qmail, aren't actually using Sendmail,
but they're almost certainly using the Milter interface. Everyone copied
the interface for Spam filtering.


>From: Daniel Hedlund <daniel at digitree.org>
> Also, doesn't the Debian community officially prefer Exim[4] now?  I 
> think that's the default email server under Debian.  Does anybody know 
> any other distributions that also choose Exim as their default?

Yes, and I sure as heck wish the Exim disaster wasn't the preference.
Using the package install scripts, all the other MTAs are similarly
simple to setup in simple installations. For complex installations Exim
isn't suitable, so why bother?

Further, during the Exim 3 days, they were making Sendmail look like Fort
Knox. I was glad to avoid needing security patches several times a month.
Further, Exim doesn't support support the username+tag syntax; the big 3
all support it, and this is very helpful for filtering.

From johnxj at comcast.net  Tue Aug 15 21:28:50 2006
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Tue, 15 Aug 2006 21:28:50 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Using an external drive enclosure -- What am I missing?
In-Reply-To: <004701c6c0d1$99ecab00$6d01a8c0@RAMA>
References: <004701c6c0d1$99ecab00$6d01a8c0@RAMA>
Message-ID: <20060815212850.e83c1b91.johnxj@comcast.net>

On Tue, 15 Aug 2006 18:16:27 -0700
"William A Morita" <wamorita at hevanet.com> dijo:

> What am I missing here?
> I have obtained an external drive enclosure.
> When I have a CD drive in the enclosure, the drive gets recognized by the OS
> (Linux or XP).
> When I put an IDE hard drive in, nothing.

Just a super wild-ass guess from someone who knows little. In the world
of IDE, hard drives expect to be the master, and are shipped from the
factory with that setting. CD drives, on the other hand, expect to be
the slave. And that's about all I ever cared to know about master-slave
relatiionships.


From m0gely at telestream.com  Tue Aug 15 22:04:19 2006
From: m0gely at telestream.com (m0gely)
Date: Tue, 15 Aug 2006 22:04:19 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Using an external drive enclosure -- What am I missing?
In-Reply-To: <20060815212850.e83c1b91.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <004701c6c0d1$99ecab00$6d01a8c0@RAMA>
	<20060815212850.e83c1b91.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <44E2A753.7030006@telestream.com>

John Jason Jordan wrote:

> Just a super wild-ass guess from someone who knows little. In the world
> of IDE, hard drives expect to be the master, and are shipped from the
> factory with that setting. CD drives, on the other hand, expect to be
> the slave. And that's about all I ever cared to know about master-slave
> relatiionships.

The master/slave setting is just another way of saying device 0 or 1. 
You cannot have two slaves or two masters on a single ribbon.  Sometimes 
there are performance reasons for having devices set as a master or a 
slave, but it won't prevent anything from working.  This has nothing to 
do with USB enclosures though.

I would imagine the drive needs to be set as a master in this case 
though, but it's not something I've ever experimented with.  The 
enclosures I have had were always master and I just left them that way.

-- 
- m0gely


From daniel at digitree.org  Wed Aug 16 00:33:13 2006
From: daniel at digitree.org (Daniel Hedlund)
Date: Wed, 16 Aug 2006 00:33:13 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] flash plugin and sound
In-Reply-To: <44E281D1.7030200@digitree.org>
References: <44E281D1.7030200@digitree.org>
Message-ID: <44E2CA39.1090807@digitree.org>

Daniel Hedlund wrote:
> - if you have sound playing when the flash plugin is first used on a web 
> page, you no longer have any sound support in flash until after you've 
> completely restarted Firefox.

I've fixed my problem relating to sound in flash when sound is being 
used by another application.  The solution was to install the 'alsa-oss' 
library and then use the 'aoss' wrapper program around Firefox, i.e. 
'aoss firefox'.  This solution seems to work well for me and even allows 
Firefox to play sounds at the same time as another application.  This 
solution may also fix some other sound problems related to flash (sound 
lagging while playing videos), but I haven't tested this thoroughly yet.

A walkthrough for Debian and Ubuntu can be found at:
http://planet-geek.com/archives/003048.html

For Fedora/Redhat-based users, you'll need to manually download an 
alsa-oss RPM from a 3rd party website and then run the 'aoss ...' 
command above; it doesn't appear to be in any common 2nd/3rd party 
repositories used with Fedora though.  CCRMA seems to be the preferred 
download site by posters on other mailing lists.

Fedora Core 5:
http://ccrma.stanford.edu/planetccrma/mirror/fedora/linux/planetcore/5/i386/alsa-oss-1.0.11-1.rhfc5.ccrma.i386.rpm

Fedora Core 4:
http://ccrma.stanford.edu/planetccrma/mirror/fedora/linux/planetcore/4/i386/alsa-oss-1.0.9-1.rhfc4.ccrma.i386.rpm

Fedora Core 3:
http://ccrma.stanford.edu/planetccrma/mirror/fedora/linux/planetcore/3/i386/alsa-oss-1.0.9-0.1.rc1.rhfc3.ccrma.i386.rpm

...

I am not sure the steps required to get flash's sound working properly 
under other Linux distributions since I don't have them installed.

I still have not yet come up with a solution to dynamically unload flash 
from Firefox while it's still running, but I did talk with someone who 
gave a suggestion of modifying mime-types dynamically within Firefox to 
prevent new Flash applets from using the library.  I have not yet tested 
this idea and whether or not it can be triggered by a shell script.

Cheers,

Daniel Hedlund
daniel at digitree.org

From daniel at digitree.org  Wed Aug 16 05:50:30 2006
From: daniel at digitree.org (Daniel Hedlund)
Date: Wed, 16 Aug 2006 05:50:30 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] flash plugin and sound
In-Reply-To: <44E2CA39.1090807@digitree.org>
References: <44E281D1.7030200@digitree.org> <44E2CA39.1090807@digitree.org>
Message-ID: <44E31496.7030803@digitree.org>

Based on my experiences with the Flash plugin and various idiosyncrasies 
between different distros/platforms, I have created a project that I've 
dubbed 'flashlight'.  It's basically the script I provided a couple 
emails ago on steroids with several new features added.  I do realize 
that Flash 9 will be coming out sometime next year, but there's still 
some time to go before then.

'flashlight' is a tool which helps user to install and manage the 
Macromedia Flash plugin on Linux-based operating systems. flashlight can 
be used to download and install flash, correct alsa/oss sound-related 
issues, fix distribution-specific bugs, and enable or disable flash on a 
per-user basis.

The flashlight project page can be found at:
http://projects.digitree.org/flashlight/

If anyone is interested in helping me to develop this further, please 
contact me off list.

Cheers,

Daniel Hedlund
daniel at digitree.org
