From wamorita at hevanet.com  Wed Aug 16 07:46:48 2006
From: wamorita at hevanet.com (William A Morita)
Date: Wed, 16 Aug 2006 07:46:48 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] FW: [ISN] Defcon 2006: Hackers can work for the Feds - no
	degreerequired 
Message-ID: <00ab01c6c142$ce2ff6d0$6d01a8c0@RAMA>

 

-----Original Message-----
From: isn-bounces at infosecnews.org [mailto:isn-bounces at infosecnews.org] On
Behalf Of InfoSec News
Sent: Wednesday, August 16, 2006 1:02 AM
To: isn at infosecnews.org
Subject: [ISN] Defcon 2006: Hackers can work for the Feds - no
degreerequired 

http://www.tomsnetworking.com/2006/08/07/defcon2006_meetthefed_nodegree/

By Humphrey Cheung
August 7, 2006

Las Vegas (NV) - The great need for qualified computer security personnel is
now forcing the government to rethink rigid hiring guidelines. At the Defcon
computer security convention in Las Vegas, more than a dozen federal agents
told attendees that traditional requirements like college degrees and
polygraph tests were no longer strictly required for government employment.
They also said security clearances are being approved quickly.

In a rather surprising change of strategy, the government is streamlining
its process of attracting hacking talent. Jerry Dixon, deputy director for
the United States Computer Emergency Readiness Team, told journalists that
his organization has hired several people without degrees. Jim Christy,
director of the Defense Cyber Crime Institute told attendees that most of
the people who come to work for him usually come in as contractors and have
no background with the government. "We're gonna teach you how to do the
job... without college degrees," Christy said.

Apparently, "very gifted" have the chance of being hired even without a
high-school degree. "I just spent five and a half hours last night talking
with someone who has never graduated high school. And he is one of the most
innovative people I know," said Dr. Linton Wells II, assistant secretary of
defense for the Department of Defense.

Wells expalined that the government desperately needs qualified people as
40% of the senior personnel will be retiring during the next few years. With
this upcoming shortage, the government is willing to accept people gaining
skills away from schools. "The last standing perception of government
service is that you need a college degree,"  
said Wells. He said this perception does not match reality. According to
Wells, many employees, contractors and even people in the senior executive
service do not have degrees.

Christy told us that becoming a contractor first is the "easiest and
quickest" way to eventually getting a government job and said 60% of his
organization is composed of contractors. Government hiring procedures often
can be "slow and antiquated" and working with contractors sometimes is the
only option to complete a critical job, he explained.

"If we need immediate help, we will hire someone as a contractor and then
try to create a government position to move him into," said Christy. He
added it can take "two to three years" for that position to be created.

Another misconception, according to the Defcon speakers, is that security
clearances take months or even years to complete. Christy told us that new
hires are getting cleared surprisingly quickly. In many cases, hires can
receive an interim secret clearance in about 3 to 4 weeks. According to
Christy, the interim check consists of a "quick little" background inquiry
and a check for warrants and convictions. "Even some people in government
are surprised at how quickly people are being cleared," Christy said.

Even though the government is willing to drop the four-year degree
requirement, other federal agents on the panel warned of requirements that
cannot be eliminated. Mike Jacobs, vice president and director of SRA and a
former deputy associate director of the NSA, warned about security
clearances.

"You gotta sit and take the polygraphy, folks," said Jacobs. However this
strict polygraph requirement only exists at some agencies - like the NSA.
Both Dr. Wells and Christy told us that polygraphs are usually not required
for other government agencies.

Many people and probably most Defcon attendees believe polygraphs are
required for most government jobs, but Wells said, "This is absolutely not
true... polygraphs are not required for most secret level jobs."  
Christy told us, "Everyone doesn't have to be polygraphed. In certain
programs, up to 90% are not polygraphed."

The applicants that must face a polygraph often just take a basic polygraph
instead of the much tougher "lifestyle" polygraph test.  
"Basically, we ask if you are a spy in the basic test," said Christy.

Other factors that could disqualify an applicant are financial problems and
drug use. Financial responsibility is the "number one"  
disqualifier, but Christy adds that drug use is also a major disqualifier.
"If you used drugs in the last year, you would probably be precluded.

From larry.brigman at gmail.com  Wed Aug 16 08:55:44 2006
From: larry.brigman at gmail.com (Larry Brigman)
Date: Wed, 16 Aug 2006 08:55:44 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Using an external drive enclosure -- What am I missing?
In-Reply-To: <004701c6c0d1$99ecab00$6d01a8c0@RAMA>
References: <004701c6c0d1$99ecab00$6d01a8c0@RAMA>
Message-ID: <ca5165530608160855u6085e2dfp43b3c5d3ad8341df@mail.gmail.com>

On 8/15/06, William A Morita <wamorita at hevanet.com> wrote:
> What am I missing here?
> I have obtained an external drive enclosure.
> When I have a CD drive in the enclosure, the drive gets recognized by the OS
> (Linux or XP).
> When I put an IDE hard drive in, nothing.
>

It is possible that you have missed nothing.

A couple of things to be aware of.
Hard drives consume more current than CD drives.  If the adapter is
being powered by
the USB port then you have a problem unless you are using a laptop
drive and even
then it still could be a problem.  Power could still be the problem if
the external PS
is under rated for HD's.

Check the web site of the manufacture to determine if they have HD
support in the
USB adapter set.

If you are using a slide in enclosure bay, change the bay.  It could
be the cable or
connector in that bay.

From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Tue Aug 15 11:14:59 2006
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (plug_0 at robinson-west.com)
Date: Tue, 15 Aug 2006 11:14:59 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Recommended Reading for Home Networking Cable Install?
In-Reply-To: <20060811194341.GA998@nellump.ath.cx>
References: <20060810204307.GA26505@nellump.ath.cx>
	<44DCC628.7000601@telestream.com>
	<727fc2ee0608111239u7f1d98edj50b0c57bca99a44@mail.gmail.com>
	<20060811194341.GA998@nellump.ath.cx>
Message-ID: <1155665699.44e20f237896d@xerxes.robinson-west.com>

Quoting Paul Mullen <pm at nellump.net>:

> On Fri, Aug 11, 2006 at 12:39:54PM -0700, User Iam wrote:
> > 
> > Does WIRELESS  mean anything to anybody???
> 
> Yes, it means "slow", "insecure", and "unreliable".
> 
> > Why drag wire if you DON'T need to???
> 
> Because it's the only way to make full use of anything faster than
> 10Mb Ethernet.

I'm not aware of any way to do network root and network booting over a
wireless lan.  It's important to note that network root over wireless 
would magnify all the security problems.  Lose signal, lose host.
I don't think anyone has played with networking enough till they
have experimented with remote root and network booting.  For large
distributed computing environments, the cost savings can be
incredible.  While wired networks are supposedly more secure than
wireless ones, it's important to note that wire tapping can 
become a big time issue with copper cables.  You csn't wire tap
a radio signal.  A software secure wireless network could indeed
be safer than a wired one that is vulnerable to tapping, but not
as fast.  Has 54mbps wireless been exceeded yet?

Regards,

Michael C. Robinon

From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Tue Aug 15 23:08:43 2006
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (plug_0 at robinson-west.com)
Date: Tue, 15 Aug 2006 23:08:43 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] ReactOS new release out...
Message-ID: <1155708523.44e2b66bf30c6@xerxes.robinson-west.com>

It is still fairly dysfunctional, but at lease there is some progress.  
With the release of ReactOS 0.3.0 RC2, I'm wondering when a 1.0 version 
will finally come out.  

I'm concerned about the developer forum comment, 
"we implemented ReactOS in ring 0 to be driver compatible with drivers
released for Windows NT."  Hmm, I have yet to even see a working device
manager implementation let alone a working clone of the Windows add new
hardware wizard.  Touting ReactOS as a future free replacement for 
yesterday's version of Windows may be a pipe dream.  Apparently, NTFS 
won't be supported for a long time because some of it's inner details 
are secret.  Who is to say that the Win32 API alone defines Windows, 
any version, completely?  ReactOS can't currently replace Windows 95 
and Windows NT very well let alone any newer version of Windows.  
Implementing a Windows like system from the ground up under the GPL 
probably isn't worth it if the result isn't sufficiently stable and 
compatible with existing Windows software.  Maybe the ReactOS 
community should focus on a good implementation of the portions of 
the Win32 API that are worthwhile and convincing vendors to write 
software specifically for ReactOS.  Just because Microsoft doesn't
pressure software vendors to write software that doesn't have to be
run as root doesn't mean that the ReactOS community can't attempt to.  

One major problem with Windows XP when you have multiple users is that 
shortcuts for new programs don't tend to propagate across all accounts 
and there seems to be no simple way to define which programs are visible 
to users on an individualized basis.  To be honest, I'm not certain how
to do this in gnome on top of Linux either.  For example, I want evolution
installed to gnome, but I don't necessarily want user XYZ to be allowed to
use it.  I want to be able as the administrator to define any lower users
desktop and enforce that definition to varying degrees.  I may, for example,
want to limit sudo on a Linux system and runas on a Windows machine.

Why doesn't Microsoft offer to help the ReactOS community get their system
out of Beta and use the system as the basis for the next commercial version 
of Windows?

This mail sent through IMP: http://horde.org/imp/

The question I have is:  is there anything in the response that indicates the
secondary should not initiate the transfer?  My eyeballing the packet decode
doesn't find anything.   It looks to me as if the event sequence is:

  Secondary - send IXFR pkt
  Primary   - respond with SOA
  Secondary - decides to initiate TCP for IXFR or not

Is this accurate?

Message-ID: <c834ec80608161455v45ca1badw3a08f064e337745@mail.gmail.com>

On 8/15/06, plug_0 at robinson-west.com <plug_0 at robinson-west.com> wrote:
> To be honest, I'm not certain how
> to do this in gnome on top of Linux either.  For example, I want evolution
> installed to gnome, but I don't necessarily want user XYZ to be allowed to
> use it.  I want to be able as the administrator to define any lower users
> desktop and enforce that definition to varying degrees.  I may, for example,
> want to limit sudo on a Linux system and runas on a Windows machine.

You could do it with groups and selective application of the x bit,
but I'd guess that any upgrade attempt would blow away your changes.

I would however, expect that it's easier to prevent user's from using
given applications to do the things you don't want them to do.  I'd
expect it's not the running of apps that you want to prevent, but the
side effects of those apps -- reading/writing from/to parts of the
disk, manipulating hardware of other types (playing audio, accessing
tape drives, etc..), or accessing the network, or parts of it.  The
means to prevent those things are well documented, and you'll have to
take those measures anyway (unless, of course, there really is an app
you just don't want anyone to run for reasons intrinsic to that
application.  I can't imagine any of the standard tools will fall into
that category though, in which case just don't give that user's group
execute permissions.)

--Rogan

From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Thu Aug 17 23:53:07 2006
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (plug_0 at robinson-west.com)

"Let's get together with the hardware providers and Microsoft to make sure
that games only work well in Windows."

Is this why you often have to buy a new computer to play the latest games
even if you bought one last year, or two years ago?  With XBOX, it seems
that Microsoft is attempting to abandon the PC as a gaming platform.  
What would stop another group of people from saying, "We'll
develop cutting edge games which do not depend on secrets that people have
to pay a ton of cash for," from being successful as well?  Games certainly 
aren't the end all be all of life, I'm most interested in the potential 
angle.  

Another problem area, CAD/CAM programs.  AutoCad or MasterCam anyone?  Anyone
seen shops where Windows 2000 or 2003 is needed to run the CAD/CAM software?  

PCC and PSU attempt to lock computer science students into Microsoft Word,
latest version, and Waterloo Maple, latest version.  Weren't the Maple 6 
and Maple 9 releases fairly close together?  I remember at PCC that the
computers tended to have multiple versions of Maple and insufficient 
memory, as well a broken MS-Windows interface.  Computers in the classroom 
that are slow or broken seem rather pointless, yet I'm guessing that PCC 
lacks the will even for the math department to abandon Microsoft.  PSU 
appears to be no better about this.  PCC is trying to shove Maple into the
CS curriculum because of PSU.  Yes there is Maple for Linux, but that
costs just as much and you don't get free upgrades as you upgrade your
Linux system.  You are buying a binary, so it's easy to upgrade the
library it depends on.

Will American colleges and Universities ever become comparable to
institutions in foreign countries that have strong open source software/open
standards policies?  Why should students depend on proprietary hardware 
and software when they are supposed to be learning?  I think there is a strong
lobby for Microsoft to remain a monopoly even if college students and foreign
governments get fleeced by the company.  Is MIT the only American institution of
higher education that has an open lab?

I cannot speak for PCC, but PSU most assuredly does not "attempt to lock
computer science students into Microsoft Word".  The vast majority of CS
(and math) classes assume and teach to a UNIX environment, often
Solaris, but frequently Linux.  I have never had a Professor ask for an
assignment in .doc format.  I *have* had professors specifically exclude
.doc from the list of acceptable formats.  As for the Maple requirement
for some classes, Maple is provided on the PSU lab computers, to which
you can SSH.  In fact the last project for which I needed to use Maple
was done entirely from home using Maple running at school, displaying at
home using the wonders of SSH X tunneling (not to mention the beauty of
a network transparent window system).

In fact, PSU is a very strong supporter of Open Source.  It hosts
freedesktop.org, for example.  They employ a number of professors who
are strong proponents and very active in different areas of OSS.
Microsoft's presence within the PSU CS department is minimal (although
growing slightly, no doubt as a result of some large donations) and it's
influence and prevalence within classes almost nil.

# from Paul Heinlein
# on Friday 18 August 2006 09:37 am:

>> bzzzzzzzzt! ?Wrong answer. ?Trade schools are designed to prepare
>> people for jobs. ?Colleges are for teaching, not training.
>
>No one is well served a lack of subtlety in this debate.

Twas a friendly buzzer.  Couldn't you hear it? :-)

>There's a gray area between "Trade School" and "Ivory Tower." While
>there's certainly room for healthy disagreement as to where in that
>gray area colleges and universities should aim their curriculum,
>there's no room at all (in my mind) for denying the existence of that
>tension.

I'm not saying colleges have to be "Ivory Tower", but should they be 
teaching architects how to weld or even swing a hammer?  I think you 
have to have a solid base in theory to be able to continue learning and 
stay innovative, but a high practical competence to be of any use.  I 
just think that you should get the practical experience from your 
summer job rather than a classroom.  If the classrooms teach only "how 
to work" and not "how things work", then you graduate in "ignorant 
consumer" mode and are powerless to innovate.  However, like the 
architect and engineer of the Hyatt Regency walkway, we have to 
understand that it just isn't feasible to run a 60' rod through three 
flying walkways without some inefficient gymnastics of temporary 
support.

Back to being topical:  what better way to gain practical software 
development experience than studying, using, and contributing to open 
source code?

There's not a huge gap between theory and practice in software anyway, 
so of course writing code that doesn't compile won't solve any 
problems.  But, repeatedly tapping F9 in visual studio without any way 
to really see what's happening just encourages the "I dunno, magic?" 
view of technology.  Maybe learning the GNU toolchain and running Linux 
is more about practice than theory though?  I'm not saying there are 
easy answers, it just seems like a CS department built entirely on 
Windows is like housing the architecture department in a strip mall.  
Except, to make that analogy work, we have to put blinders on everyone 
in the department to illustrate the black-box-as-counter-example thing.

And of course developers are both craftsmen and scientists, so a good CS 
department should probably be a "Towering Ivory Trench".

--Eric

software:  a hypothetical exercise which happens to compile.

</snip>

From guy1656 at opusnet.com  Sat Aug 19 11:21:15 2006
From: guy1656 at opusnet.com (GLL)
Date: Sat, 19 Aug 2006 11:21:15 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Windows conspiracy theory...
In-Reply-To: <200608181253135.SM06656@mx01-oc.opusnet.com>
References: <1155883987.44e563d3768fb@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
	<200608181253135.SM06656@mx01-oc.opusnet.com>
Message-ID: <200608191121.15825.guy1656@opusnet.com>

Re: CAD/CAM in Windows.

Some of the more mature, and full-functional class solid modelers, such as 
Pro/Engineer and Unigraphics NX, and possibly IDEAS as well, were originaly 
written to run on Unix big-iron. (I ran EDMICS in Unix on an SGI Spark 
Station in 1991.) NASTRAN is another fossil that's still used heavily in 
automotive design world for things like crash-simulation and crumple-zone 
designs.

When Windoze came on strong in the mid-90's. these companies had to re-write 
their core software to run in the MS world. Pro/E version 10 did this by a 
wierd kind of internal Unix emulator - in your C:/Program Files/proe 
directory were a bunch of subdirectories labled 'usr,' 'lib,' 'bin,' and so 
on...

COSMOS/M though, emerged out of the MS-DOS text-adventure command line epoch; 
I don't think it has any *nix roots.

- GLL

From ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com  Sun Aug 20 13:38:29 2006
From: ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com (Carlos Konstanski)
Date: Sun, 20 Aug 2006 14:38:29 -0600 (MDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Personalized Search Page Generator?
In-Reply-To: <20060820105754.GA1530@gate.kl-ic.com>
References: <20060820105754.GA1530@gate.kl-ic.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0608201359320.9459@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>

I'll be willing to bet that most search engines work like google, in
that they are stateless apps that use the querystring to convey all
needed information.  For example, here's a google URL to search for
"linux":

http://www.google.com/search?hl=en&lr=&q=linux&btnG=Search

It wouldn't be tough to use wget or some other HTTP client to send
pre-crafted search querystrings to a list of search engines, and then
parse the responses and spew the data into your own view.

You mention java.  In any OO language, I readily envision a class called
SearchResource (or something) that is a superclass or interface for a
host of individual classes named "GoogleResource", "YahooResource", etc.
Each one could implement a method called "public String buildSearchUrl(String
searchQuery)" (or something) that would contain the logic needed to
craft a URL for that particular search engine, given the search query
that would normally be typed into that search engine's textbox.  Adding
a new search engine to your app would be as simple as adding a new
SearchResource subclass.

Oracle Global Search is a product that gives the corporate world a
google-like interface to all their data, including websites, database
tables and views, and (since it is an Oracle product) Oracle
Collaboration Suite.  It is a typical Oracle product, bloated to the
extreme.  Someone needs to come along and demonstrate that this task is
doable with a minimum of tight, concise code.

There are several fun challenges here: the reverse engineering of the
URLs of the major search engines; the abstraction of their differences
into a generic framework; the parsing of their text responses,
extracting the raw data and discarding the rest; and the display of the
data (well, this part is not so interesting).

Carlos Konstanski

From daniel at digitree.org  Sun Aug 20 21:16:58 2006
From: daniel at digitree.org (Daniel Hedlund)
Date: Sun, 20 Aug 2006 21:16:58 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Setting the Refresh rate for FC5???
In-Reply-To: <000a01c6c4d0$ccfea540$6d01a8c0@RAMA>
References: <000a01c6c4d0$ccfea540$6d01a8c0@RAMA>
Message-ID: <44E933BA.4040502@digitree.org>

William A Morita wrote:
> I am using FC5 with a Dell E173FP flat screen.
> The monitor claims that 1280 X 1024 @60Hz is the "optimal" setting.
> The only refresh rate that is selectable is 75Hz.
> XP has no problem offering a 60Hz refresh.
> At 1280 X 1024 @75Hz resolution some of the smaller text flickers.
> I am unable to set the refresh rate to 60 Hz
> I have poked at xorg.conf.  
> There does not seem to be a setting in there for refresh rate.

I assume you've tried using the "Screen Resolution" program under 
Preferences in GNOME and found that it only displays 75Hz, correct?  I'm 
also assuming that you're using the standard [S]VGA connector as opposed 
to DVI.  Have you tried changing your monitor type to something else 
(Generic LCD Display/LCD Panel 1280x1024, or even one of the generic CRTs)?

Another solution that might be easier is in the "Monitor" section of 
your X11 configuration file, set the vertical refresh to only 60Mhz:

Section "Monitor"
     ...
     VertRefresh  60.0 - 60.0
     ...
EndSection

Depending on the type of video card you're using, it might also be 
corrected with proprietary NVidia or ATI video drivers.

Cheers,

Daniel Hedlund
daniel at digitree.org

Subject: [PLUG] Setting the Refresh rate for FC5???
In-Reply-To: <44E933BA.4040502@digitree.org>
References: <000a01c6c4d0$ccfea540$6d01a8c0@RAMA>
	<44E933BA.4040502@digitree.org>

On Sun, 2006-08-20 at 21:16 -0700, Daniel Hedlund wrote:
> William A Morita wrote:
> > I am using FC5 with a Dell E173FP flat screen.
> > The monitor claims that 1280 X 1024 @60Hz is the "optimal" setting.
> > The only refresh rate that is selectable is 75Hz.
> > XP has no problem offering a 60Hz refresh.
> > At 1280 X 1024 @75Hz resolution some of the smaller text flickers.
> > I am unable to set the refresh rate to 60 Hz
> > I have poked at xorg.conf.  
> > There does not seem to be a setting in there for refresh rate.
> 
> I assume you've tried using the "Screen Resolution" program under 
> Preferences in GNOME and found that it only displays 75Hz, correct?  I'm 
> also assuming that you're using the standard [S]VGA connector as opposed 
> to DVI.  Have you tried changing your monitor type to something else 
> (Generic LCD Display/LCD Panel 1280x1024, or even one of the generic CRTs)?
> 
> Another solution that might be easier is in the "Monitor" section of 
> your X11 configuration file, set the vertical refresh to only 60Mhz:
> 
> Section "Monitor"
>      ...
>      VertRefresh  60.0 - 60.0
>      ...
> EndSection
> 
FYI, here's the relevant stanza from my FC5 xorg.conf:

Section "Monitor"
 ### Comment all HorizSync and VertSync values to use DDC:
        Identifier   "Monitor0"
        VendorName   "Monitor Vendor"
        ModelName    "Samsung SyncMaster 955df"
        DisplaySize  360        270
 ### Comment all HorizSync and VertSync values to use DDC:
        HorizSync    30.0 - 85.0
        VertRefresh  50.0 - 160.0
        Option      "dpms"
EndSection

Good luck,
Mike

-----Original Message-----
From: Daniel Hedlund [mailto:daniel at digitree.org] 
Sent: Sunday, August 20, 2006 9:17 PM
To: wamorita at hevanet.com; General Linux/UNIX discussion and help; civil and
on-topic
Subject: Re: [PLUG] Setting the Refresh rate for FC5???

William A Morita wrote:
> I am using FC5 with a Dell E173FP flat screen.
> The monitor claims that 1280 X 1024 @60Hz is the "optimal" setting.
> The only refresh rate that is selectable is 75Hz.
> XP has no problem offering a 60Hz refresh.
> At 1280 X 1024 @75Hz resolution some of the smaller text flickers.
> I am unable to set the refresh rate to 60 Hz I have poked at 
> xorg.conf.
> There does not seem to be a setting in there for refresh rate.

I assume you've tried using the "Screen Resolution" program under
Preferences in GNOME and found that it only displays 75Hz, correct?  I'm
also assuming that you're using the standard [S]VGA connector as opposed to
DVI.  Have you tried changing your monitor type to something else (Generic
LCD Display/LCD Panel 1280x1024, or even one of the generic CRTs)?

Another solution that might be easier is in the "Monitor" section of your
X11 configuration file, set the vertical refresh to only 60Mhz:

Section "Monitor"
     ...
     VertRefresh  60.0 - 60.0
     ...
EndSection

Depending on the type of video card you're using, it might also be corrected
with proprietary NVidia or ATI video drivers.

Cheers,

Daniel Hedlund
daniel at digitree.org





From alan at clueserver.org  Mon Aug 21 10:40:02 2006
From: alan at clueserver.org (alan)
Date: Mon, 21 Aug 2006 10:40:02 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Interesting Greylisting issue
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0608211035120.9621@blackbox.fnordora.org>

I ran into this yesterday. In case someone runs into a similar problem.

After a power reset, my mail server rebooted.  (I was troubleshooting a 
problem at the breakers and got the wrong one due to bad labeling.)

After the reboot, mail did not work at all.

The problem was that the greylisting database got corrupted when the 
reboot occurred.  The only indication was a message in /var/log/maillog 
about a timeout on the socket used by the greylisting daemon.

I stopped the greylisting daemon, deleted all the files in the greylisting 
database directory and restarted postgrey and postfix and everything was 
happy.

--

from... the FIRE HOOOOOSE!"

How about $29.99 for 6 months Comcast cable Internet promotion plus
Vonage for $27/month after taxes?  $68 is arguably a bargain if the 
non promotion price of $54 something has to be paid for cable Internet
unless: long distance, speed, or a second phone is an issue.  Some 
folks say that Vonage isn't interesting to them because it will go up 
dramatically in price.  Note that Vonage is only sold month to month, 
so anyone can get out of it.  Also, a second line on Vonage for faxes
is cheap and yes faxing does work over VoIP in my experience.

Vonage and cable Internet isn't likely to save you money, but it will
save you a headache by freeing you from the confusing phone bills of
traditional phone companies.  Some phone companies, like CenturyTel,
hit you with so many strange deposits that cable plus Vonage is 
always cheaper.  Unless you have to have a static ip subnet,
I recommend cable plus Vonage as the primary choice.  Comcast Cable and 
the local phone company are parallel monopolies right now.  No matter 
which one you choose, there are definite pros and cons.  Even if you get
DSL, I recommend stripping the POTS line down as much as possible and 
adding Vonage.  Sadly, most EAS areas shove the extension down people's 
throats so that traditional phone service for less than $35/month is 
hard to find anywhere anymore in Oregon.  EAS should be available, but 
not required.  Unfortunately, the telecommunications lobby must be 
strong enough to enforce an, "everyone pays for it, or noone gets it," 
policy.  EAS wiped out a lot of long distance where featherbedding and
monopoly status are contributing in my opinion to what the phone companies
do these days with their pricing.  If the phone companies want to survive,
they need to replace the frame relay infrastructure with either an ip
infrastructure or something else that's cheaper and higher bandwidth.

The biggest problem with phone companies today is that they require
contracts, so they are a poor option for say college students
that don't want to stay at the same residence for a whole year.  Cell
phones are popular, but they are not a cheap route to go for broadband
service.  Any kind of wireless is harder to secure than the wired 
alternatives.

Michael C. Robinson

   What syntax error have I made in the include_path? I am not seeing it.

From bsr at spek.org  Tue Aug 22 08:48:12 2006
From: bsr at spek.org (Brent Rieck)
Date: Tue, 22 Aug 2006 08:48:12 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] PHP include_path Syntax Question
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0608220835540.29366@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0608220835540.29366@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <44EB273C.8000405@spek.org>

Rich Shepard wrote:
>   In /etc/apache/php.ini the include path contains
> /var/www/htdocs/obm/obminclude. But, when running the install script I see
> this warning message (part of a bunch of similar messages) that prevents 
> the
> script from succesfully completing:
> 
> PHP Warning:  main(): Failed opening 'obminclude/global.inc' for inclusion
> (include_path='.:/usr/lib/php/includes:/var/www/htdocs/obm/obminclude') in
> /var/www/htdocs/obm/php/admin_data/admin_data_index.php on line 25

It looks like the php is looking for 
/var/www/htdocs/obm/obminclude/obinclude/global.inc and not 
/var/www/htdocs/obm/obminclude/global.inc which is probably where the 
globals.inc file actually is.

If that is the case then you should either change the include path to 
"/var/www/htdocs/obm" or change the include to "global.inc"; probably 
changing your php.ini include path would be easier (remember to restart 
apache).

--Brent


From wcooley at nakedape.cc  Tue Aug 22 08:53:49 2006

> /var/www/htdocs/obm/obminclude. But, when running the install script I see

I'm assuming the full path to the file
is /var/www/htdocs/obm/obminclude/global.inc, right?  And you want to
include 'obminclude/global.inc', so you want to have
'/var/www/htdocs/obm' in the include path.

> How about $29.99 for 6 months Comcast cable Internet promotion plus
> Vonage for $27/month after taxes?  $68 is arguably a bargain if the
> non promotion price of $54 something has to be paid for cable Internet
> unless: long distance, speed, or a second phone is an issue.  Some
> folks say that Vonage isn't interesting to them because it will go up
> dramatically in price.  Note that Vonage is only sold month to month,
> so anyone can get out of it.  Also, a second line on Vonage for faxes
> is cheap and yes faxing does work over VoIP in my experience.
>
> Vonage and cable Internet isn't likely to save you money, but it will
> save you a headache by freeing you from the confusing phone bills of
> traditional phone companies.  Some phone companies, like CenturyTel,
> hit you with so many strange deposits that cable plus Vonage is
> always cheaper.  Unless you have to have a static ip subnet,
> I recommend cable plus Vonage as the primary choice.  Comcast Cable and
> the local phone company are parallel monopolies right now.  No matter
> which one you choose, there are definite pros and cons.  Even if you get
> DSL, I recommend stripping the POTS line down as much as possible and
> adding Vonage.  Sadly, most EAS areas shove the extension down people's
> throats so that traditional phone service for less than $35/month is
> hard to find anywhere anymore in Oregon.  EAS should be available, but
> not required.  Unfortunately, the telecommunications lobby must be
> strong enough to enforce an, "everyone pays for it, or noone gets it,"
> policy.  EAS wiped out a lot of long distance where featherbedding and
> monopoly status are contributing in my opinion to what the phone companies
> do these days with their pricing.  If the phone companies want to survive,
> they need to replace the frame relay infrastructure with either an ip
> infrastructure or something else that's cheaper and higher bandwidth.
>
> The biggest problem with phone companies today is that they require
> contracts, so they are a poor option for say college students
> that don't want to stay at the same residence for a whole year.  Cell
> phones are popular, but they are not a cheap route to go for broadband
> service.  Any kind of wireless is harder to secure than the wired
> alternatives.
>
> Michael C. Robinson

From ianburrell at gmail.com  Tue Aug 22 10:03:09 2006
From: ianburrell at gmail.com (Ian Burrell)
Date: Tue, 22 Aug 2006 10:03:09 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Docs not local...
In-Reply-To: <965f90c20608220939q1b45703ek66f66621414abf6f@mail.gmail.com>
References: <1156233134.44eab7ae916ff@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
	<965f90c20608220939q1b45703ek66f66621414abf6f@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <d91f09cd0608221003t3b827807kbb63438624a18273@mail.gmail.com>

On 8/22/06, Ali Corbin <ali.corbin at gmail.com> wrote:
>
> There's still a kernel source package, but it's now a
> kernel.*.src.rpm, where it used to be a kernel-source.*.rpm.  And yum
> won't get src rpms for you (grumble, grumble) so you have to drag it
> down from one of the mirrors yourself.
>

That was fixed recently.  yumdownloader in the yum-utils package
downloads packages.  With the --source option, it downloads src.rpm
packages.  FC5 added repositories for sources but they are disabled by
default.  The -e option was recently added to yumdownloader to enable
repositories on the command-line.

yumdownloader -e updates-source --source kernel

 - Ian

From larry.brigman at gmail.com  Tue Aug 22 12:34:07 2006
From: larry.brigman at gmail.com (Larry Brigman)
Date: Tue, 22 Aug 2006 12:34:07 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Docs not local...
In-Reply-To: <1156233134.44eab7ae916ff@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
References: <1156233134.44eab7ae916ff@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <ca5165530608221234n245cc271u1844b9c1742b9d49@mail.gmail.com>

On 8/22/06, plug_0 at robinson-west.com <plug_0 at robinson-west.com> wrote:
>...
>  I don't understand in Core 5 what the note
> that an initial ramdisk is required now means.

This has been the case if you have used the default install options since
FC1.  The kernel is built as modular as possible but the default install uses
LVM and must load these prior to mounting the Logical volume that contains
your root file system and swap partitions.

The initial ramdisk contain all the modules for mounting the Logical volumes
and a script to make all that happen.

The thing that got me was as I researching just this type of thing, I found out
that Fedora was using a different format for the initial ramdisk than what
everybody else had been using (and documented online).  Typically a ext2
FS gzipped.  Fedora a cpio gzipped.

If anyone is interested and does not have cable as an option

You can get a DSL loop throttled to 896K/1.5M + IP from
Easystreet for $43.50/month + taxes.

It is basically a phone line (called a loop in telco'eze) with
DSL (subscriber) but with out POTS (old school phone)

You can then subscribe to Vonage for $15 300mins/month
or $20/month unrestricted usage + free adapter to a POTS phone
for free.

So if dont have a cell phone, $65~70/month you get unlimited local
(such as my 3 miles = more $ with regular land line) and US long
distance and an IP connection.

Hmmm me thingk my POTS at home is going away, ....

Pete Lancahire wrote:
> Sorry just ment the use is someone reading email, web surfing, etc
> 
> \\> plug_0 at robinson-west.com wrote:
>>> How about $29.99 for 6 months Comcast cable Internet promotion plus
>>> Vonage for $27/month after taxes?
>> Pete said something about "web+web/email".  While I'm not sure exactly
>> what that means, if he intends on running a web or email server then he
>> better check up on Comcast's TOS.  Last I checked running a server of
>> any kind on their consumer service was not allowed.

I also think that this 'general rule' is way overrated and unenforcable. If 
they would disallow *all* incoming connections then you wouldn't be able to do 
much with your internet connection at all. Things like VoIP would fail to work, 
p2p traffic etc (even legal) stops functioning and technically those are 
'services' (think of your vonage box as a server accepting incoming VoIP phone 
calls).

The provider is covering his ass and wants you to prohibit making money off 
your service by running a pr0n site at home (or worse!). He does not want to 
prohibit you from running a webmail app for you and your wife, but he doesn't 
word it as such to deter 'fair use'. After all, all he wants is control over 
what you do with your connection ;) (world domination is next BTW).

There's no reason the ISP should go after you for hosting a ssh service on your 
box at home, or run a webmail app to check your personal mail. It's much the 
same as allowing remote help in windows (vnc for unix!)...

Cheers,

Auke

Quoting Bill Ensley <bill at bearprinting.com>:

> There is this new fangled thing called a "Printer"!!! I hear that it lets
> You make Permanent!! Copies of any document!  Think of the possibilities ???
> 
> -Bill Ensley
> Bear Printing 

Space, space, and space again.  Printed books are even worse.  One of the 
reasons people will use a computer is to save space.  A lot of web pages, 
especially those in wikis, don't print right.  The Fedora Core community 
has done a very poor job from what I can see of providing printer friendly
documentation web pages.  In Windows, one can print postscript output to 
a file and view the file later.  I don't know about Linux on this.

Printing also costs money, name one place that will let anyone print
whole books for free?  Being the interest of broke college students,
one would think that the documentation community for Fedora could be
more thoughtful with their wikis.  ASCII text or Tex version anyone?

What I want to see more of are standalone web pages that I can download
in a documentation pack and burn to a CD or DVD.  I should be able to
download the final Fedora Core 5 docs when Fedora Core 6 comes out...

Michael Robinson

> On 8/22/06, plug_0 at robinson-west.com <plug_0 at robinson-west.com> wrote:
> > Is anyone else concerned with the move to wikis and web based

> > especially for recent Linux distros such as Fedora Core 5, that local
> > documentation will be increasingly treated as a thing of the past?
> 
> I much preffer up-to-date, on-line, searchable docs that I can fix
> when they're wrong to whatever was thrown together "because we've got
> to ship something" when the packaged binary was created.
> 
> I *do* take advantage of the debian local docs, (in
> /usr/share/doc/<packagename>) but generally that's the last resort.
> (Well, before looking at the source.)

Fine Rogan, but at some point interest is lost in the current release
of Debian, Ubuntu, Fedora Core, etc.  It is likely to get harder
to find relevant docs online.  You can't always upgrade your Linux
distribution because support for older computers is quickly dropping.  
You have to have a 400 mhz Pentium for Fedora Core 5 and it is 
supposedly optimized for the P4.  I don't think it's unreasonable
to pursue a local copy of the last docs that are posted for 
todays most popular Linux distro that I am using.

Maybe what is needed is an rsyncable repository of high quality documentation
written with the future in mind.  Out of say 100 documentation files, only
a handful need to be replaced/updated if the writers are careful.  If there 
is a way to download the wikis for Fedora Core 3, I'd love to hear about it.  
Thing is, I think a long term documentation project dealing with all of the
Fedora Core releases makes more sense.  As far as answering ones questions 
by looking at the source Rogan, how can you manage to be quick enough with 
that method?  Source perusal takes a lot of time and requires a lot of skill. 
If Linux distributions are only practical for prorgammers, then a lot of
people who could be good administrators otherwise are just simply out of
luck I guess.  It's not just an understanding of Ansi C that's needed to
read Linux's source code, I'd say one needs to understand much more than
that.

See, I just don't believe that it's becoming that much more difficult
to get a current distro on old hardware (There is one exception:  If
your hardware doesn't support networking and has no input devices
newer than a 5.25" disk -- even then, you can still install almost any
version of slackware.).  The kernel has improved in performance and
hardware support constantly over the years, and the infinite
customizability means you can remove bloated packages in favor of more
streamlined applications and environments.

The defaults of the mainstream distros have changed, and now those
defaults (such as gnome / kde) take more resources as the defaults
used in older versions -- however that does not mean that the "lean"
apps are no longer available.  There is still a large segment of the
computing market that deals with new, but computationally weak
machines (compared to the current desktops).  Kiosks and embeded
devices come to mind.

Unless you start citing references, and demonstrate that you've made
an honest attempt at the things you say are impossible, I see no
reason to believe your ramblings.  Particularly since my experience is
contrary to your statements.

> written with the future in mind.

This is much what TLDP was -- I'm not sure of the state of it now,
however.  (Note that tldp wasn't that much different from a wiki that
was printable....)

> that method?  Source perusal takes a lot of time and requires a lot of skill.

satirical comment.

I *have* looked at the source to figure out what an app does, but
that's just because I'm weird.

From daniel at digitree.org  Wed Aug 23 21:12:12 2006
From: daniel at digitree.org (Daniel Hedlund)
Date: Wed, 23 Aug 2006 21:12:12 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] DHCP Question
In-Reply-To: <44ECBFBB.4070102@gisnet.com>
References: <44ECBFBB.4070102@gisnet.com>
Message-ID: <44ED271C.4010105@digitree.org>

Bill Thoen wrote:
> I just installed Fedora Core 5 as a workstation using pretty much the 
> default settings. This morning the machine was running Windows XP and 
> the DHCP connection to the internet was working fine (i.e. all hardware 
> and connections were okay.) But for some reason FC5 on this same machine 
> isn't geting a DHCP connection. It recognizes the eth0 card, but seems 
> to hang trying to get IP information and then fails to connect.

I would attempt the following things to see what the problem is:

1.  Restart the computer at least once with the network cable plugged in 
the entire time (I'm sure you've already done this).

2.  Try using a static IP address with the values DHCP gives you in 
Windows.  If you don't know how to change this manually, you can access 
the graphical network configuration program in the Fedora menu by going 
to: "System" -> "Administration" -> "Network".  When you've set up a 
static IP address, try pinging the router or another computer on the 
network (ping the gateway address for example:  "ping x.x.x.x").

If pinging works, then you can change the settings back to DHCP as your 
network card appears to have been properly detected under Linux.  If it 
doesn't work, you may have some bigger issues involving hardware 
compatibility (or firewall, see below):

3.  While the default firewall settings for FC5 have holes automatically 
built-in to handle DHCP, if you've made any modifications to the rules, 
it could be your firewall.  Try temporarily disabling your firewall by 
running the following command as root:  "service iptables stop".  This 
disables the firewall until you restart your computer or type "service 
iptables start".  Once the firewall is disabled, try running (also as 
root):  "service network restart", which will try to restart the network 
services and bring up DHCP.

Let the list know if any of this does/doesn't work.

Cheers,

Daniel Hedlund
daniel at digitree.org

From bthoen at gisnet.com  Thu Aug 24 07:37:38 2006
From: bthoen at gisnet.com (Bill Thoen)
Date: Thu, 24 Aug 2006 08:37:38 -0600
Subject: [PLUG] DHCP Question
In-Reply-To: <44ED271C.4010105@digitree.org>
References: <44ECBFBB.4070102@gisnet.com> <44ED271C.4010105@digitree.org>
Message-ID: <44EDB9B2.4040106@gisnet.com>

Curiouser and curiouser... It seems that during the night a miracle 
occured, because when I started up the machine this morning everything 
worked. The problem with the eth0 card not getting a DHCP connection 
seems to have fixed itself! Apparently, waiting 5 minutes yesterday 
before a cold boot wasn't long enough (which I tried twice), but waiting 
all night did the trick.

So I guess there's some truth to the rumour that if you have a machine 
with nVidia chips and something called "forcedeth" and you run Windows 
XP before you install Linux, that Windows leaves the ethernet card in 
such a state of shock and awe that it won't connect to DHCP under FC5 
(even though all evidence other than the obvious says it's working) 
until you shut the whole thing down for a while (longer than 5 minutes.)

Anyway, thanks everyone for your kind assistance and patience with my 
lack of knowledge.
- Bill Thoen

I used to dual boot laptops and desktops and generally didn't experience this 
behavior. Once in a while when I did have DHCP issues in Linux I would 
usually just restart the network service which caused the DHCP client to also 
be stopped and restarted.

If you're really curious you might run something like "ethereal" and watch the  
traffic on your network if you have anymore problems like this. You should be 
able to see the DHCP request go out from your client box and see if the DHCP 
server's responds or not.

Tony

From larry.brigman at gmail.com  Thu Aug 24 07:50:35 2006
From: larry.brigman at gmail.com (Larry Brigman)
Date: Thu, 24 Aug 2006 07:50:35 -0700

On 8/24/06, Bill Thoen <bthoen at gisnet.com> wrote:
> Curiouser and curiouser... It seems that during the night a miracle
> occured, because when I started up the machine this morning everything
> worked. The problem with the eth0 card not getting a DHCP connection
> seems to have fixed itself! Apparently, waiting 5 minutes yesterday
> before a cold boot wasn't long enough (which I tried twice), but waiting
> all night did the trick.
>
> So I guess there's some truth to the rumour that if you have a machine
> with nVidia chips and something called "forcedeth" and you run Windows
> XP before you install Linux, that Windows leaves the ethernet card in
> such a state of shock and awe that it won't connect to DHCP under FC5
> (even though all evidence other than the obvious says it's working)
> until you shut the whole thing down for a while (longer than 5 minutes.)
>

Modern machine don't power all the way off when you press the power
button.  One of the things that is left on is the ethernet chip as it has
an option to wake up the machine from what would otherwise be an
off condition.  This is a management option of most motherboards these
days.  That option should be able to be turned off in the bios setup.

> the --bind switch?
> 
> It appears I'm clueless on this but I have RFTM or at least the man page 
> for the mount command but still don't see a method.
> 
> All I'm seeing is the regular mounts; partitions, nfs, etc.

Mine shows 'bind' in the mount-option field of the 'mount' command:

$ mount|grep bind
/home on /net/rheingold/home type none (rw,bind)
/var/ftp on /net/rheingold/var/ftp type none (rw,bind)

(This is some cleverness on automount's part, since it's not generally
possible to mount an NFS export from the same machine that's exporting
it.  The bind mount puts it in the right place so the NFS server doesn't
have to be a special case.)

Wil
-- 
Wil Cooley <wcooley at nakedape.cc>
Naked Ape Consulting, Ltd

In-Reply-To: <6779.206.190.139.254.1156282760.squirrel@petelancashire.com>

Thanks for the easystreet tip Pete.  Unfortunately, your phone lines
apparently have to be owned by Qwest.  My folks are trapped with CenturyTel,
CenturyTel won't even release your current phone number for use with Vonage.  
Getting POTsless DSL in a number of areas, including Scappoose, probably 
involves having your own dry pair laid.  One might as well have fiber optic 
cable laid instead.  My girlfriend and I had reasonable luck using mgetty and 
kermit chipset modems to do dial-up.  Something like that, your down to the 
cost of the phonelines.  You don't want to do dial-up if it can be avoided.
I think the cheapest ISP is $10.95/month, peoplepc.  I doubt that the 
el-cheapo outfits are Linux friendly.

If the climate is dry, wireless should work really well.  If you can establish
a stable wireless connection to broadband somewhere else, go with VoIP and 
you're set.  Either that, or start a cooperative and run landlines over your
partners' private property to hook everyone together.  If the prices are steep
enough in the area, a cooperative could prove very cost effective.

Concerning comments that Comcast doesn't want anyone to make money with their
residential service, why would that be so?

Has any company come out with decent Linux friendly two way satellite service?


Michael Robinson

From wamorita at hevanet.com  Sun Aug 27 10:26:46 2006
From: wamorita at hevanet.com (William A Morita)
Date: Sun, 27 Aug 2006 10:26:46 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] RESOLVED -- Setting the Refresh rate for FC5???
Message-ID: <000901c6c9fd$f9922050$6d01a8c0@RAMA>

Daniel

I have finally resolved my refresh problem.
In addition to adding your suggested: VertRefresh  60.0 - 60.0
I needed to add to the monitor section: Option "DDC" "false"

With the addition of the option, the refresh rate is now 60

Thanks
- Bill Morita

wamorita at hevanet.com
  
> ;; This buffer is for notes you don't want to save, and for Lisp
evaluation.
> ;; If you want to create a file, visit that file with C-x C-f,
> ;; then enter the text in that file's own buffer.
> 
> William A Morita wrote:
> > I am using FC5 with a Dell E173FP flat screen.
> > The monitor claims that 1280 X 1024 @60Hz is the "optimal" setting.
> > The only refresh rate that is selectable is 75Hz.
> > XP has no problem offering a 60Hz refresh.
> > At 1280 X 1024 @75Hz resolution some of the smaller text flickers.
> > I am unable to set the refresh rate to 60 Hz
> > I have poked at xorg.conf.  
> > There does not seem to be a setting in there for refresh rate.

Daniel Hedlund Wrote:
> I assume you've tried using the "Screen Resolution" program under 
> Preferences in GNOME and found that it only displays 75Hz, correct?  I'm 
> also assuming that you're using the standard [S]VGA connector as opposed 
> to DVI.  Have you tried changing your monitor type to something else 
> (Generic LCD Display/LCD Panel 1280x1024, or even one of the generic
CRTs)?
> 
> Another solution that might be easier is in the "Monitor" section of 
> your X11 configuration file, set the vertical refresh to only 60Mhz:
> 
> Section "Monitor"
>      ...
>      VertRefresh  60.0 - 60.0
>      ...
> EndSection
> 
> Depending on the type of video card you're using, it might also be 
> corrected with proprietary NVidia or ATI video drivers.
> 
> Cheers,
> 
> Daniel Hedlund
> daniel at digitree.org










From russell at personaltelco.net  Sun Aug 27 11:12:07 2006
From: russell at personaltelco.net (Russell Senior)
Date: 27 Aug 2006 11:12:07 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Personal Telco Field Day 2006 report
Message-ID: <86odu6gia0.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>


The Personal Telco Project held a Field Day[0] yesterday in which it
practiced its ability to construct long-distance wifi links.  By
Slashdot standards these were pretty short ones, but for most of us
doing the practicing they were pretty long ones.

The Field Day has been an annual event of Seattle Wireless[1].  This
year we were invited to participate with a coordinated event here.  In
the end we discovered that Seattle Wireless had decided to put off
their event until October.

On Wednesday evening, a group of PTP activists hiked or biked to the
top of Mt Tabor to scout out sites.  I was surprised when, unaided by
special antennas, I was able to intermittently connect about 2 miles
to one of our nearby nodes, the Hawthorne Hostel [2], with my laptop.
With a viable link to the outside world, we considered other
destinations.  One of our group, Tamarack Birch Wheeles, works at
Portland State University and has access to the roof of the Ondine
building, which we could see clearly from our perch on Mt Tabor.
Another feature visible was Council Crest, which has good access by
vehicle, so we hopped into Tamarack's car and headed over to scout
there as well.

Council Crest has good views to the northeast, less good to the east,
but if you stood in just the right spot, in a gap between a couple of
trees, through binoculars you could make out the street light above
the spot where we'd been standing on Mt Tabor.

Over the next couple days, scurrying ensued, as we had not really been
preparing or planning very much since we first found out about this
event about 6 months ago.  I worked on getting some of our two-radio
outdoor enclosures[3] ready, assembling needed high-gain antennas and
other gear, considering power supplies, etc.  Then, at the last
minute, we discovered two things: a) that Seattle Wireless had
postponed their end, which took some of the pressure off; and b) that
due to family obligations, I (aggregator of most of the gear) wouldn't
be able to get started until late morning on the day of the event.  Oh
well.  We carried on as best we were able.

When I arrived on Saturday morning at about 11 am, Tom Higgins,
Tamarack and Don Park had set up near the reservoir on Mt Tabor and
were scouting out signals from a vehicle-mounted yagi.  This location
was further down the hill from our original site.  Tom could hear the
Hostel node, but not associate, which might have been an issue either
with the location, or perhaps the radio card or associated drivers.
Meanwhile, I set about configuring our Metrixes to make sure we had
them working correctly at close range before attempting the longer
shot to Council Crest.  Tom Higgins had brought a nice marine battery,
from which he operated an inverter which allowed me to keep my laptop
charged up, as well as supplied the Power-over-Ethernet injectors to
energize the Metrixes.

After about 90 minutes of fiddling, during which Sam Churchill
arrived, we had the Metrixes ready to go and configured for the most
interesting Mt Tabor-Council Crest link.  Tom and Don took their
Metrix, Sam's tripod-mounted 24 dBi dish antenna, and an omni with
them to Council Crest, while Tamarack departed for the Ondine and Sam
and I headed for the top of Mt Tabor.

The upper loop at Mt Tabor is beyond a locked gate, requiring us to
cart our gear up on foot.  Having anticipated this, I had brought
along a jogging stroller.  Sam and I parked at the gate, loaded up the
stroller with about 50-75 lbs of gear secured with gaffers tape, and
pushed it through the gate and up the paved road about a 5-10 minute
walk to the top.  Arriving on site, we set about assembling a
non-penetrating "tripod"[4] we had borrowed from the Mississippi
Grant Project stores.

Don had brought along FRS radios for the event, and we were able to
maintain voice contact between Mt Tabor and Council Crest while we
were getting set up.

At the Mt Tabor end we attached two panel antennas to the mast.  One
was a 19 dBi loaned by Sam Churchill, pointing at Council Crest.  The
other was a 13 dBi, pointing at the Hostel.  We discovered a slight
problem with the mast mounting hardware, which consists of machined
aluminum brackets, on one of the antennas.  The bolts that hold the
opposing brackets together around the mast were inexplicably not long
enough to accommodate the diameter of the mast.  We swapped bolts with
the other antenna so that the more critical 19 dBi antenna had the
better mounting and then jury-rigged the other less-critical antenna
with one bolt and some gaffers tape.

All this activity, with the odd, un-park-like gear, attracted a fair
number of questions from passersby.  One gentleman approached and
started to ask some questions, and it soon became apparent that he was
there "on purpose".  Jay was in town from his home in Seaside for
another event.  He'd heard about our Field Day from a friend of his
who lives in Arizona, who had suggested that our thing might be worth
checking out.  While Jay got filled in on some of the details, he pitched
in and helped as we assembled the final bits.

On Mt Tabor we didn't have a nice marine battery and inverter to use
with the PoE injector, so after mounting the Metrix to the mast, we
opened the enclosure and applied 12V to the internal power jack and
device sprung to life.

We used channel 1 on the longer link, since the Hostel's node operated
on channel 11.  I had configured the Mt Tabor node radio to AP mode
with an SSID of: www.personaltelco.net/tabor.  I logged into the local
Metrix from my laptop via ethernet.

Right away, we had a solid signal to the Hostel, and I was seeing what
our driver (madwifi-ng) reported as "associations" from the Council
Crest Metrix and one other, which turned out to be Tamarack's Apple
laptop from the Ondine.  However at first, neither of them were
_actually_ associated.  As we started fiddling with the gear to get
the Council Crest link working, Tamarack got his Cisco 1242ag device
out.  We fiddled a little with our antenna aim, which didn't help
much.  Then, ultimately, the Council Crest crew decided to try
flipping polarity of the antenna.  We'd set up vertical polarity at
Mt Tabor, and Council Crest initially thought they had as well (I
think they were just confused).  They flipped the tripod over 90
degrees, and BAM, association!  

We spent another 15 minutes or so getting some minor NAT issues and a
little local DNS problem worked out, until I was able to authenticate
with the NoCatAuth captive portal software at the Hostel node, and
then Council Crest was online!  Tom got on the IRC channel, made a
Skype call over the link, and was generally just pleased.

Afterwards, we repointed the Mt Tabor antenna about 5 degrees to the
right to hit Ondine, but despite polarity flipping, could not
establish an association there.  We decided, as the Ondine roof was no
doubt quite warm by this time, it was not worth trying to debug on the
spot.  We called it quits and began tearing down the gear.

The link to Council Crest was about 5.6 miles (9 km), according to our
GPS coordinates and an online calculator.  The radios at either end
were ~19 dBm, with 24 dBi and 19 dBi antennas at the end points.  We
had some modest Fresnel zone interference at the Council Crest end.
The link to the Hostel was about 2.0 miles (3.2 km), which we
connected to with a 19 dBm radio and 13 dBi panel.  The Hostel's gear
consists of an unspecified Cisco outdoor AP and antenna originally
donated by Intel and installed about 4 years ago.

We were pleased with our success, modest though it was, and resolved
to try something slightly more ambitious next time: perhaps an
additional link to Rocky Butte, and/or (if we can do it before winter
weather sets in) a longer link to the top of Larch Mountain, about 25
miles to the east.

In-Reply-To: <1153533983.2845.34.camel@atlas.cadtocam.com>

Quoting "Kenneth G. Stephens" <kens at cad2cam.com>:

> On Fri, 2006-07-21 at 11:08 -0700, Jason Van Cleve wrote:
> > > -----Original Message-----
> > > From: plug-bounces at lists.pdxlinux.org 
> > > [mailto:plug-bounces at lists.pdxlinux.org] On Behalf Of Kenneth 
> > > G. Stephens
> > > Sent: Friday, July 21, 2006 6:58 AM
> > > To: josh at emediatedesigns.com; General Linux/UNIX discussion 
> > > and help;civil and on-topic
> > > Subject: Re: [PLUG] setting up SSH to chrrot to user homeDir
> > > 
> > > Think about it.  ~/.login
> > > /usr/sbin/chroot $HOME
> > 
> > Thinking about it, I guess you'd need some way to keep the user from
> > editing, moving or deleting that file, else they'd be able to disable it
> and
> > then log in normally.
> > 
> > --Jason
> Sort of reminds me of working for a government contractor at a
> governnment facility.  The removed the rm function so we could not
> delete files.  We figured out we could delete files anyway with the move
> command.  cp /dev/zero to ~/zero.  cp ~/zero file-to-delete.
> 
> Hehe
> 
> Ken of CAD 2 CAM.

How much damage can you do with a normal account anyways?  If all software is
safe and can't be operated in a malicious manner against the system, or in such
a way that a normal user becomes root, how much risk is there?  One of the
beauties of Linux is that even baseline security allows for the seperation of
users.  How much damage can an ordinary user do with rm -rf * from /?  Is
chrooting users to their home directories an attempt to solve a problem that
doesn't exist?  Maybe a better solution is per user permissions on programs
and not allowing any user to install or run a program in his/her home directory
without oversight.  With chroot, you are talking massive overhead and
duplication of software.  I believe SELinux is an attempt to implement finer
grained security that goes beyond user, group, and world permissions on files.

Maybe the overriding concern is that you want users to write to one particular
place on your filesystems and nowhere else.  On an ordinary system, they can't
write to a directory they don't own unless they get permission to somehow. 
There may be ordinary users that are a problem, they set their permissions
wrong.  How about wrapping ftp with ssh and going back to a true ftp session?

Michael C. Robinson

Subject: [PLUG] Recommended Reading for Home Networking Cable Install?
In-Reply-To: <c834ec80608161322m286c7e44w6a69e278d17d344c@mail.gmail.com>
References: <20060810204307.GA26505@nellump.ath.cx>
	<44DCC628.7000601@telestream.com>

	<20060811194341.GA998@nellump.ath.cx>
	<1155665699.44e20f237896d@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
	<c834ec80608161322m286c7e44w6a69e278d17d344c@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <1156538877.44ef61fd84302@xerxes.robinson-west.com>

Quoting Rogan Creswick <creswick at gmail.com>:

> On 8/15/06, plug_0 at robinson-west.com <plug_0 at robinson-west.com> wrote:
> 
> > While wired networks are supposedly more secure than
> > wireless ones, it's important to note that wire tapping can
> > become a big time issue with copper cables.  You csn't wire tap
> > a radio signal.
> 
> Correct me if I'm wrong, but isn't a large part (if not virtually all)
> of the concern about wireless networking preciesly because it's much
> easier to intercept signals? Even if it does take some non-standard
> equipment, you don't need physical access to anything (aside from
> being within range of the signal, which I assume is largely a function
> of how sensitive your listening gear is.)

Given infinite time any code can be cracked.  This isn't a problem if the
information being protected becomes irrelevant.  The ether in ethernet is 
a radio term, there actually is a radio field out to a certain distance 
around an ethernet cable.  Thing is, tapping a copper cable is a cheaper 
way to spy on wired lan than purchasing the expensive radio equipment.

If you have encryption disabled, common considering how much of a pain 
wireless ethernet encryption schemes are to work with especially in 
Linux, then wireless is significantly less secure than it's wired 
cousin.

About non standard radio equipment, if you have to be in the same block
to tap into an ethernet cable's RF field, that could look awfully 
suspicious.

By abandoning dhcp and firewalling wireless links tightly, you can 
discourage people from hopping on and getting a free ride.  If you
have a skilled radio technician that records your signal...  you
better be encrypted if anything important is going out.  The radio
interception problem exists for wired networks, but the proximity
that the spy has to be within is different.

How secure is wireless via laser light compared to wireless via an 
RF signal?  If laser light can be pinpointed and interception can 
be caught in real time, it is a potentially more secure then RF.

people say that slackware's rc scripts are easier to imderstand than Fedora's
init scripts.  

The way the pango etc. libraries are twisted into packages, I question if 
there is a Linux distribution today that isn't library and dependency hell.  
I think a library manager would be far more helpful than a package manager 
if indeed a library is broken.  I wonder if http://www.linuxprinting.org/
has a mailing list where this problem is mentioned?

oldest brother abandoned Debian.  He got overwhelmed by Debian wanting
to install kde on him.  Being a slackware fan before, he still had 
problems with dependencies in Debian.  I think he's back to Slackware,
though he could just be an OSX user.

problems more often with CentOs more than I have with Fedora.  Fedora does
seem to be getting fat though.  Fortunately, the focus now appears to be
trimming  the fat.  Is there a free up-to-date maximum rpm floating around?

Subject: [PLUG] Invisible Cursor
Message-ID: <20060829001237.GA12968@www.gisnet.com>

I've got a problem with the cursor in the graphic interface going
invisible. Here's the scenario: I boot up my Fedora Core 5 system in
runlevel 3 (text mode). Then, when I want to switch to the Gnome desktop, I
run startx. Everything's fine; the monitor is running at 1280 x 1024
resolutoin and the cursor is visible. Then later, I log out back to text
mode. The trouble happens when I run startx again. This time the cursor is
invisible. The cursor is still working, but I just can't see it. The only
way I've been able to fix this is to reboot the system, but this isn't an
ideal way to operate.

Has anyone got any ideas how I can fix this?

I don't know if this helps, but here's some bits from /etc/X11/xorg.conf:

Section "InputDevice"
        Identifier  "Mouse0"
        Driver      "mouse"
        Option      "Protocol" "IMPS/2"
        Option      "Device" "/dev/input/mice"
        Option      "ZAxisMapping" "4 5"
        Option      "Emulate3Buttons" "yes"
EndSection

Section "Device"
        Identifier  "Videocard0"
        Driver      "nv"
        VendorName  "Videocard vendor"
        BoardName   "nVidia Corporation C51 PCI Express Bridge"
EndSection


Thanks,
- Bill Thoen

> runlevel 3 (text mode). Then, when I want to switch to the Gnome desktop, I
> run startx. Everything's fine; the monitor is running at 1280 x 1024
> resolutoin and the cursor is visible. Then later, I log out back to text

This is appears to be a know bug and relates to NVidia cards.  Have a 
look at the following bug report:
https://bugzilla.redhat.com/bugzilla/show_bug.cgi?id=182517

Also have a look at the following forum thread:
http://www.linuxquestions.org/questions/showthread.php?t=447233

If these don't directly help, make sure that you've upgraded to the 
latest RPMs.  Also try changing "nv" to "vesa" and see if that solves 
the mouse issues when restarting X...if so then it's probably definitely 
with the "nv" driver.

Cheers,

Daniel Hedlund
daniel at digitree.org


From ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com  Mon Aug 28 20:45:44 2006
From: ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com (Carlos Konstanski)
Date: Mon, 28 Aug 2006 21:45:44 -0600 (MDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Invisible Cursor
In-Reply-To: <44F39070.7060905@digitree.org>
References: <20060829001237.GA12968@www.gisnet.com>
	<44F39070.7060905@digitree.org>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0608282141300.26445@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>

If you're using an nvidia card with the nv driver, why not take the next
step and use the nvidia driver from nvidia corp (rather than the nv
driver in the kernel)?  If the problem is a driver issue, then a
different, better (opengl) driver just may fix it, and you'll be able to
play tux racer too.

I'm a gentoo guy, therefore I'm accustomed to getting the driver using
portage.  Perhaps you can get the driver for fedora using the usual
package management system, whatever it may be?

When using the nvidia driver, you change "nv" to "nvidia" in xorg.conf.
Also, after every kernel rebuild you'll have to reinstall the driver
(quick operation, easy to do).

Carlos Konstanski

From ehem at m5p.com  Mon Aug 28 21:36:08 2006
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Mon, 28 Aug 2006 21:36:08 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] For The SysAdmin Gurus
In-Reply-To: <20060828190006.F0DC44604@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200608290436.k7T4a8N0002399@m5p.com>

>From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com
> On package management being so hyped by people, that's exactly why my
> oldest brother abandoned Debian.  He got overwhelmed by Debian wanting
> to install kde on him.  Being a slackware fan before, he still had 
> problems with dependencies in Debian.  I think he's back to Slackware,
> though he could just be an OSX user.

It was going to install KDE because he wanted a package that requires the
KDE libraries to install. Debian's front ends make it pretty easy to
determine what package(s) depend on those libraries if you want to remove
the libraries. Debian will even listen to you if you get insistant and
tell it to remove libraries and damn the consequences. I have noticed the
situation of all the booby-trap packages that suck in KDE/emacs without
it being obvious, it would be nice to have a
do-not-even-suggest-installing-this-package flag, similar to the "hold"
flag.

The decision is simple, do you go for package management systems which
guarentee (or should guarentee) that all packages will work, but will
suck in packages/libraries you don't want; or do you go for a system
where you manually select all packages to install and can prevent the
installation of undesirable ones, but at the cost of manual dependancies
and possibly broken packages due to forgetting a library? I don't think
it is worth my time handling dependancies fully manually.

> According to the posted interview, Fedora is not bleeding edge.  I've had
> problems more often with CentOs more than I have with Fedora.  Fedora does
> seem to be getting fat though.  Fortunately, the focus now appears to be
> trimming  the fat.  Is there a free up-to-date maximum rpm floating around?

Heh! Fedora *isn't* bleeding edge?! Then what is??? You suggesting
nothing other than Debian-unstable qualifies? (I'd place it beyond, but
oh well)

From daniel at digitree.org  Tue Aug 29 00:35:14 2006
From: daniel at digitree.org (Daniel Hedlund)
Date: Tue, 29 Aug 2006 00:35:14 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Invisible Cursor
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0608282141300.26445@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>
References: <20060829001237.GA12968@www.gisnet.com>	<44F39070.7060905@digitree.org>
	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0608282141300.26445@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>
Message-ID: <44F3EE32.8070405@digitree.org>

Carlos Konstanski wrote:
> If you're using an nvidia card with the nv driver, why not take the next
> step and use the nvidia driver from nvidia corp (rather than the nv
> driver in the kernel)?  If the problem is a driver issue, then a
> different, better (opengl) driver just may fix it, and you'll be able to
> play tux racer too.

Unfortunately, people who experience the problem with the "nv" driver 
also seem to experience the same problem with the [proprietary] "nvidia" 
driver.  It appears to be more related to using hardware acceleration to 
draw the mouse with certain video cards.  It could be corrected in the 
driver, but it's part of an initial flaw possibly in the chipset; I 
don't know enough about this to be sure, just read the bug reports at 
RedHat and upstream at freedesktop.org.  The problem goes away for most 
people by turning off the hardware accelerated mouse support in the 
X.org configuration file, which the bug reports explain how to do as a 
workaround.

Cheers,

Daniel Hedlund
daniel at digitree.org

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Tue Aug 29 07:31:30 2006
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Tue, 29 Aug 2006 07:31:30 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Invisible Cursor
In-Reply-To: <44F448B3.5080802@gisnet.com>
References: <20060829001237.GA12968@www.gisnet.com>
	<44F39070.7060905@digitree.org> <44F448B3.5080802@gisnet.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0608290725250.10421@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Tue, 29 Aug 2006, Bill Thoen wrote:

> I just like the black background and white text of the text inteface
> better than the white background and black font of the terminal window
> when I need to do any coding or editing, but it seems like it's a bit of a
> chore to get it on this machine and distro.

Bill,

   Nah, it isn't the machine or the distribution.

   What virtual terminals are you running? rxvt? eterm? aterm? xterm? All of
'em support the command line options of '-fg white -bg black'. You can
change the cursor color, put the scroll bar on the right instead of on the
left, and do all sorts of customization. Look at the man pages for the
details. My preference is for rxvt, but I use an xterm within pine, and both
a- and eterms support transparency if you like looking at the pattern of
your root window decoration behind your text. Cool effect, but I found it
too distracting for older eyes.

   From within X, whichever desktop/window manager you use to invoke
applications will let you specify the command line to invoke terminal
windows. Put the options there.

   Well, there goes the morning as you have fun trying different virtual
terminals and deciding just which options you want for them. :-)

Rich

From daniel at digitree.org  Tue Aug 29 08:37:35 2006

Bill Thoen wrote:
> Thanks, Daniel!   Putting :
> Option "HWCursor" "Off"
> in the Devices section of /etc/X11/xorg.conf did the trick!

> Now I have a new problem... Now if I run startx too many times, the 
> "power daemon" refuses to to reload. I'm getting tired of chasing these 
> little bugs just because I want to do things differently from the norm, 
> so maybe I'll just boot up in graphic mode and be done with the stupid 
> thing! I just like the black background and white text of the text 
> inteface better than the white background and black font of the terminal 
> window when I need to do any coding or editing, but it seems like it's a 
> bit of a chore to get it on this machine and distro.

I have a question for you.  Have you ever thought about running X just 
once and using <CTRL>+<ALT>+F1|F2|F3|F4|F5|F5 to get to the text 
console...and then <CTRL>+<ALT>+F7 to return to the GUI?  It would save 
you from having to continually start and stop X all the time.

One of the things I really really like to do with my terminals is to use 
gnome-terminal, set the background to black, foreground to white, remove 
all scrollbars and menubars.  I then set up a shortcut key inside the 
keyboard shortcuts preference to make <ALT>+<ENTER> enter fullscreen 
mode.  I then go <ALT>+<ENTER> and I'm presented with a fullscreen 
terminal window that is almost indistinguishable from the normal 
console...except that it's running at a higher resolution, has the fonts 
I choose to use and I can run X apps (ie. Firefox) without having to get 
sidetracked and start X.


> Warning to other clueless Linux newbies... Don't buy a machine with 
> nVidia chips in it and expect smooth sailing with FC5!!  But if you do, 
> then at least you can get great help on this forum!

Just to comment on NVidia cards and the invisible cursor problem.  The 
problem is in absolutely no way specific to Fedora Core 5.  The problem 
exists with just about all of the major Linux distributions as it is a 
problem with X.org itself and not any changes that the Fedora project 
has made.  Note that many NVidia cards also do not have this problem and 
it appears to be limited to certain chipsets (maybe firmware).  The 
problem also occurs in a graphical boot-up (runlevel 5) configuration, 
not just 'startx'.  The reason it can affect graphical boot-up modes is 
because many distros are using a graphical X session to display bootup 
statistics and then restart X to bring up the GDM/KDM (GNOME/KDE display 
manager).  It's this process of restarting X that appears to corrupt the 
hardware mouse cursor on some NVidia hardware.

On Mon, 2006-08-28 at 18:12 -0600, Bill Thoen wrote:

> 
> Has anyone got any ideas how I can fix this?
> 
> I don't know if this helps, but here's some bits from /etc/X11/xorg.conf:
> Section "Device"
>         Identifier  "Videocard0"
>         Driver      "nv"
>         VendorName  "Videocard vendor"
>         BoardName   "nVidia Corporation C51 PCI Express Bridge"
> EndSection

First, grab the binary nvidia drivers.  Aside from being good with the
3D stuff, they're supposedly faster (I can't say, I use ATI these
days).  

Then, add to the above section of your xorg.conf:

Option "SWcursor"
Option "NoRenderExtension"

At least, this is what worked when I had a similar problem on another
system a while back.

-- 
Dave Houston <dave.houston at gmail.com>

What mode are you in where ctrl-enter selects a column? I've wanted to
do that in the past, and not known an easy way (so I just make a
selection and use C-x r k to kill the rectangle and C-x r y to yank it
back into *scratch*).  Or better yet, what's the name of the function
bound to C-ret ?

Since I'm not sure how to make rectangular selections, I can't test
this, but 'narrow-to-region' *might* work, once you make the
selection.  It will restrict the buffer to the selected text, at which
point you can work as if the remainder of the document didn't exist.
Run 'widen' (C-x n w) to go back to normal.

On Tue, 29 Aug 2006, Carlos Konstanski wrote:

> I'm using gnu emacs 21.4, and I don't see "Rectangles" anywhere.  Is this
> an xemacs thing?

Carlos,

   No. If you place the cursor on the space where you want to start marking
then press C-@ (or C-space for most of us), then you move the cursor to the
end of the rectangle + 1 space (just as with regular block marking). You can
then press C-x-r <key> to kill, yank, clear, etc. the marked rectangle. The
addition of the 'r' tells emacs you're dealing with a rectangle and not a
block that extends to the end of each line.

Rich

> Slackware 10.1 is a lot easier to network boot than Fedora Core 1.  A lot

> people say that slackware's rc scripts are easier to imderstand than
> Fedora's
