Fedora Core 1 has a number of issues.  Fedora Core 5 is much more stable.

As for Slackware init scripts...  It depends on what you mean by readable.
Almost all Redhat init scripts look alike.  Once you understand how one
works, the rest are simple.  Slackware init scripts are from the bad old
days of Solaris style tangled init script.  If you are used to them, they
are not too bad, but they are still a mess and they are very hard for an
installer to work with without serious kludges.  (I had to build a Slackware
box reciently.  I was amazed how little they had learned from the
experiences of others since 1995.


> The way the pango etc. libraries are twisted into packages, I question if
> there is a Linux distribution today that isn't library and dependency
> hell.
> I think a library manager would be far more helpful than a package manager
> if indeed a library is broken.  I wonder if http://www.linuxprinting.org/
> has a mailing list where this problem is mentioned?


The problem of library interdependacies is distro independant.  Each handle
it differently.

The problem is library interface changes and security.

If you have a program that wants version 1.1.1 of library foo and another
that wants 1.2.0, what do you do?  Keep both? What if there is a security
related bug in version 1.1.1? Do you backport the patch to 1.1.1 from 1.2.0?
What if it is a rewrite?  Multiple libraries are a pain.  RPM handles it by
adding in compat-foo packages and then trying to backport packages.  I
assume Debian does something similar.  Slackware just lets you have
overlapping libraries.  Who knows if they get patched.



> On package management being so hyped by people, that's exactly why my
> oldest brother abandoned Debian.  He got overwhelmed by Debian wanting
> to install kde on him.  Being a slackware fan before, he still had
> problems with dependencies in Debian.  I think he's back to Slackware,
> though he could just be an OSX user.


The problem becomes knowing what to install and why.  Software in Linux is
becoming more and more interconnected.  As libraries use chunks from other
libraries, it generates this big web of dependancies.  Because the packager
is usually trying to address the largest audience as possible, they drag in
more and more packages.  The only way to get out of this is to break
packages the Slackwear way or use Gentoo and build just what you need.


According to the posted interview, Fedora is not bleeding edge.  I've had
> problems more often with CentOs more than I have with Fedora.  Fedora does
> seem to be getting fat though.  Fortunately, the focus now appears to be
> trimming  the fat.  Is there a free up-to-date maximum rpm floating
> around?



I have been looking at building an "core and extras" set of dvds for FC5.
Just to busy trying to get Compiz to work on my laptop.

For the most part, I have been very happy with Fedora.  Yes it is bleeding
edge, but that is because they are trying to do useful things.  If they
didn't, it would not be as useful.


From daniel at digitree.org  Wed Aug 30 11:30:33 2006
From: daniel at digitree.org (Daniel Hedlund)
Date: Wed, 30 Aug 2006 11:30:33 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] gpm Marks But Does Not Paste
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0608292041340.5568@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0608292041340.5568@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <44F5D949.5040308@digitree.org>

Rich Shepard wrote:
>   The gpm daemon is started with '/usr/sbin/gpm -m /dev/mouse -t ps2'.
> /dev/mouse is a soft link to /dev/psaux. Neither of these has been changed
> in years. I'd really like to fix the problem and your help will be
> appreciated.

There is a gpm option on my system called "-2" which forces emulation of 
a two button mouse.  From the gpm man page:
"Force  two  buttons.  This means that the middle button, if any, will 
be taken as it was the right one."

If that doesn't work, there is a program called "inputattach" that is 
used to change the type of mouse that gpm uses...I think.  It comes 
bundled with the gpm package on my system.  Have a look at the following 
command:
$> inputattach --help

That will list all the support mouse types.  The ones you'll probably be 
interested in are the "2-button Microsoft mice" (--microsoft) or 
"3-button mice in Microsoft mode" (--mshack).  The former one uses 
right-click for paste, the latter one uses right-click and middle-click 
for pasting.  There might be a better driver for your trackball but the 
MS ones seem to work for most people.

So you might try:
$> inputattach --microsoft /dev/mouse

I am not sure how well X plays with this or whether the inputattach 
settings are saved across gpm restarts.

Cheers,

Daniel Hedlund
daniel at digitree.org

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Thu Aug 31 06:55:43 2006
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Thu, 31 Aug 2006 06:55:43 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Rectangular Editing
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0608310650540.28921@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

   A couple of days ago I asked about editing a rectangular region using
emacs, and Russell pointed me to the docs on how to do this. While emacs
will let me block and move (or delete) a rectangular region within the text
file, it won't let me edit within it. That is, no restricted find or search
and replace.

   This morning (too late for this one data file I was modifying a couple of
days ago), I re-discovered that 'joe' can do this. Press ctrl-t x, and use
the usual block marking commands (ctrl-k b and ctrl-k k) to mark the
rectangular area. Within that one can find (using ctrl-k f) and search and
replace (ctrl-k r).

   Do not take this as a slur on emacs, but as a resource for when you, too,
need to change columns in a data file.

Rich

From wcooley at nakedape.cc  Thu Aug 31 10:38:29 2006
From: wcooley at nakedape.cc (Wil Cooley)
Date: Thu, 31 Aug 2006 10:38:29 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] fun with routing and wireless
In-Reply-To: <200608301639.08375.carla@bratgrrl.com>
References: <200608301639.08375.carla@bratgrrl.com>
Message-ID: <1157045909.6373.13.camel@willow.odshp.com>

On Wed, 2006-08-30 at 16:39 -0700, Carla Schroder wrote:

> Pyramid does not have bridge-utils, so as near as I can tell there is no way 
> to bridge the wireless NIC to the wired LAN NIC without rebuilding the whole 
> dern operaring system (insert much cussing.)  So what other options do I 
> have? I spose a couple iptables rules will do the job- what about routing? 

Are the internal wired, wireless and Internet all interfaces on the same
unit?  Are the internal wired and wireless separate IP networks?  If so,
assuming you're using NAT between the wired LAN and Internet, then it
should just be a matter of one iptables rule to do the outbound NAT.
Since the router interfaces are the default gateways for both the
wireless and wired LAN, the router should be able to forward packets
transparently between the two; you will, of course, want to lock down
what the wireless LAN can read on the wired or, depending the default
rulesets, need to open the rules a bit.  Should be pretty
straightforward.

Wil

Wil Cooley <wcooley at nakedape.cc>

From aaron at bavariati.org  Thu Aug 31 10:56:29 2006
From: aaron at bavariati.org (Aaron Burt)
Date: Thu, 31 Aug 2006 10:56:29 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] A qmail SMS Server
In-Reply-To: <ee892c770608301004y172bc118uc28001ab56136944@mail.gmail.com>
References: <ee892c770608301004y172bc118uc28001ab56136944@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <20060831175629.GT30417@syrinxpc.com>

On Wed, Aug 30, 2006 at 10:04:55AM -0700, Joe Langley wrote:
> Hello All,
> 
> I am interested in building an SMS server, and understand that qmail may
> have this capability. Does anybody have any suggestions as to where to
> start, or if qmail does have this capability?

First: Search Freshmeat.net for "SMS".  Manymanymany things there.

Second: What does "SMS server" mean?  What does it do?

Third: As far as I know, any ol' email server can send SMS three ways: 
(1) Using a modem and a cell company's TAP pager gateway.
(2) Using an email->SMS gateway provided by the cellphone company.
(3) Using commandline utilities such as Gnokii to send SMS via a
    cellphone or cell modem.

I've used (1) and (2) with Nagios at previous jobs.  Problem with (1) is
that TAP gateways are going away.  Problems with (2) are that you need a
working internet connection and you have to know the target's cellphone
provider.

I haven't tried (3) yet, but you need a cheap cellphone account for it.
That one might allow you to receive SMS as well.

Pyramid is based on Ubuntu Breezy.  You can get whatever package you
need for pyramid, unpack it on your Ubuntu live-cd box, copy the
necessary files to the pyramid image.  dpkg -L <foo> for the list of
files in the package.  I did this at least once (twice or thrice i
think) for PTP nodes.

I like pyramid.  No reason not to use it I can see.  It's biggest
drawback was that the image was hand-hacked to fit in 64meg, which
means it isn't easy to rebuild on a newer distribution.  It also lost
the dpkg/apt/etc in the process, so you can't install packages easily
on the fly, like you used to be able to with pebble.

Another quirk of pebble/pyramid is that in order to write to your file
system, you need to make the file system writable, e.g. using the
convenience script "remountrw".  When done, reverse to readonly with
"remountro".  Some files that need to be writable are symbolic linked
to a tmpfs filesystem.  Those files, such as hostname, resolv.conf,
etc are located in a /ro tree that gets copied to /rw on startup.  The
symbolic links point at the /rw tree.

If you've got any more pyramid questions, please let me know and i'll
try to help.

Russell Senior, Secretary

On 8/29/06, plug_0 at robinson-west.com <plug_0 at robinson-west.com> wrote:
> Will Vonage work over a private TCP/IP network that is Internet connected
> via a Linux gateway?  My friend's Linksys made Vonage router configures
> itself via dhcp designed for direct connection to the Internet.  Theoretically,
> it should be possible to give a Vonage router a private network router and have

It will work but could give you bad voice quality as the linksys has
built in QOS to give sip traffic priority.  You can do it on linux but you
will have to install class based queuing and give the ip traffic from
your linksys priority.


From larry.brigman at gmail.com  Thu Aug 31 12:39:39 2006
From: larry.brigman at gmail.com (Larry Brigman)
Date: Thu, 31 Aug 2006 12:39:39 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Voicetronix...
In-Reply-To: <1156963046.44f5dae6bf74d@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
References: <1156963046.44f5dae6bf74d@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <ca5165530608311239t61794a2qf14608a2d538c876@mail.gmail.com>

On 8/30/06, plug_0 at robinson-west.com <plug_0 at robinson-west.com> wrote:
> For the hardware on http://www.voicetronix.com.au/index.htm,
> does anyone have any experience with it?  I could use some help
> understanding the terminology.  What is SIP?  How about FXO and
> FXS?

You ask a lot of questions that could be answered by going to wikipedia.
Sip is the udp protocol for real-time voice traffic. It actually covers the
call setup,ring, and call teardown.
For fxo fxs look here.
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Foreign_exchange_office

>
> I've looked into the Skutch CK-4 and Teltone line simulators, they
> are exorbitently expensive and I'd have to replace some phone lines.
> Worse than that, I have more than 4 locations that should have their
> own phone number if possible in my network.

You will have to buy hardware but what you spend depends on
the level of quality and expectations.
http://www.digitnetworks.com/store/ is one of the places I would start
looking to buy hardware but not necessarily the only place.  For a home
system you should look into asterisk or sipX.
>
> In a few places I have wireless links, but no phone lines.  For cost
> and appearance reasons, I don't want to run physical lines everywhere.
>
> Do the voicetronix products only allow people to spread a POTS line(s)
> over an IP network?  I want to allow telephony across my ethernet
> lan, but not beyond it unless a code is entered.  I want to avoid
> expensive POTS simulators.

Doable but not without a local pbx system like asterisk or sipX

> PSTN phone lines are too expensive and there aren't enough of them coming
> in anyways for every single room in my network.
You will have to have a network drop for each phone or fxo gateway.
>
> Can the Linksys made Vonage routers be reprogrammed to work with open source
> software?  I wish Voicetronix would offer a nice solution similar to the
> Linksys router that Vonage uses.  There are plenty of people talking about
> VoIP as a paid service, but what about those of us who want to deploy it
> over our own private lan?

You can use the sipphone service.  I use it with a softphone and it works
quite well for out-going calls but incomming requires changes to the
router to allow sip traffic into the network.  My work is not ready to do
that yet without using a voip provider or local voip pbx.

On 8/31/06, Aaron Burt <aaron at bavariati.org> wrote:
>
> On Wed, Aug 30, 2006 at 10:04:55AM -0700, Joe Langley wrote:
> > Hello All,
> >
> > I am interested in building an SMS server, and understand that qmail may
> > have this capability. Does anybody have any suggestions as to where to
> > start, or if qmail does have this capability?
>
> First: Search Freshmeat.net for "SMS".  Manymanymany things there.
>
> Second: What does "SMS server" mean?  What does it do?
>
> Third: As far as I know, any ol' email server can send SMS three ways:
> (1) Using a modem and a cell company's TAP pager gateway.
> (2) Using an email->SMS gateway provided by the cellphone company.
> (3) Using commandline utilities such as Gnokii to send SMS via a
>    cellphone or cell modem.
>
> I've used (1) and (2) with Nagios at previous jobs.  Problem with (1) is
> that TAP gateways are going away.  Problems with (2) are that you need a
> working internet connection and you have to know the target's cellphone
> provider.
>
> I haven't tried (3) yet, but you need a cheap cellphone account for it.
> That one might allow you to receive SMS as well.
>
> _______________________________________________
> PLUG mailing list
> PLUG at lists.pdxlinux.org
> http://lists.pdxlinux.org/mailman/listinfo/plug
>

Ok, I take it back :)

Matt Westervelt at Metrix was very helpful and pointed me to their Subversion 
repo. http://pyramid.metrix.net/

The current build has bridge-utils and pcmcia support, though I still can't 
make it see any of my pcmcia 10/100 cards. I'll dig into this later, 
hopefully it's something easy like missing yenta_socket or something.

>
> I like pyramid. ?No reason not to use it I can see. ?It's biggest
> drawback was that the image was hand-hacked to fit in 64meg, which
> means it isn't easy to rebuild on a newer distribution. ?It also lost
> the dpkg/apt/etc in the process, so you can't install packages easily
> on the fly, like you used to be able to with pebble.

No package management, no user forums, and not much documentation are all 
little hurdles to deal with. In this era of 8-gig CF cards it might not be 
vitally important to produce the smallest possible distribution, but I like 
that devs are trying.

>
> Another quirk of pebble/pyramid is that in order to write to your file
> system, you need to make the file system writable, e.g. using the
> convenience script "remountrw". ?When done, reverse to readonly with
> "remountro". ?Some files that need to be writable are symbolic linked
> to a tmpfs filesystem. ?Those files, such as hostname, resolv.conf,
> etc are located in a /ro tree that gets copied to /rw on startup. ?The
> symbolic links point at the /rw tree.

It helpfully tells you the scriptnames when you SSH in:

/sbin/rw - remount the file system read-write
/sbin/ro - remount the file system read-only

> If you've got any more pyramid questions, please let me know and i'll
> try to help.

Thanks!

On Thursday 31 August 2006 10:38, Wil Cooley wrote:
> On Wed, 2006-08-30 at 16:39 -0700, Carla Schroder wrote:
> 
> > Pyramid does not have bridge-utils, so as near as I can tell there is no 
way 
> > to bridge the wireless NIC to the wired LAN NIC without rebuilding the 
whole 
> > dern operaring system (insert much cussing.)  So what other options do I 
> > have? I spose a couple iptables rules will do the job- what about routing? 
> 
> Are the internal wired, wireless and Internet all interfaces on the same
> unit?  Are the internal wired and wireless separate IP networks?  If so,
> assuming you're using NAT between the wired LAN and Internet, then it
> should just be a matter of one iptables rule to do the outbound NAT.
> Since the router interfaces are the default gateways for both the
> wireless and wired LAN, the router should be able to forward packets
> transparently between the two; you will, of course, want to lock down
> what the wireless LAN can read on the wired or, depending the default
> rulesets, need to open the rules a bit.  Should be pretty
> straightforward.
> 

Thanks, it is easy with iptables, I was just not wanting to. The latest build 
put bridge-utils back in, so I'm going to build me a bridge.

From carla at bratgrrl.com  Thu Aug 31 22:31:10 2006
From: carla at bratgrrl.com (Carla Schroder)
Date: Thu, 31 Aug 2006 22:31:10 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] more fun with Pyramid Linux
Message-ID: <200608312231.11091.carla@bratgrrl.com>

For anyone who's interested in this small saga- I had originally installed the 
pyramid-1.0b1.img.gz using good ole dd from a PC with a CF reader/writer. 
Then pop the CF card into its slot on a nice ebay Soekris 4521, and voila! 
All done. (you may follow my adventures in detail here,
http://www.enterprisenetworkingplanet.com/nethub/article.php/3627861
http://www.enterprisenetworkingplanet.com/nethub/article.php/3627861
http://www.enterprisenetworkingplanet.com/nethub/article.php/3629216)

Unfortunately that particular release has a number of problems, including no 
ethernet bridging or pcmcia support. So the nice folks at Metris pointed me 
to the Subversion repo for the current build. Which is all nice, but it's 
just the filesystem with no boot sector. I don't know how to build a bootable 
image, but of course in Linuxland there are many paths, grasshoppers. I 
popped the CF card back into the reader/writer, deleted all the non-volatile 
directories, and copied over the new ones. Stuffed it back into the Soekris 
board, booted up, and voila! It boots, it runs, and it detected both of my PC 
cards. Haven't tried bridging yet, but it looks like all the right files are 
there.

Just try to do that with certain redmondware, or applegoo....

--

?and sysadmin's guide! http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/linuxckbk/

From russell at personaltelco.net  Thu Aug 31 22:56:56 2006
From: russell at personaltelco.net (Russell Senior)
Date: 31 Aug 2006 22:56:56 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] more fun with Pyramid Linux
In-Reply-To: <200608312231.11091.carla@bratgrrl.com>
References: <200608312231.11091.carla@bratgrrl.com>
Message-ID: <86k64oruxj.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>

>>>>> "Carla" == Carla Schroder <carla at bratgrrl.com> writes:

Carla> For anyone who's interested in this small saga- I had
Carla> originally installed the pyramid-1.0b1.img.gz using good ole dd
Carla> from a PC with a CF reader/writer.  Then pop the CF card into
Carla> its slot on a nice ebay Soekris 4521, and voila!  All
Carla> done. (you may follow my adventures in detail here,
Carla> http://www.enterprisenetworkingplanet.com/nethub/article.php/3627861
Carla> http://www.enterprisenetworkingplanet.com/nethub/article.php/3627861
Carla> http://www.enterprisenetworkingplanet.com/nethub/article.php/3629216)

Carla> Unfortunately that particular release has a number of problems,
Carla> including no ethernet bridging or pcmcia support. 

You don't have to rebuild anything, you just boot your Ubuntu Breezy
Live-CD on a box of your choice, figure out which package you want to
add to your pyramid image, run:

  dpkg -L <package-name>

and look at the file list.  E.g.:

  $ dpkg -L bridge-utils
  /.
  /etc
  /etc/network
  /etc/network/if-pre-up.d
  /etc/network/if-pre-up.d/bridge
  /etc/network/if-post-down.d
  /etc/network/if-post-down.d/bridge
  /usr
  /usr/sbin
  /usr/sbin/brctl
  /usr/share
  /usr/share/doc
  /usr/share/doc/bridge-utils
  /usr/share/doc/bridge-utils/README
  /usr/share/doc/bridge-utils/THANKS
  /usr/share/doc/bridge-utils/TODO
  /usr/share/doc/bridge-utils/FAQ
  /usr/share/doc/bridge-utils/FIREWALL
  /usr/share/doc/bridge-utils/HOWTO
  /usr/share/doc/bridge-utils/PROJECTS
  /usr/share/doc/bridge-utils/SMPNOTES
  /usr/share/doc/bridge-utils/WISHLIST
  /usr/share/doc/bridge-utils/changelog.gz
  /usr/share/doc/bridge-utils/copyright
  /usr/share/doc/bridge-utils/README.Debian.gz
  /usr/share/doc/bridge-utils/changelog.Debian.gz
  /usr/share/man
  /usr/share/man/man8
  /usr/share/man/man8/brctl.8.gz

Figure out which ones are needed (basically /usr/sbin/brctl, and
/etc/network/if*/bridge), and copy them over to your pyramid image
(mount it somewhere and copy away, using utility of your choice).
Test and... Viola!  Package (or the interesting bits anyway)
installed!

You need to test because brctl might depend on something not already
present (I vaguely remember a library, ldd helps here).  Rinse and
repeat.  You might also need a kernel module, not sure.  A few
iterations and you are done.  Not.  That.  Hard.

Russell Senior, Secretary
russell at personaltelco.net
